LIFE’S STRUGGLE . . .
Becomes more and more intense as the Nineteenth Century advances.
Mentally and physically we must conserve our energies build up our
strength, and equip ourselves for the contest. We must have sleep, good
digestion, steady nerves, bone and muscle, clear minds.
These can be secured, maintained and
enhanced by the constant use of
CAMEO CIGARETTES.
THE RED PAGE.
George Black writes—
I have patiently awaited a reply from someone,
with more taste for the task than I, to the attacks
on Robert Burns which appeared on Red Page of
23rd ©ct. and 4th Dec. last. Loath to engage in
controversy with you, it cannot longer be evaded.
* * * •
Condensed, your arguments were these : (1)
That Burns, for his own uses, stole the ideas, and
even words, of preceding writers; (2) That his
countrymen are wholly unaware of this alleged
fact. Quotation will prove the verity of this
synopsis. You said : ‘Nearly all that is best in
the songs of Burns never belonged to Burns at all.
. . . Burns, the songster, was an inspired vam-
per, mender, tinker, and little more.’ The idea
presented in No. 2 permeates both articles, and is
most forcibly stated in the following sentence :
‘ The average Scotchman does not know how good
or how bad Burns’s (sic) verses are, and he does
not know how much Burns plagiarised.’ Now, if
you will grant me space to reply to your eight
columns of railing, I will undertake to disprove
categorically every statement worthy of reply
which you have made.
• • * •
For the charge that Burns' oounbrymen were
and are misled as to his sources of inspiration :
the Scots of his day could not be so misguided.
The songs which lie collected, and made printable
for Johnson’s ‘Musical Museum ’ (of which he
was practically the editor), and Thomson’s ‘Col-
lection of Original Scottish Airs,’ were in
common use at the time. Had he claimed
aught not rightfully his, it is one of the
curiosities of literature that he was not exposed
by his contemporaries. Had there been grounds
for charges, these would have then been made.
As half of Burns’ work was published posthum-
ously and oue-third of it without his commands,
it is not surprising that some verses, mostly
inferior, have been improperly issued behind his
name by catch-penny publishers of little-rfegarded,
and now obsolete, editions. This is true of almost
every voluminous bygoue poet. But the ancients
had no misconceptions regarding Burns. As for
the alleged ignorance of the moderns —I know
personally that the average Scotch man and lad,
of from 25 to 35 years ago, knew just exactly what
was Burns’ and what was not. The Currie, Cun-
ningham, Stewart, Aldine, and Nimrao editions,
with explanatory notes, and the letters to John-
son, Thomson and 50 others, were open to him.
(At least 150 editions of Burns have been pub-
lished in Great Britain, besides those of American
issue, and at least one-third contain copious notes.)
I refuse to believe that the Scot of to-day, with
his wider education, is more ignorant of his coun-
try’s literature than his father of 30 years back.
• * * *
As for the plagiarism : If “ the average Scotch-
man does not know how much Burns plagiarised,”
it is simpLy because Burns was not a plagiarist.
He took, the rude or impure songs of his day—the
namelessness of whose authors lie bewailed—and
the fragmentary snatches that floated from mouth
to mouth at fair and market, and made them a
part of literature, saved them from certain anni-
hilation. Many hundreds of songs, some by his
contemporaries, put into print by his endeavours,
owe little or nothing to his hand and have never
been published with his works. Let the fair-
minded read his correspondence and doubt, if
they can, that his desire was to enrich Scots
literature by saving many fine songs from
oblivion and by developing or polishing the many
floating fragments that lived chiefly in the
memory of a passing generation, rather than to
augment his reputation by association with these
musical collections. This is made more evident
if it be remembered that the poet indignantly re-
fused remuneration for h.is priceless work. He
also rescued from oertain destruction many of the
songs without words composed or-circulated by
the wandering minstrels of his time and immor-
talised them by wedding thereto appropriate
language. Of intent, he hid nought. He
acknowledged the sources of phrase, a line, a
title, a chorus, a stanza, a tune, on which he had
built up a complete structure of song. To me,
the finest trait in his - character is the unselfish
enthusiasm he gave to this work. His country-
men know what he did—without fee or reward, at
the cost of health and prosperity—for Scottish
song literature, and are grateful. In moments of
exhilaration, some may have styled hi in the
greatest poet that ever lived—l have heard similar
Australian estimates of Gordon—but that pro-
vides- no excuse for the persistence of your
attempts to depose him wholly from their hearts.
Burns is not to be so easily set aside : those who
best know him can most easily dismiss the
littleness which joys to think that Henley is the
promulgator of a damaging doctrine concerning
the dead poet.
• * * •
The narrowness of vision, which views Burns
through the spectacles of to-day rather than
through the media of his own time, is sur-
prising. IBs influence on succeeding literature
is forgotten ; the fact that millions have re-echoed
his sentiments is not recognised. He is ‘ very
commonplace ! ’ But Was he commonplace in his
day and generation? ‘His homely verse suits
homely minds.’ His subjects were certainly
homely : Domestic .Toys and Screws ; the Beau-
ties of Nature; the Wrongs of Mankind; Love
and Hate; Life and Death. What else, good
Monsieur Frangipani, is there to write about?
He is homely in the company of those who have
always had first place in the hearts of the people.
To them, it matters little how they may be re-
garded by oold-blooded cynics of the nil admirari
school ! Let these things be remembered :
Robert Burns, for love, put new words to
many blackguard Scotch, Highland, and
Irish songs, or scraps of songs, in order
to preserve what was loveable in them,
and made full acknowledgement of indebted-
ness, where it existed. William Shakespeare, for
personal profit, took the o’er-long rambling plays
of his day and pruned them into proportion, and
made no acknowledgment. Not one Englishman
in 10,000 is aware of it, but no one heaves bricks
at Shakespeare’s tomb and calls him ‘ filcher ’ in
order to disabuse the ‘ average ’ Englishman of
the idea that Shakespeare is the greatest poet
that ever breathed. Shakespeare kept his own
counsel as to his founts of inspiration—he is a god ;
Burns, whoowned up, and whose so-called plagiar-
ism is not a circumstance to that of the Bard of
Avon, is a comtnon literary thief !
Your reviewer’s knowledge of Burns appears to
be derived solely from Henley, but there is naught
in the Centenary Burns to warrant this direct
statement :—‘Burns’ friends and relations, with
intent to heighten his reputation, deliberately
destroyed many of the old ballads from which he
borrowed.’ This is not only a malignant slander,
it is also silly, as iB the further assertion : ‘ Some
of his material was also lost in the process of
years; some, probably, is still suppressed by the
holders, and some has remained unknown in
various collections.’ These evil suspicionings of a
jaundiced mind could be used to tarnish the fame
of any man. It is not for me to disprove them ;
the onus of proof rests with the slanderer. If
idly made, then we know how to estimate their
author. It is impossible to believe that Burns
was the sole repository of any old song ; the other
wild charges are equally incredible. The fine art
of the New Critic is also evidenced in attempts to
belittle the poet by much talk of the man’s
drunkenness—he lived in a drunken age—or of
his very occasional servility to patrons—in days
when the servility of authors was proverbial. This
is not criticism —it is mere scolding 1
It is too late in the day to attempt the destruc-
tion of Burns’ reputation. His strength, his
directness, his vividness, his easy certainty, his
passion, wit, pathos, and lightness of touch have
not only made him the greatest poet and satirist
of his country ; they have also raised him to a
place of high honor among the few great song-
writers of the world. Even Henley styles him :
‘ The most exquisite artist in folk-song the world
has ever seen.’ Of his laurels, the carping of an
obscure unknown cannot rob him—here or else-
where. ‘ He is secure,’ says Henley, ‘ even out-
side the Vernacular, of the fame of a unique
artist.’
* • • *
Henley is usually fair, or disposed to be fair.
‘ The Bulletin ’ only applauds him when he is
hypercritical or unjust. For instance, when he
accuses Burns of borrowing a measure from Fer-
gusson. This, the poet did when first launching
forth on callow pinions into the high atmospheres
of poesy. But to his prototype—cut off before
he fully flowered-the Ayrshire peasant is as the
sun to a farthing rushlight. Henley’s strongest
indictment is that Burns lacked, the power to
originate a new vein of thought, a new rhythm.
Has Tennyson done either ? Wordsworth ? Byron
—who borrowed his ottava rima? Milton ? Can
it be said that there is one poet of the Victorian
era, except, perhaps, Browning and Whitman,
against whom the same charge could not be as
forcibly laid ? Most measures, if not all, are in
oommon use; they are no man’s copyright. Is
there a poet, alive or dead, whose measures were
all original ! Burns borrowed his measures 1 So:
and who did the man that he borrowed from,
borrow from?
* • • •
I have been much awed by the keen moral sus-
ceptibility which causes you to shed inky tears
over the ethical bluntness that permits a Scotch
poet to use in combination any two words similarly
conjoined —before or since. Should this become
the vogue, the Scot who wishes to remark that
the sun rose will be compelled to find a new lan-
guage to do it in, or he will be nailed as a
plagiarist and damned in a chorus of inconse-
quential snarls from The Bulletin—while its
cover keeps on.
♦ * * »
Your reviewer says on Dec. 4 : —“ln the clean-
steal category are ‘ The Primrose’ (ravished from
Thomas Can-w —Burns himself calls it ‘an old
English song which I daresay is but little known ’
4 —herefore he ravished); ‘Bonnie Dundee’;
‘Whistle and I’ll Come to You. My Lad ’ ;
‘Landlady count the Lawin '; ‘Laddie, Lie Near
N'e’ ; ‘ We’re a’Noddin’’ ; and some two score
more. In tln-se cases, if Burns has not annexed
the whole of his originals, he lias annexed so much
of idea, phrase and rhythmus that the song would
be nothing without tlUm.”
* * * *
It is interesting to observe the lengths to which
unscrupulous prejudice will go. ‘ The Primrose ’
was collected for Thomson by Burns. He wrote
thus to him : “ For ‘Todlin’ Ham's,’ take the fol-
lowing old English song, which I daresay is but
little known. N.B. I have altered it a little.”
(He did not even Scotticise it.) I know no better
proof of the malignity of your bludgeoner. Re-
viewing Henley’s Look ; basing his attacks pre-
sumably on the evidence therein, he accuses the
poet of ‘ clean stealing ’ Can w’s poem. Let me
hoist him with a quotation from II nley 1 On page
104 of volume i v. this editor says : “ Burns did but
ply his trade with a view to filling a page in
Thomson’s book ; and as he claimed not the
maker’s honors but was content with the vamper s,
there is no more to be said about the matter, so
far as he is concerned. But one can’t help wish-
ing that he had lived to curb the enthusiasm of
certain of his commentators.” It is never safe to
review a book after merely smelling the paper-
knife ! I have read many editions of Burns and
have never yet met ‘ The Primrose ’ in any. The
commentator alluded to by Henley is Scott Douglas
—a very incompetent literary authority, who pub-
lished, £ am told, a very heterogeneous edition of
the poet: a menace, from the rubbish included,
to his reputation.
» „ • •
The so-called ‘clean-steal’ of ‘Whistle and 111
come to you, my lad,’ lies chiefly in an adaptation
of its chorus from that ol an old Scotch song.
Cunningham somewhere says—‘ Burns’ version
of this lyric is founded on an old fragment,
but he poured his own feeling and fancy so
happily through the whole, that not a single line
remains entire, nor can the new be pronounced
free of the language of the older minstrel.
Burns, then, acknowledged his debt,. and Cun-
ningham proclaimed it. Yet, Burns is styled a
thief, and the Scotch are accused of ignorantly
participating in the robbery, of reset after the
theft.
* • • •
Burns laid no claim to ‘ Bonnie Dundee.’ The
version now most popular—it begins ‘To the
Lords of Convention, ’twas Ciaverhouse spoke ’
is by Sir Walter Scott. In a letter to Cleghorn,
Burns says —‘ You will see by the above that
I have added a stanza to ‘ Bonnie . Dundee.’
This was the one beginning— ‘ My blessin’s on thy
sweet wee lippie.’ He used infinitely less of the
various editions in circulation than did Scott, who
has taken the old chorus —holus-bolus. But
Burns, who died before his sun had attained its
meridian, leaving his poetical affairs in confusion,
is styled a thief, though his borrowings were less
and his acknowledgments no less ample than were
those of the later poet, who goes scot-free of
abuse.
• ♦ * *
With the MS. of * Landlady, count the Lawin',’
Burns acknowledged his indebtedness to two old
songs. Let me quote for the purposes of compari-
son. -Sings Burns:—
Landlady, count the lawin’ Cog, an’ ye were ay fou,
The day is near the dawin ; Cog, an’ ye were aye fou,
Ye’re a blind drunk, boys, I wad sit and sing to you,
And I’m but jolly fou. If ye were ay fou.
Hey tutti, taiti,
How tutti, taiti— Weel may ye a’ be 1
Wha’s fu noo ? 11l may ye never see!
God bless the king, boys,
And the companie!
JACOBITE SONG. POLITICAL BALLAD.
Here’s to the King, When you hear the pipe
Ye ken wha I mean, soun’s
And to ilka honest boy Tuittie tattie to the drums,
That will do it again. Up your swords and down
your guns,
And at the louns again.
Even had there been, no acknowledgement of
the source of suggestion, would any honest man
call Burns’ verses a ‘ Clean Steal ’ ?
• « • •
It will surprise no one who has followed me
thus far to learn that Burns made no claim to
‘Laddie, lie Near Me.’ He was asked, quotes
Henley, to set words to the air of a song thus
named, and refused on the grounds that he did
not know the tune. Henley, however, is of opinion
that Burns amended and condensed the original
for Thomson. In his ‘ Remarks on Scottish
Song ’—appended to Cunningham’s edition of his
verses—Burns says : ‘This song is by Blacklock.’
Is this the conduct of a ‘ Clean-Stealer ’ ? It is a
safe thing to libel a man dead for over 100 years.
• * ♦ • ' '
I have wearied myself looking through editions
of Burns for ‘ We’re a’ Noddin.’ He supplied it
to Johnson’s ‘ Musical Museum ’ and the note
therein says ‘ corrected by Burns.’ Henley prints
one or two poems and songs in the ‘Centenary
Burns ’ which never before, so far as I know,
were attributed to the poet. He has no authority
for assuming that this is Burns’ song; your writer
has even less for stating that Burns stole it.
_____
The Local Dogmatist: This is only half George
Black’s apology for Burns (the other half will
appear presently), so reply is deferred. But some
minor points may be poohed while space permits.
. 1. G.B. begins by putting the case incorrectly.
Vide attributed statement ‘(2) That his country-
men are wholly' unaware of this alleged fact.’
Compare actual (quoted) statement that ‘The
average Scotchman . . . does not know how much
Burns plagiarised!’ Thus what I say of a parti-
cular class of Scotchmen G.B. applies to the whole
body : my qualified allegation of ignorance he
converts into a ehatge of complete ignorance. If
I also were of the race which bred Hume and
William Hamilton, I should be ashamed of such
a controversialist. Being a mere Australian, I say
Pooh !
2. G.B. adds a superior ‘ (sic) ’ to my ‘ Burns’s.’
Pity so many superior persons have such inferior
knowledge—of the inflection of nouns and other
matters. I do not vehemently object to * Burns’ ’
-Mor the usage is unsettled ; but ‘ Burns’s ’ is
(rightly) preferred by usage and by authority. If
G.B. will ask (as I asked) a bookseller for stan-
dards, he may be handed (as I was handed) ‘The
English Language,’ by J. M. D. Meiklejohn,
M.A., Professor of the Theory, History, and
Practice of Education in the University of St.
Andrews (has G.B. heard of the University of St.
Andrews?)- published by Holden, Lond , 1895
and he miy read (as I read) that ; We say and
ought to say, Jones's books, Wilkins s hat
James’s, Chambers’s Journal, etc. And G.-B.
enquires further, the bookseller may give him (as
he gave me) ‘A Higher English Grammar, by
Alex. Bain, L.L.D., Emeritus Prof. KHor of Logic
in the University of Aberdeen (mark! Aberdeen)
—published Longmans, 1891-and he may read
(as I read) at page 134, ‘ say “St James s
and St. Giles’s,” “ Moses s, f 8 s ’
“ Burns’s.” The general, rule is adhered_ to as
much as posse L. When the word consists of
more than two syllables, the “s” is dropt, as
“Euripides’ dramas.”’ I might add examples
from classical mil hors; but the Red Page does not
exist to teach rudimentary grammar to Scotchmen
uninfluenced by their own Universities of St.
Andrews and Aberdeen So against G.B. *
‘ LSurns’s (sie)’ I write Pooh ! (sic) and pass on.
3. If only ‘at least one-third’ of the, editions
of Burns ‘contain copious notes ’ pointing out the
alien origin of many of the songs attributed to
Burns, is not ‘ the average Scotchman's ’ ignor-
ance ‘ how much Burns plagiarised ’ amply ac*
cotinted for by the other two-thirds? (Here G.
B(, if he chooses, may say Pooh !)
4. G.B. says ‘Burns was not a plagiarist. He
took the rude or impure songs of his day. . .
Lower down—‘ He acknowledged the sources
. . . on which he had built up a complete struc-
ture of song.’ Lower down —he ‘put new words.
Lower down—he '’collected.’ Lower down
—his * adaptation .’ Losver down—his ‘borrow-
ings.’ So, according to G. 8., Burns took, built
up, put new words, collected, adapted, borrowed—
other fellows’ songs or fragments of songs. I said
Burns plagiarised. Why, apologist is almost in
agreement with assailant 1 Let us magnanimously
consent to pooh-pooh our difference ! There must
be some common ground of language upon which
we can stand and rub amicable noses. . . .
Aha ! I have it— 'convey, the wise it call! ’ Burns
did not plagiarise those disputed songs—he con-
veyed them —good ! Brother Tweedledum ! em-
brace your Tweedledee !
5. So far as their conveyings are concerned,
Burns, for the most part, gilded refined gold :
Shakespeare made gold out of lead.
6. G.B. incorrectly speaks of ‘ attempts to be-
little the poet by much talk of the man’s drunken-
ness.’ The reference tc Burns’s habits of drinking
and loving neither belittled nor implied belittle-
ment. I think no whit the less of Burns the poet
because he frequently got drunk and frequently
fathered illegitimate children. I think rather
more of Burns the man—and of other Scotchmen—
when he and they exhibit such virility. For I know
that these things flower from the root of the old sav-
age stamina which our city-spawning civilisation
tends to destroy ; I know that modern ‘ morality ’
implies not so much spiritual resistance to temp-
tation as physical incapacity to be tempted ; and
in the interests of myself, my kin, my country,
my humanity, I ery with Whitman, ‘Muscle and
Pluck for ever ! ’—and all their implications for
ever ! I believe that illegitimacy is preferable to
sterility ; that last-century’s three-bottle men
were better race-types than this century’s three-
teacup men ; and, however a comparison of indi-
viduals may issue, I hold that the race must argue
to its own standard, and let the individual go
hang. Thus, from a racial point of view, Burns
was superior to Shakespeare, for he was a better
propagator of his species. And if Brother
Tweedledum thinks drink should necessarily be
despised and illegitimacy should invariably be
denounced, all I can say is—Pooh !
7. 8; 9, 10, 11, 12, and the rest are reluctantly
held over for future investigation. In the mean-
time—Let us Pooh !

PRIVATE, NERVOUS & SKIN
DISEASES.

many years, obtained the highest position in his
profession. The various diseases embraced in his
speciality are those which have hitherto caused
more misery and suffering than any other kind of
trouble that affects the human frame.

Bards of the Backblocks.
Although respect for the feeling that prompts
in memoriam newspaper verse has always disin-
clined The Bulletin to scoff at its characteristic
grotesquerie, the appended effusion —from Dun-
munkle (Vic.) Standard of 13/8/’97—is printed
in full, as an example of honest, inarticulate,
human emotion which it would be pity to let pasa
into oblivion: —
IN MEMORIAM.
OUR DEAR MOTHER AND MY DEAR WIFE.
Dear Mother, thou hast left us here
In sorrow and in tears,
And some of us remember you
For more than twenty years.
We looked upon thy cold, cold form,
Upon thy icy face,
We squeezed thy hand and kissed thy brow
It was our last embrace.
Dear Mother, if thou have the power
To look down on us now,
To see us when around thy form
We kissed thy icy brow,
And as we looked upon thy face
And kissed thy icy brow—
Dear Mother, it is hard to think
We’ve got no mother now. £
Now let your father say a word—
Your Mother was his wife;
I had her long before to us
A child was brought to life.
No man on earth except myself .>.
Can write her happy life.
She was a Mother to you all
And was a loving Wife.
Oh, God, I pray that she may find
A resting place with Thee,
And all her children meet again,
Please do the same for me.
And let our children and our friends
I ask—I beg—I pray,
That all may meet each other when
We all are called away.
I remember that she would go
To the sick with fever;
She’d go away and stop a week—
Now she’s gone for ever.
When others did refuse to go
To nurse the sick for pay,
She’s gone to them and seen them through,
Take nothing, come away.
Sometimes in temper she would say
Some words she did not mean,
Then go away from me and cry
And let it not be seen.
She was a friend to everyone
That was in need of bread ;
Some swagmen will be sorry when
They hear that she is dead.
Sometimes they come to our back-door,
Beg something for to eat;
She’s filled their little bag full up
With onions, bread and meat.
Sometimes they come into the bar
And beg a glass of ale ;
“ You had too much last night,” she said,
“ I see you’re looking pale.”
“ I had too much last night,” he said,
“ I long for one to-day,
The place I spent my money at
Has turned me right away.”
“ But why do you come here to me,
You spent no money here,
And how can you expect for us
To give away our beer?”
And as she looked at that man’s face,
And saw his trembling form,
Says to herself, “a glass of beer
Will do that man no harm.”
She gave a glass of beer to him,
Tell him to go away ;
He said, “ I’ll come again sometime
If I should pass this way.”
Sometimes I said unto my wife,
“ You're doing wrong, I think,”
“ It was for God’s sake, Jack,” she said,
I gave that man a drink."
“ It was for God's sake,” she would say—
I won’t forget my wife—
“ That glass of beer,” she often said,
“ Might save that poor man’s life.”
She gave them food, she gave them clothes,
She gave them money too,
That they might travel far away
To get some work to do.
Now, perhaps we’ve said enough this time,
We’ll bring this to a close ;
That body will decay to dust
Just like a fading rose.
But one more word to Him above—
That we may meet again,
In joy, in happiness, and love,
To sing and pray—Amen.
“BETTER THAN FAME IS STILL THE
WISH FOR FAME.”
—Owen Meredith.
If, fighting through life, you’re a failure,
Don’t grieve overmuch !
If efforts to climb or to scale your
Ambitions and such
Are waited upon by ill-fortune,
Then while you the Fates still importune
To give you a chance for one more tune,
My lyre I will touch !
I’ll show you that though for rank, riches,
Wine, women, cigars,
The mouth of the failure still itches,
Though poverty jars;
Though the stamp of “ poor devil ” is written
Upon you; though hunger has bitten.
And Love from its throne has been smitten ;
Cease cursing your stars!
The way is all stretching before you,
Your fight’s to be fought;
You’ve sized up the blows that will floor you,
The tide’s to be caught.
To win where you’ve never been beaten
Is black bread to whitest of wheaten,
Is winning where leeks have been eaten,
Cause rations were short.
You’ve delved ’neath the crust, and discovered
Away down below,
The black soil where great hearts lie covered
With anguish and woe.
They’re fighting their battles, and straining
A hand to the weaker ones waning,
And fainting on tracks where the training
Has broken them so.
And if through the wild upward seething
You never come clear,
’Tis honest, the sweat and hard breathing,
And game may be near.
You try —and go down —well, your dying
Will send a soul silently flying
To where the dead failures are lying,
With never a fear.
N.S.W.
J. JINGLE.

THE PARSON OR THE TIGER.
“The Sunday Opening of Sydney Zoo. — lb was reported at the monthly meeting of the East Sydney Ministers’ Association, held yesterday morning in the
Bourke-street Congregational School-hall, that the action of the Government in subsidising the Zoological Gardens for the purpose of admitting the public free
on Sundays had already considerably affected the attendance of children at the Sunday schools in the district. And it was resolved unanimously that the free admis-
sion of children to the Zoo on Sundays, on the plea of educational advantages, is unnecessary, as all children of the public schools are admitted free on certain week-
days; and, further, that it is wrong for Government to vote money to secure for one section of the community privileges of which another section cannot
conscientiously avail itself.”— Daily Paper.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or ivash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 5, 1898.
The “Honest” Artisan and the Other
Fellow.
Henry Wrixon is a dull and “ right-think-
ing ” Victorian lawyer, who emerged from
comparative obscurity when he married an
immensely wealthy heiress, and became fora
while a second-rate politician. For a year or
two the community took Wrixon seriously
as a man of possibilities ; then it realised the
hopeless mediocrity of its long barrister, and
he is now only a H.C.M.G-. and old Money
Miller’s son-in-law. And Henry Wrixon,
with the horrible unfitness of people and
things, got up recently to deliver himself of
a plaint about the over-educated state of the
new Victorian generation, its loathing for
cheap and muddy “ graft,” and its desire to
wear a black coat and be “genteel.” This
is the same plaint which every Tory gets off
his chest at intervals. The new generation,
it is alleged, wants to live in a city and be a
clerk, instead of going on the land and
driving the honest bull-team of its fathers.
It objects to agriculture. It doesn’t want to
be a slavey and wash up the plates in
Wrixon’s kitchen. It yearns to wear a hard
hat, or a leghorn with a feather in it. It
wants to be a lady or else a gentleman, and
if it can’t be a gentleman it wants to be a
gent, and if it can’t manage to be a gent it
wants to be a city bounder. And over-edu-
cation, in the opinion of Wrixon and his
fellow-Tories, is the cause of this truly
lamentable state of things.
* ♦ ♦ •
If there is any lamentable state of things,
which isn’t at all certain, the Tory of
Wrixon’s stamp is certainly the last man
who should complain. The typical Wrixon
lives in cities ; he doesn’t go on the land, and
never did ; he follows no kind of horny graft,
and he produces nothing ; he doesn’t bring
up Ids son to he an or his
daughter to clean up dishes and struggle
with the family wash. He says that people
ought to do these things, yet he does none of
them. He says that they don’t do them
because they are over-educated, and have a
vulgar craving after “ gentility,” so presum-
ably he is an over-educated person with a
useless desire for tawdry fashion. If the
Victorian farmer and his sons, in the inter-
vals of stump-grubbing, delivered a severe
lecture to Wrixon and co. about the mean
ambitions of the kind of man who prefers to
be a fourth-rate lawyer, a tenth-rate poli-
tician, and a useless non-producer sweltering
beneath a tall hat in a city, rather than go
forth like a man and do some good to his
country by honest toil, the situation would
be intelligible. But when the useless and
non-productive swell city Tory reproaches
a community, four-fifths of which is toiling
hard for a living at anything which turns
up, for not tackling honest, hard work, the
situation is wildly ridiculous.
« * * »
In any case, the hackneyed tale about how
an over-educated generation has grown
ashamed of labor and won’t work as its
fathers did, rests on no visible basis. In
the first place, there is no evidence that it is
over-educated There is no standard of
education. At one time the individual who
could read and write became an archbishop
or a minister of state on account of his vast
erudition, and the average earl or baron
made his mark on important documents with
a straggling cross. As for a common, dread-
ful person like Wrixon, or Gillies, or
Harper, he was expected to know practi-
cally nothing. Now the middle-class man,
like Wrixon, is over-educated to an in-
credible degree according to the view of the
Tory of some centuries back, and he knows
a dozen times more than used to suffice for
an emperor. The ploughman of to-day isn’t
further ahead educationally of the plough-
man a few centuries back than the lawyer of
to-day is ahead of the lawyer of a few cen-
turies back, and the one is just as awful an
example of the evils of too much education
as the other. Nowadays the average plough-
man stands, educationally, where the average
emperor stood a very long time ago ; and the
emperor—if he is a decently-educated em-
peror—knows more than God was formerly
supposed to know. But the distance between
them remains the same, and one is just as
much sodden with superfluous culture as the
other. Five or ten or twenty centuries hence
it is quite possible that a man will need to
know about as much as Edison and Huxley
put together in order to get a job at fencing
or tank-sinking, and that the Edison and
Huxley of that period will know more than
the wildest dreamer has ever yet dreamt of.
But the relative distances between them will
probably be the same as they are now ; and
the man who knows least will still be doing
the tank-sinking job, or whatever the most
animal and least scientific job may be ; and
he won’t be over - educated even then.
There is absolutely no limit to the amount
that a tank-sinker ought to know. Ap-
parently there will always be some work
which requires the minimum of intelligence.
The man with the minimum of intelligence
will always get it, and there will always be
the man with the minimum of intelligence.
That is the whole story. Far back in history
the said man was a naked ruffian with matted
hair, and only half a remove from a beast.
Far ahead in history he may be sinking
tanks because he only knows four languages
and half-a-dozen sciences. But no one can say
that he is over-educated, unless that dogmatist
inows how much education there is and how
much of it anybody ought to have. And the
spectacle of any commonplace person with a
little money undertaking to define these
things is one for the gods to howl at in
Olympus.
* m * *
Moreover, though it is the custom of the
bell-toppered city man to lament the grow-
ing disinclination of other people to go on
the land, and their increasing yearn after
“genteel employment,” and the needless
education which gave rise to the evil—is
there any evil, after all ? Wrixon and his
black-coated kind, when they flutter about
their city haunts and contemplate the
struggle after clerkships and other billets
that have decent clothes attached to them,
conclude that there is quite an unreasonable
rush after jobs of this sort while hard,
honest “graft” out in the backblocks is
perishing for want of hands. But it isn’t.
If the narrow little city Tory went out and
spent 20 years in the bush he would probably
realise that the rush after tank-sinking and
mining and railway-construction jobs is quite
as furious as the rush after clerkships. Then,
being a narrow man who can only take in one
side of a question at a time, he would prob-
ably conclude that over-education was caus-
ing the new generation, which ought to be
doing honest toil in an office, to scramble
for ploughing and fencing jobs with a fury
that is simply unparalleled. The wail of
the Tory gent, sunning his shiny bell-topper
down Collins-street, about how a new and
awful generation won’t “ go on the land ” is
very pathetic, but when the Government
throws open for selection at a reasonable
price a piece of soil which isn’t .absolutely
desert, it is quite common for somewhere
between 10 and 380 applicants to apply for
it. The real facts of the case are that no
generation ever tried harder to get on the
land. If it hasn’t got there to any great
extent, there are plenty of reasons for it.
Vast areas are held idle, or held for sale at
hopelessly unreasonable prices, by the city
Tory who wants to know why the people
don’t go on the land, and who covers the
land with notices that the man who goes on
it will be prosecuted. And the man who
gets on the land generally succumbs to low
prices, drought, bush fires, caterpillars, high
interest, and the like, and his mortgagee,
the same city Tory, hustles him off again
with an energy which is quite surprising.
• * * *
The theory that a community with over-
much history and geography in its brain has
grown too dignified for labor is a convenient
way of explaining that it isn’t borrowing,
booming, land-monopoly and lory adminis-
tration which has brought Australia to its
present pass. But anybody who advertises
for a miner, carpenter, blacksmith, shearer,
rouseabout, or bullock-driver, or anything
else with hard work, long hours, and low
pay, and considers the rush of applicants,
knows perfectly well that the explanation is
a hollow lie. The only industry in which
labor is scarce is domestic service, and the
reasons for that are sufficiently apparent.
The household “ slavey ” has no fixed hours
—her hours are from the time she rises in
the morning till she goes to bed at night.
She has no privacy, no home of her own ;
she is always under the remorseless eye of
the “missus.” Also, though the class which
gives so much good advice to the poor is
always explaining that her position is quite
as honorable as any other, the members of
that class will marry almost any girl except
the honorable kitchen toiler. She is the one
female of the rising generation who is under-
stood, on account of her occupation, to have
practically abandoned all hope of rising in
the social scale —and so long as it is the
ambition of the average woman to have a
home and an affluent male of her own, the
domestic will be just as scarce as she can
make herself —unless public opinion alters.
* • * *
The class of which Henry Wrixon is a
sample asks with a sneer why the Australian
youth wants to go into an office and drudge
in a black coat at 255. a week, instead of
being an honest artisan—the honesty of the
artisan is always insisted upon—or bushman
at twice or three times the wage. The simple
reason w’hy he should do so is that the
artisan now is very fortunate if he averages
30s. a week all the year round, and the bush-
man is lucky if he makes 20s. And their
lives include longer hours, heavier toil,
rain, sunstroke, liability to accidents, little
amusement, few r books, rough food, uncom-
fortable lodging, and practically no hope for
the future. Whereas the youth in an office
has a multitude of things the other man
lacks, together with the chance of rising to
be a merchant, lawyer, or bank or insurance
manager at a big salary, or becoming a
politician and marrying a rich man’s
daughter, and dying in the odor of K.C.M.G.
If Wrixon had followed his own advice when
he was young he would probably be putting
up cookies’ fences at 20s. a week to this
very day.
The Federal River Problem.
The bitter struggle in the third Convention
over the Federal control of rivers is a rnelau-
choly evidence that the national spirit has made
very little progress up to date. The question
at issue is whether rivers which flow through
two or more states, and the waters of which
are consequently joint property, should be
placed under Federal control. The alternative
is that each state should have absolute control
of as much of the river as flows through its
territory, and that the one above .should, if it
is so inclined, have power to use all the water
for irrigation purposes, and leave a dry channel
for the one below. Also that either state
should have power, by letting its part of the
river channel silt up, by diverting the river, or
by neglecting to clear it of snags or sunken
vessels, or otherwise, to block the other’s com-
merce if it has any interest in so doing. The
Murray system is the only one of any import-
ance in Australia which flows through several
states, and it is around it that the controversy
rages. South Australia, which holds the lower
part of the stream and sees possibilities of its
water-supply being cut off by irrigation-works
and its commerce obstructed by neglect of
the upper channel, favors Federal control. Vic-
toria, which has hitherto borne almost the whole
expense of keeping the Upper Murray—a river
that is claimed as the sole property of N.S.W.
—open for navigation, also favors the Federal
view, as tending to lift a heavy charge off its
shoulders. N.S.W. has systematically followed
the policy of drawing trade by rail to Sydney,
and this policy would be advanced if the
Murray and lower Darling became hopelessly
unnavigable, consequently it is solid on the
principle that each province should do as it
darned well likes with its own fragment of
river.
• • » *
According to the bitter howl of Lyne and
Rkid and M‘MILX,AN, N.S. Wales, under the
Federal control idea, is asked to give up every-
thing and receive nothing. This is a hopelessly
one-sided statement of the case, and quite
worthy of those narrow provincialists. South
Australia proposes to hand over, as its share,
the whole lower Murray—some 500 miles of the
most navigable portion of the river—and the
command of its sea-exit. Victoria offers to
throw in its share of the upper Murray ;
that province has no legal share, certainly,
but it holds a half-share as a matter of
fact, and, seeing that it has done
almost all that has been done to make
the river useful and navigable, and that,
down to the Murrumbidgee junction in the
far west of N.S.W. it supplies the
bulk of the water, it has claims which it
probably wouldn’t surrender unless N.S.W.
could grab the disputed river by military force.
It nlso gives in the Loddon, (Joulburn, Carn-
paspe, Ovens, and a few other rivers. N.S.W.
is asked to throw in the Lachlan, Murrain-
bidgee, and their affluents, also its share of tha
Darling and its affluents, and its attitude is that
it will see the other provinces dead first. The
Darling is the principal subject of contention.
Every drop of water in the Darling, according
to Reid, Lyne and M'Millan, belongs to
N.S.W., which remark, seeing that half the
Darling water comes down from Queensland,
shows hideous ignorance of the whole business.
But while retaining the Darling, Lachlan,
Murrumbidgee, &c., Reid offers to federalise
the Murray, and as Victoria commands the
main water-supply of the Upper Murray and
S.A. owns the Lower Murray bodily, the offer
to go shares in these streams is a rather smart
proceeding.
* * * •
Whether the Murray system remains a
navigable group of rivers, or whether it is
ultimately so used up for irrigation purposes
that its navigability practically disappears, the
position of Lyne and Reid is equally illogical.
In the former case the object of Federal control
is to give power to the central authority to keep
these rivers open for navigation. So far S.A.
has done its share in order to bring commerce
into South Australia. Victoria has also done
its share, because the Murray and the South
Australian ports are the easiest outlet for
the north - western part of the province,
where railways are still non-existent, and
also because the river brings trade to its rail-
way at Swan Hill and Echuca. New
South Wales is building a railway running
parallel with the Murray in order to drag the
trade, at a tremendous waste of money and
mileage, to Sydney. Also, it has systematically
neglected the lower Darling in order to force
the Western trade on to the railway line at
Bourke or Cobar. If any Federal authority
took measures to make the lower Darling always
navigable, three results would ensue. Sydney
would lose some trade. South Australia would
gain some. And western New' South Wales
would gain enormously by having constant
access at small cost to a port comparatively
near at hand, instead of having to send its goods
over a long and enormously expensive railway
journey, and haul them up a mountain range
3000 feet high, and haul them down again,
and finally land them at a port 1000 miles
further from Europe than the river out-
let ! In this matter, western New- South
Wales would probably gain far more than
Sydney W’ould lose. But the fact that S.A.
would gain something at the same time is a
fatal objection in the eyes of Lyne, and Reid,
and M'Millan. M ‘Millan, by the way, being
a Sydney commercial man, is an interested
witness.
* * * *
As regards irrigation, the question is dif-
ferent. No one knows at present how far irri-
gation may yet go, but in all probability it will
one day be conducted on so vast a scale that, in
dry or even moderately dry years, practically all
the w'aters of the Murray system will be used
up, and, so far as navigation purposes are con-
cerned, that group of rivers will cease to exist.
S.A. lies on the lower reaches of the great
river which combines the Murray, Murrum-
bidgee, Lachlan, Darling, &c., and wmuld then lw
wholly at the mercy of the other provinces, which
could mop up the available waters and leave
their barren neighbour nothing but a streak oi
sand. Consequently S.A. asks that the Federal
authority shall have power to control the use of
the waters, and share them out among the
parties interested. Victoria is agreeable.
N.S. W.,by the voice of Lyne, Reid, and M‘Mie-
lan, again says that it will see its neigh
bour dead first. The Darling, allege Reid & Co.,
is exclusively “our” river; every drop of its
waters belongs to “ us,” and the proposal that
“ we ” should hand over “ our ” river is simply
outrageous. The fact that N.S.W. isn’t asked
to hand over its rivers to its outrageous neigh-
bour, but only to a partnership of which it will
itself be the largest and most influential
member, is a view of which Reid, Lyne, and
their fellow-provincialists say nothing.
• * * *
This provincial view, if Reid, Lyne, and
M‘Millan were only capable of understanding
the situation, may produce remarkable results.
Supposing the policy of Federal control is
made part of the constitution, Queensland,
when it comes in, as it certainly must some day,
will come in under that proviso and be governed
by it. As it is, it will probably come in under
a system which allows each province to do with
its own waters as it pleases. Ihen, if a vast
irrigation system is ultimately established in
all the three eastern provinces, the possible re-
sults are these : The sources of the Darling are
located well up towards Central Queensland.
Queensland controls a large part of the water
supply, and by using it for its own purposes in
dry years it can leave the Upper Darling,
just at the time when N.S. W. wants a plentiful
water-supply in the west, a mere series of mud-
holes. Victoria commands almost all the tri-
butaries which create the Murray until il
junctions with the Murrumbidgee out west, far
past Albury and Echuca and Swan Hill; and,
if Victoria ever starts irrigation-works on a
huge scale, it can wipe a big share of New South
Wales’ other big river practically off the map.
And the N.S. W. delegates, mainly for the sake
of preserving full control of their Darling river
in view of a possible great irrigation system
some time far ahead, are clamoring for a policy
which will enable Queensland to annex a great
part of it bodily. Also, N.S. W. claims to be sole
owner of the Murray, and yet its representa-
tives advocate a policy which gives Victoria
a very large control of the Murray waters.
Plainly, it doesn’t take much knowledge to
make a politician—even a leading politician in
Australia.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Lukewarm Holder.
The suggestion made by Treasurer Holder, of
S.A., that the less populous provinces should
wait to see the result of the Federal vote in
N.S.W. before taking a poll, so that, if the
national cause goes down, they can save the
expense of taking any poll at all, shows that
the lukewarm preacher - man from Adelaide
has got things wrong end up. If arrangement
can’t be made for taking the vote in all t he pm


vinces simultaneously on an early (lay, then the
next, best thing is for t lie small provinces to
hustle all they know. The cornstalk Premier
is the only one of the five who has deliberately
proposed postponing the Federal referendum
tor six months or so—apparently with a view
to letting things freeze. N.B.W. is the pro-
vince where there is the least chance of getting
an affirmative vote, because there the propor-
tion of voters required to carry Federation is
now tar larger than it is anywhere else. Also,
it is the only province which, by the terms of
the Enabling Act, must be included before
t here can be any Federation. And these are
just the reasons why it shouldn’t be waited
tor. If the other provinces go ahead with-
out delay, there will be all the less
excuse for the large, lukewarm laggard
to hang back; and, considering how eagerly
the Reid Government is looking for excuses to
hang back, that is so much the more cause why
it should get none. Further, if the other pro-
vinces carry Federation by big votes, there is
so much the more chance of the requisite vote
in N.S.W., for enthusiasm is contagious as well
as indifference and panic. Also, if the other
provinces fall into line, and Federation only
scores, say, 70,000 votes at the N.S. W. polls,
it will then be so obvious that the national
cause has been wrecked solely through the
treachery of the cornstalk Legislature in depart-
ing from the original agreement (under which
the required minimum vote was fixed at 50,000),
that it is just possible the Act may be amended
back again, if only for shame’s sake. And if
Federation does fall through, and Premier
Reid’s breakage of the original agreement is
the cause, the other Premiers, for the sake of
their own good name, need to paste it up in ten-
foot letters on the biggest dead wall available
that such was the cause, and that neither they
nor their provinces are responsible. Otherwise
it will only be remembered in after years that
N.S. W. took a vote on the subject without suc-
cess, and that sundry wretchedly-unpatrioticper-
sons named Kingston and Turner and Braddon
and Forrest were so indifferent to their mag-
nificent opportunity that they wouldn’t even
submit the matter to their respective states. If
all the other provinces let the matter drop, and
Tasmania alone carried the acceptance of the
Federal Constitution by a majority of one, and
that last lone voter was a disreputable charac-
ter, just out of gaol, it would at least be
remembered to Braddon’s credit that he was
the one boss politician who saw the matter
through, however uselessly, to the bitter end,
while louder men of greater political ton-
nage fell in the ditch. And that would be
something to be proud of, anyhow.
The Gathering 1 of the Clans.
Writes Thos. Ewing, M.L.A.:—
Dear Bulletin,—Many professedly fervent Federalists
in N.S.VV. attested their genuineness recently by passing
legislation which isrnored the time-honored principle that
the niajority is entitled to rule. They strengthened the
position of the anti-Federalist by stipulating that, al-
though a majority of one against Union is to be sufficient
to destroy it, a majority of 79,000 in its favor doesn’t
necessarily carry it. The niajority in favor of Union must
consist of 80,000 suffrages or the vote is to be regarded as
tantamount to a defeat. You have so fully exposed the
injustice of this procedure that it is not necessary to
make any further reference to the main question. The
anti-Federalist understands the difficulty likely to be ex-
perienced in persuading any people to surrender pro-
vincial rights even to participate in the greater privileges
of nationhood. When the union of England and Scotland
was accomplished, in the early part of the eighteenth
century, few Highlanders would have relinquished
the privilege of massacring the retainers of a rival
chief in an adjoining glen, on any terms what-
ever. The Highlander’s intense loyalty to his chief,
and .his obligations to his clan, were the only responsi-
bilities to which he was accustomed, and he could imagine
no other. Any change from the state of isolated rapine
in which he lived would, to him, have seemed unbear-
able. His ignorance and petty spirit was bred of loyalty
to a state of things not worth preserving. For the High-
lander we find an excuse, since clan cohesion was, for a
lengthy period, essential to his existence ; but it is diffi-
cult to explain why the question of union should be
viewed in the same light by communities which have not
been hammered into serious antagonism. Still the same
species of argument which obtruded itself at the Union
of England and Scotland found its counterpart in the
debates during the critical times when the American
colonies paused on the brink of union, and again at the
creation of the Dominion of Canada.
The fact that time has refuted these doleful apprehen-
sions does not affect the provincialist in Australia; he
grinds out the same old dirge, which has done dntv on
every previous similar occasion, as strenuously as if the
world had never before heard it. The essence of this wail
is the splendid quality of his own clan (or province), and
the degeneracy of those separated from him by a parallel
of latitude or the width of a stream.
The fervent Federalists, who have been borrowed by
the provincialists, endeav our plausibly to defend them-
selves under the pretext that unless union springs spon-
taneously from an overwhelming niajority of the people
it is not worth having.
This passionate demand by the whole people for Feder-
ation is possible only in the trail of some appalling
humiliation, which, by union, it is possible to avoid.
Let me direct the attention of the fervent Federalist to
the difficulty experienced in obtaining majorities for the
unions previously mentioned, with regard to the supreme
wisdom-of which there is now no question.
In .Scotland, Union was ushered in with the direst fore-
bodings and prophecies of misery and humiliation. The
step was taken in the face of hitter opposition ; no enthu-
siasm was manifested—nothing more than a sullen acqui-
escence. Of the wisdom of the action, and the vast
material advantage therefrom, there is now no question.
Again, those who endeavor to find an excuse for aiding
the anti-Federalist by placing a snag in the Federal
stream, make no attempt to show any evidence of any
outburst of enthusiasm at the creation of the United
States of America.
Of course, the Constitution of the United States is very
defective, and suffered much from the absence of some
of our Australian statesmen ; but it does well enough for
sixty millions of people. Notwithstanding the terrible
indictment.against- it which has been delivered by our
local authorities, and the fact that the American people
generally regret that a benign Providence did not create
these able men in sufficient time to enable them to have
fully advised the framers of that Constitution—generally
speaking, an American values his nationality more than
his stateship, and believes the Union has been a splendid
success. But there was less enthusiasm than adverse
comment at its birth. Localism (as here) was stirred up
by squibs and pamphlets abler and coarser than anything
the anti-Federalist has yet produced in Australia. Hamil-
ton and Madison were “ mere boys” ; Franklin, an “ old
dotard”; Washington, a “born fool.” “The colonies
had done well enough without union ; why make anv
change?” 1
In some of the States the proposed new Constitution
was cursed. The State of New'York approved by a bare
majority of three. No enthusiasm, few immense majori-
ties; hut sensible men deliberately did what was right in
the face of scurrilous and virulent opposition.
Again, in Canada—but everyone knows the story of the
croakings in that country and their refutation.
In no instance has Federation been accomplished by
the sweeping, overwhelming niajority which the ferveiff
federalists profess to expect in Australia, but reluctantly,
m the face of large minorities, has the step been taken,’
and in every instance it has been fully justified by subse-
quent experience.
The provincialist is busy fomenting local jealousy ; he
understands that “ a man s shirt is closer than his coat.”
The anti-Unionist scored when he persuaded the fervent
federalists to give provincialism a double chance.

The Alleged Tropical Chow.
Pritchard Morgan, the painfully British indi-
vidual who was once a Queenslander, is per-
fectly frank about his reasons for advocating
the establishment of a Chinese-and-nigger pro-
vince in Northern Australia :
His idea is to draw a line across the continent of Aus-
tralia barring off those portions wherein European labor
has been found practically impossible. This would form
a fresh colony called “North Australia,” and to it the
immigration of Chinese, coolies, etc., would be permitted.
By taking this step the colonies concerned would gain
much and would be opened up by the capitalists, who are
now scared by the impossible labor conditions, and in
return for permitting Chinese immigration you could
extract important concessions. “At present,” says Mr.
Morgan, “ when you complain to Li Hung Chang of'the
English trader being barred out of China, he smiles
sweetly and observes that his countrymen are similarly
treated in Australia.”
There is no disguise about that, anyway. Aus-
tralia, according to Morgan’s idea, is to endure
the presence of the leprous Chow, not for any
payment which it will personally receive, but
to pa,y China for admitting the English trader
to his Flowery Land. And what better will
Australia be if the English, or the Greek, or the
Yankee, or any other trader is admitted to the
Flowery Land? Also, why should this country
care one bush swear whether anybody is ad-
mitted there or not, or pay for anybody’s
admission there ? Moreover, there is no part
of Australia where Australian white labor
has ever been found impossible. In N.S.W.,
Victoria, S.A., and South Queensland a new
white race is already formed which can stand
the climate of any part of Australia. Whether
European labor is or is not possible in the
North doesn’t matter in the least ; no one asks
European labor to go there. But white Aus-
tralian labor is possible there, and the white
Australian will swarm into the North when the
South gets a little more crowded. And in any
case the Chow, as a man who is specially
adapted to a hot climate, is a very foolish
superstition. He comes from a comparatively
cool country. The most tropical part of China
is only in the same latitude as Bowen (Q.).
The coolest part of China proper is in the
parallel of Southern Tasmania, and the coolest
part of the Chinese empire is equivalent to
being far south of the furthest south part of
Maoriland—well on the way to the South Pole.
China may be averaged as being in the same
latitude as Katoomba, in the N.S. W. Blue
Mountains. r lhe Chow capital has an average
temperature five degrees colder than that of
Melbourne, and the Chow really dies off faster
in North Australia than the white man. The
only reason why he is supposed to be specially
fitted for the place is that he dies with much
less fuss—in which respect he resembles the
Kanaka, who is supposed to be specially fitted
for work in the Queensland canefields (where
he dies several times as fast as the white),
simply because the white takes no notice of his
awful death-rate. And when Pritchard
Morgan, or anybody like him, prattles through
his shiny silk hat about the special fitness of
the yellow heathen to live in the Far North,
he is gibbering through his tile in a very foolish
manner indeed.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lyne’s announce-
ment that when he becomes Premier Lyne he
will have a land-tax for Local Government pur-
poses only, ought to be made a trifle more ex-
plicit. At present, certainly, the situation is
very much mixed. A very large part of
N.S.W. is under no municipal Government and
pays no local rates. The municipally-governed
portion pays most of its own local expenses,
and a State land-tax, and (per medium of its
contribution to the general revenue) more than
half the local expenses of the part that has no
municipal Government. Theunincorporated por-
tion of the province pays a State land-tax and
(through its contribution to the general revenue)
rather less than half its own local expenses. If
Premier Lyne (supposing there ever is such a
person) established a good system of Local
Government over the whole of N.S.W. ; enacted
that local rates were to be levied on the value
of lands alone (not on improvements); enacted,
also, that the minimum rate was to equal the
present average local rates and the present
State land-tax ; abolished all State aid to
municipalities except in the thinly-peopled
regions of the far west, and greatly re-
duced the borrowing powers of munici-
palities ; and then abolished the State land-tax,
it would be in some respects as broad as it is
long. The land would pay about the same, and
the distinct advantage would be that there
would be much less buying of political support
with roads and bridges and the like. The dis-
tinct disadvantage would be that there w’ould
be little chance of applying the graduated
taxation principle and very little prospect of
the much-needed absentee tax. And, anyhow,
The Bulletin hereby sorrowfully bets its
other boots that there is nothing so comprehen-
sive and radical as this in Lyne’s programme.
R. E. O’Connor, Convention delegate from
N.S.YV. : “In the surrender of the Murray
N.S.W. has given up the exclusive rights over
the best collecting ground for the supply of
water in Australia.” The Bulletin desires to
present the usually accurate but, in this case, the
horribly ignorant “ Dick ” O’Connor with the
following: A very large part of the waters
from the N.S.W. range known as the Snowy
Mountains—the collecting-ground in question
—fall into the Snowy River and go down into
Victoria, therefore the Murray arrangement
doesn’t affect that part. The Murray doesn’t
rise in N.S.W. at all, but (under the
name of the Indi) in Victoria among
the Australian Alps, therefore N.S.W.
never had exclusive possession of the
“collecting ground” above-mentioned. As
a further commentary on the said “ex-
clusive possession,” Victoria supplies the
following tributariesGoulburn, 345 miles
long; Mitta-Mitta, 175 miles long; Lod-
dan, 225 miles ; Campaspe, 150 miles ; Ovens,
140 miles ; the Cudgewah, Corryong, and a
great network of creeks flowing down from the
Alps. N.S. VV. supplies only three creeks large
enough to have a name on the map until the
Murrumbidgee drops in, which isn’t till
the Murray is nearly out of N.S. Wales
territory. But the surrender which O’Connor
was speaking about was that of the Murray,
apart from the Murrumbidgee, and if the
Murray only is federalised, and N.S.W. retains
control of the Murrumbidgee, Lachlan, and
Darling, it is Victoria which is giving up almost
exclusive control over what is nearly, though
not quite, the best collecting-ground in Aus-
tralia. N.S.W. is giving up just about nothing
in the way of collecting-ground or water-supply.
If a politician of O’Connor’s standing doesn’t
know a little thing like this, wdiat can be ex-
pected from the smaller fry ? And what chance
has Federation, when even its leading advocates
won’t take the trouble to learn the position of
things, and block progress by sheer gross
ignorance and blundering ?
When the promised Bill authorising a Referen-
dum on the fiscal question and on Upper House
reform in N.S.W. conies along, The Bulletin
presumes that both Reid and Lyne will demand
that there must not only be a majority, but a
majority including at least 80,000 votes, before
any change is made. In this matter Protection
and Federation and Council-reform stand on
exactly the same level. Freetrade and Provin-
cialism and the existing crowd of ancient
nominees are the things which exist. They
shouldn’t be interfered with unless the country
is ripe for a change. If 80,000 voters won’t roll
up to advocate a change, then it is evident that
the country isn’t ripe. That is the way that
both Reid and Lyne argued in the case
of Federation, at all events, and this paper,
which tries to be logical as well as objection-
able, doesn’t see why the same reasoning
shouldn’t apply all round. In fact, it doesn’t
see why, on LYNE’sown reasoning, the mode of
Parliamentary election shouldn’t be altered
and Reid’s crowd be allowed to hold office,
even if nobody votes for them, unless Lyne’s
crowd can raise 80,000 votes against them.
Reid represents the statu* quo just as much as
provincialism does, and if 80,000 people won’t
take the trouble to vote that he be thrown out
then evidently the country isn’t ripe, &c., &c.
The familiar wail about the painfully hard and
green state of the country comes in just as well
in one place as in the other.
•*
Somebody draws attention to a string of
carriages “ standing in Little Collins-street,
Melb., every Sunday morning, waiting for their
godly owners—a class that howls against Sun-
day labor.” The Bulletin, with all its born
antipathy to church-carriage people, is not
quite sure that they do howl against Sunday
labor. The sanctified small capitalist who
walks to the tabernacle, attended by his un-
happy offspring, and leaves his wife to cook
the dinner, is the representative howler in this
matter. And what is a coachman’s labor, any-
how ? He prepares a horse and vehicle for a
drive, and gets a drive - “ on his own.” In short,
he does for wages very much the same thing that
an ordinary Sunday sinner who can borrow a
buggy does for love every time he gets the
chance. Ihe oppression of the workingman,
as regards driving Midas to church, lies mainly
in the fact that Alidas “ orders ” the carriage,
instead of saying to his coachman : “ Lo ! it is
the Sabbath-day, John ; suppose we drive to
church together. I will endure the sermon
whilst you wait outside on the box-seat and
praise the Lord.” From which point of view
the bloated plutocrat seems to be providing
rational Sunday recreation for the poor toiler.
The official tabooing of Sydney and Melbourne
“ hospital stamps ” by the British Philatelic
Association, or whatever it calls itself, is one
of those unexpected things which was most
likely to happen. An awful rush for these
engravings, at twelve times their postage value,
arose from the simple philatelic fact that a
certain number of wealthy cranks in various
parts of the world will pay a more or less extra-
vagant price for every stamp, and inasmuch
as the number of cranks exceeds the number of
2Jd. hospital stamps issued at 2s. 6d., dealers
reckoned themselves safe in applying for large
parcels of the silly “ stock.” Nobody cared a
dump about the hospitals; everybody was a
dealer, with usurious designs upon the
stamp - collecting plutocracy. The Sydney
scheme, subsequently imitated in Melbourne,
was an appeal to cupidity, not charity. Seem-
ingly the B.P.A. found itself cornered as
regards Australian hospital stamps ; individual
collectors couldn’t buy the stock at a reasonable
advance, so they arose in one great indignant
body and disqualified it. Unless Baron Roths-
child, or the Duke of York, or some similarly
notorious crank, can be induced to tighten the
silken bonds by getting this disqualification
removed, for the sake of Her Majesty’s loyal
Australian stamp dealers, public and private,
the hospital stamps will be worth nothing more*
than their postage value. And this, in a great
many cases, has been already destroyed by
postmarking, which the Government consented
to perform gratis at the request of philatelic
customers.

“ Hermes ” to The Bulletin:
Re your statement of 25/12/’97, the writer, as an ex-
patient, can honestly say that the Sydney Coast Hospital
scandal is a very small scandal indeed—if it is a scandal
at all. The poor down-trodden nurse is certainly “on
duty” on an average 11 hours per day, but by no stretch
of imagination can she be said to work. All the drudgery
is done by convalescents, and, failing these, men are sent
from the lock wards to do it. The dainty nurse does little
but make up a few beds in artistic style ; cool the fevered
brow, and that sort of thing; do a'little dressing, and
talk metaphysics with interesting young men patients;
while the lock-warder sweeps out the ward, scrubs the
floor and lockers, bath-bricks the tables and forms,
washes-up the dishes, polishes the forks, carts round hot
water and beef-tea, carries the food from the kitchen, and
acts as a servant generally. Nurses have nothing to do
with the laundry ; it is run by a special staff of strong
Hibernian ladies, assisted by a contingent of loek-
warders. As regards the little work the ministering angel
does do there are plenty of her to do it: and all
the talk about “duties protracted to exhaustion” is bosh.
Truly the nurses should have one-day-in-seven rest, but
even without it they are better off than thousands of
good women in domestic service, shops and factories, who,
after the doors are shut and the front lights out, have to
work much harder for as long, and often longer, hours in
a very different atmosphere. Little Bay is one of the
healthiest places in N.S.W., the ward's are all small
separate buildings, and have a continual stream of fresh
air flowing through them. But there are two people in
the Coast Hospital treatment is a scandal ;
and they are the medical superintendent and the
wardsman in charge of the lock wards. Includ- .
ing the medical superintendent there are only three- *
doctors to the whole big hospital and sanatorium, and
besides supervising the institution, the supfe, has to do a
big share of the medical work, and to be present at nl*
operations (sometimes they operate two days a week)
the hospital supplies “married” quarters for the
mechanics, who only work from 8 a.m. till spm and
never on Sundays, but the married wardsman, who has
all the dirty work of two lock-wards, and who is required
at nights and on Sundays, is charged ±5O per year by a
niggardly Government for singde quarters, and has to
feep a home in JBolany for his wife and family. And if
the Govt, can’t afford to treat these two men better, it can
afford to lessen their work by suppressing the quacks who
flood the hospitals with their wrecks after having ex-
tracted all the hard cash from their pockets.
Geo. Black, M.L.A., writes (on both sides of
the paper) as follows :
,J'! F®Pb’ to an art ' c ' e of this week’s issue, let me say
that the question asked me was : “ Is it not better to tax
foreign products than our own?” I replied that a tax on
land was, in most cases, by no manner of means a tax on
products. At the same time, your position with regard
to unimproved value is far from impregnable. Unim-
proved value chiefly arises from the concentration of
Population. When the population departs, it leaves the
land behind, but the value has emigrated—with the
people. When they form a new settlement, it again
squats on the land. But it is not in the land; it is out-
side it. Not from within but from without. An exterior
condition removable with the circumstances that oc-
casioned it. Therefore, the results of no man’s exertion
or foresiuht; therefore, strictly, no man’s property;
therefore, a fit subject of taxation.
What The Bulletin said was that the Single-
taxer is fond of splitting dreary hairs by deny-
ing that he wants to tax land, and alleging that
he only wants to tax the unimproved value of
the land. And Dibbs, in the same manner of
speaking, didn’t put any tax on the great bulk
of the articles on bis tariff—lie only taxed the
imported value of them. Tor instance, tie put
10 per cent, on telescopes and thermometers,
telephones, tacks, and iron tanks. Unless there
are people to look through the telescope, and
keep water or possibly rum in the tank, and to
drive the tack in straight, these things have no
value whatever. The same applies to every-
thing else. A whole forest of mahogany isn’t
worth a cent unless there is a popula-
tion _to use it, and all the edible
fish in the sea have no cash value unless
there are people to catch and eat them.
Everything, as well as land, owes its value to
the presence of population. And the Single-
taxer who says that he doesn’t tax land, but
only the unimproved value of land, should be
equally accurate about the Protectionist, and
say that in most cases he doesn’t tax imports,
but only the imported value of imports. On
the other hand, if the latter fellow, when he
levies ad valorem duties, taxes imports, as the
Single-tax man says he does, then the Single-
tax man does tax land, and the hair that he
splits so carefully is a very ridiculous hair, and
not worth splitting. The other thing that The
Bulletin said was that it advocated a land-
tax, and didn’t mind saying so straight-out—
that it didn’t want to waste time splitting the
small, aggravating hair aforesaid.
Federation Convention
Vignettes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
George Reid.
A sophist-statesman, ever-gay,
Whose brow no cares corrode ;
He loves the shady, crooked way,
And hates the plain, straight road.
Barton.
A full, rich-brained, rich-blooded man,
Who, when the doubting cliques
Exclaim “ We might,” says out “ I can
And knows more than he speaks.
Brunker.
A pious statesman ! Phoenix rare
In any time or clime !
He makes men wish that Virtue were
A punishable Crime.
B. R. Wise.
A brilliant man,
Too keen to rise ;
Too large of plan,
Too always—Wise.
George Turner.
A man who hates exaggeration,
This cool, strong Leader of the State ;
A soupgon of imagination
Would make him almost great.
Graham Berry.
Grey Tribune of a vanished day,
Though sunk is now your star,
You, looking on the rest, might say—
“ I was more than they are.”
Peacock.
He laughs when graven statesmen groan ;
So gay is he.
Alas ! He even now is known
As—“ L'Homme qui rit.”
Isaacs.
Incisive, keen, without endeavour ;
Were he—so friends suppose—
A little deeper, and Jess clever,
He might be what he chose.
Trenwith.
The People’s Tribune ! sound and straight,
And hard to harm ;
He lacks, to make a Leader Great,
But one thing—Charm.
Alfred Deakin.
A man of talent and of clean good sense,
Who speaks with polished air—
On silver floods of his own eloquence
Ho floats to God knows where.
Kingston, President of Convention.
His bluff voice has a hearty ring ;
The man himself is straight ;
He might be almost anything
But —Maker of a State.
Braddon.
One who, in days gone by, has been
An actor brave in dramas bold,,
But who, for this Historic Scene,
Is all too sick, and tired, and old.
Van Dobson.
Tasmania’s Tower of Strength, you see,
First Tory in that little nation —
An o d red sandstone statesman he,
Of Heav’n alone knows what formation.
Adye Douglas.
A reverend figure, Moses-like,
With glances ever rearward cast,
Who, while the Clocks of Progress strike,
Advances backwards towards the Past.
Forrest.
A kind of King Dontgiveadam,
Without regalia,
Whose air and carriage say “I am,
By G d, Westralia ! ”
CREEVE ROE.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty's “Babes in the Wood."

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK
