Royal “ Dick Whittington.”
Opera House “Jack the Giant-Killer.
Alexandra Dan. Barry’s Dramatic Co
The lamented vanishment of “ Miss
Brown” has been temporarily averted, a pro-
mising state of business having induced the Cri-
terion management to extend that lady till to-
morrow (Friday) night. In bygone times, when a
lady was extended, it was generally done by %
machine which dragged her out to an unnatural
length on account of her religious opinions—but
this is a mere aside. Next Saturday Thornton
proposes to stage “ Mamma,” which is, so far as
.Ihjs Bulletin remembers, the only modern play
in which a gentleman smacks a lady in public. A
great many ladies in this country are smacked
very hard privately—some of them, perhaps,
being considerably improved thereby—and among
them are quite a number of ladies who look the
very last persons that any one would expect to be
smacked ; but the publicity given to the spanking
of the lady in “ Mamma ” makes the case some-
what unusual. The present version of “ Miss
Brown ” has gone well so far. Miss Clare Mani-
field has Noney Seabrooke’s old part as Angela,
and does it in a bright, fresh, off-hand fashion.
She looks young enough to have just escaped
oyer the wall of a boarding-school with her
nightdress in a bag, and youth counts for a lot in
such a case. Miss Madge Corcoran is the dusky
maid from the isles where the unripe alligator
chases the mosquito in the spring, and the land-
crab goes laboriously along the pebbly beaches in
the twilight. This paper hasn’t decided in its
own mind whether Miss Corcoran is as good as
the last brown girl or not, the last brown girl being
one that requires a lot of beating ; but, if she
doesn’t get quite there, she arrives very near to it.
Messrs. Cochrane and Carey fill the places of two
gentlemen who went off with a loud report since
Thornton’s last Sydney appearance, and the rest
of the capable cast is mostly as it was before.
• * * *
In the present issue appear the portraits of
Misses Clare Manifield and Florence Poole of the
Criterion co. They attained the distinction of a
place in this usually rather unappreciative and
grumpy paper by reason of the excellent work
which they did in “Sweet Lavender.” Miss
M inifield shook a shower of golden curls at the
universe generally in the character of Lavender
to the satisfaction of a discriminating public, and
the Minnie Gilfillian of Miss Poole was a par-
ticularly agreeable vision dressed in a lot of some-
thing which looked, to the ignorant male eye, as if
it had cost a good deal and a halfpenny per yard.
Both girls did their parts cleverly and intel-
ligently, and they look nice enough to excuse the
appearance of their photos, even if they hadn’t
done so.
** * *
The present Sydney Lyceum drama goes
off on Friday, on which night Diver will crumple
up in the dock for the last time. Diver is sup-
posed to have heart disease (not aggravated corns),
and when the jury finds him guilty of pouring
Maud Williamson’s vital fluid all over the pre-
mises he slowly telescopes together with his head
hanging over the spikes, and two policemen try
vainly to prop up the remains. The curtain goes
down with the remains hanging in every direction
except the right one in the arms of policeman
H’X, and flatly refusing to stand up straight and
be lectured, while the judge, with his sentence
unfinished, vainly endeavors to catch the eye of
the deceased and get some impressive remarks off
at him. As the eye of the deceased is all over
the shop, according to how his head wobbles on
the shoulder of policeman H’X, the failure of his
Honor is quite natural. The next Lyceum pro-
duction isn’t officially announced at time of
writing.
** * *
“Babes in the Wood” has lasted into a
second edition at Her Majesty’s, and is now the
lone survivor of the three pantomimes which came
down on Sydney at Xmas. Another week will
see it extinguished also. The new edition
songs are. not more of a success in a topical sense
than the last ones, but the topical element has
been weak all round this year. Probably the
trouble was that there was very little material
available. There are no politics going, except the
Federal Convention, about which the public is
very tired ; it is unsafe to say anything severe
concerning the Carruthers libel-case, as it
might lead to the warbler being committed for
contempt; and the bank which looked like
bursting appears to have come round. Still the
panto, gets on about as well without any topical
element. Alice Leamar has quitted the co., and
Miss Carrie Moore, formerly a “Babe,” is Maid
Marion in her stead. Two much smaller babes,
Connie Solomon and Isabel Hindley, now appear
in place of the original pair, but without having
any fault to find with these two cheerful
mites, this paper prefers its babes large. The
sho.v is as beautiful as ever, and Miss Reeve's
Robin Hood is a dream of loveliness as usual. So
are most of the other girls. As for Uncle,
he is still drinking himself into an early grave as
formerly, and a tombstone that hangs sideways,
with its epitaph tipped over its ear, is being got
ready for him.
** * *
Rickards’ show at Sydney Tivoli makes
good time as usual along the heavy road to success,
and some of the old people are doing their last
nights, and some new people of immense musical
and comedy tonnage are to arrive shortly. Emily
Lyndale has reappeared, and Newbury and Austin
Rudd, having taken solid root in the public soul,
appear to stay on indefinitely.
** * *
By the time this appears in print Rickards’
pantomime will have gone to sleep with its fathers,
and Sydney Royal will have relapsed into its
usual darkness.
** * *
At Melbourne Princess's, last Saturday, a
crowded audience gave a lot of silent attention to
“ Ben-my-Chroe,” adapted and altered from “The
Deemster.” “ Ben-my-Chree ” is doubtless a
“tremendously powerful play” from every, or
nearly every, actor’s point of view. It is loaded
to the muzzle with situations and business of a
highly theatrical character. It represents the
leading characters in such a large variety of suffer-
ings that it must be a painful entertainment for
those who feel persuaded by it. Is “ Ben-my-
Chree ” a truthful-seeming story of what might
have happened on the Isle of Man 150 years
ago ? Tne present writer finds it unconvinc-
ing, and therefore dull —a gloomy disappoint-
ment, in fact. Whereas the tragic interest of
“The Manxman ” seemed to arise naturally from
the narrative, the appeals for interest in “ Ben-my-
Chree ” have a false ring. It seems a play sug-
gested by Wilson Barrett, the actor, Jor actor
Wilson Barrett, who fills the principal part, and
is seldom absent from the centre of the stage. A
very frequent recurrence of the same character in
the centre of the stage grows oppressive when the
character is not a great reality. Mr. Barrett starts
operations as an unsober young man addicted to
bursts of hollow merriment and small jokes. After
sharing a rather feeble love-scene with Miss
Jeffries, in the course of which they sing a doleful
song, he falls under false suspicion of having
visited her bedroom by stealth. Next act he has to
kill the lady’s indignant brother in self-defence,
though it were easy to explain matters, and Act
HI. brings him to trial before a couple of Judge
Windeyers, who conduct the prosecution On
bloodthirsty Manx principles, and condemn the
prisoner to death. Wilson Barrett would have to
die on the gallows at this point but for his aged
father, the bishop of the ridiculous little island.
Aged Bishop commands the Windeyer men to get
off their judgment seats, and they get, whereupon
his reverence assumes sole control of the tribunal,
by virtue of an obsolete Manx law. The sentence
of this weird, peculiar court is that Wilson Barrett
be set apart from humanity in general, and left to
endure a Robinson Cruso« existence on the Isle of
Man. Stage thunder and lightning intensifies the
horror of the situation. The court and population
retire hastily, and a tableau of the felon kneeling
alone amidst the fury of the elements brings the
judicial proceedings to a close.
* * * *
For the next few months Wilson Barrett
is a sad and solitary herring-catcher, and then a
young fisherman (the' least irrational being in the
whole show) coming to see him, despite the paren-
tal Bishop’s decree, takes him away towards his
best girl, Mona. Things are going badly with
Mona. The Governor of the island has lodged
a malicious charge of unchastity against
her, but the dear old Bishop excavates
another Minx law which enables the virgin,
to proclaim her innocence in church, there-
by convicting his Excellency the Governor of
criminal libel. Miss Jeffreys swears, also Wilson
Barrett comes along to confirm her oath, and the
Governor is arrested on the spot. Good old
Bishop, having praised his son, W. 8., for nobly
testifying the lady’s innocence, orders him off for
execution in fulfilment of the original Windeyer
sentence. Fortunately, Mona drops dead in her
lover’s arms. By the light of the lime streaming
down upon his countenance one can see that Wil-
son Barrett hopes to rejoin his sweetheart in
Heaven the moment he is hanged. Here falleth
the curtain on a play which, according to London
D.T. and other reliable journals, “ unlocks the
casket of human nature and shows us the deeper
man, the truer woman, the nobler life.’’ Rats !
• • * *
“ Ben-niy-Chreo ”is beautifully mounted
(scenery by Goateher), and the stage-management
is melodramatically effective. Wilson Barrett’s
performance is strongest in the manslaughter
scene, but throughout the piece he acts up to the
best traditions of the school in which he was
trained, and the roll of his elocution should be an
example for the youth of this country. Pretty
Miss Jeffries scores as Mona, and the company
give able support all round. If “ Ben-my-Chree ”
runs for a second week several members of the co.
will get honorable mention in next issue. But
this is a quick-change season, and “Silver King ”
will be wanted shortly.
** * *
The pantomime at the Royal published its
second edition on Saturday. The new items
were probably an improvement on some that got
cleared away to make room for them, and the
brightest item in the panto. - a coon song-atid-
dance by eight youngsters—had been left in much
tie same condition as at first. “Dick Whitting-
ton.” seemingly, will run for about six more
nights and two matinees, and when the bulky
spectacle has breathed its last in Melbourne the
Holloway-Anderson management will produce
“Grattan Riggs,” or some other drama of that
description.
* * *
A few more remarks about the wonderful
Hegelmanns at Melb. Opera House are postponed
indefinitely because the Hegelmanns, and Miss
Lilian Tree, and Whitburn, and every other mem-
ber of the present variety co., will shift their
quarters after Friday. The Rickards’ pantomime
from Sydney is due at the Opera House on Satur-
day next (sth), and, seeing that several gorgeous
accounts of “Jack the Giant Killer ” have been
received in advance, the forthcoming sensation
may reckon on a boomsome greeting when it
arrives. Prices for the panto, season are fixed at
3,2, and 1, and Melbourne is cordially invited to
book its seats a fortnight in advance.

“ Never Too Late to Mend ” is putting in
a week at Melb. Alexandra, where Charles
Reade’s respectable old drama puts a severe strain
on the resources of Dan Barry’s eo. At times like
the present The Bulletin regrets to mention
that the resources aforesaid don’t amount to much.
Some of the Haleck comedians can show a little
cause and reason for their histrionic tendencies,
but there are others among them who should be
arrested in their mad career and bound down not
to act for the remaining term of their, natural
lives. All the same, “Never too Late.” drew a
fair'y large attendance on the first night, and the
announcement of a Startling Novelty for next
Saturday indicates that Dan Barry has further
designs upon his friends in front.
At Poverty Point .
A theatrical divorce-case now on the tapis should make
pood reading. It seems the husband is a barman, whom
the lady—very beautiful and very talented—somehow
or other contrived to marry four years ago. She declares
she left him next day, and that no conjugal fondlements
occurred. He tells another tale, and defies her to divorce
him, as his record is clean.
At a variety show in an Australian city on a recent
Saturday night three items were contributed hy female
serio-comics. All three songs were about prostitutes. Of
late street-walking has quite displaced child-birth and
under-garments in our 'alls as a humorous theme.
Melbourne tobacconist Bentwieh, who some years ago
printed a fat testimonial from G. A. Sala on his bags
(trade bags, not personal), is retiring from biz. “ Day
tickets at Bentwitch’s” has been a familiar line in Melb.
theatrical advts. for years, but he had no wild longing to
sell benefit tickets. His laugh is said to be the heartiest
in Australia, but the man who heard him laugh is so far
undiscovered.
Adelina Patti has been left a widow by the faithful
Nicolini, who for years watched over her valuable health
and checked the diva’s occasional disposition to take half-
an-ounce more of food or wine than was good for her.
Tenor Nicolini was an operatic hero until about the time
when he formally took up the position of Patti’s husband
in succession to the Marquis de Caux. Nic. had figured
as the co. in the Marquis s divorce suit.
Patti could never get people to tolerate Nicolini. He
was a shiny, hlack-e.ved man, always dressed in Continen-
tal-cut garments, and a tall hat, and patent-leather
boots. He looked a sort of lackey', and not. of the first
class either. And he had no conversation. But he could
sing, and play' billiards, and never gave Adelina any
trouble, and so was suited to the sphere of work to which
it pleased Providence and the diva to call him.
Albani will be accompanied to Australia by that sweetest
and most effective of all ballad tenors, Orlando Harley,
who was fairly worshipped here during his all-too short
previous visit. The veteran will also have with her Miss
Sarah Berry, contralto, and basso Pauli.
They were speaking of “ Virginius ” at the Princess bar
in an interval between drinks. “Who is the author of
this piece, anyhow?” asked the fatman. “Knowles,”
taid his friend over his glass. “ Knowles ? That’ll be the
seller that was singin’ here a while back. Shouldn’t ’a
thought it was in his line.”
Sheridan Knowles, author of “ Virginius,” renounced
the stage and took to Baptist preaching. An
evangelical brought this under Henry Irving’s notice.
Irving pointed out that Knowles went on taking the
royalties from his plays as long as he lived. He had mar-
ried a brilliant young actress, Miss Elphinstone, whose
portrait appears as Constance, in ‘The Love Chase,’in
Cumberland's ‘ British Drama.’ It is remarkable that
Miss Elphinstone, though comparatively in the heyday of
.youth, took on the drab color even more strongly than
Knowles.

all variously settled. Fred. Kaye married little
Murray', of the co., on landing in London ; the cold-
looking, floppy Blanche Massey married an English ad-
mirer ; Decima Moore accepted her Hope, and has to keep
the family pot boiling; Ethel SeUvyn left the boat, at a
way-port, and hasn't yet returned to public life; Claire
Leighton, in Sydney, married Mr. Alf. Marks and settled
down to country life, and intends soon to take a trip to
Europe with her husband and infant daughter.

“ Anything beautiful must be true,” writes Mrs. Brown-
Potter in the New York Journal. Well, Mrs. B.P. her-
self is passing fair, but see what she says “ Labor is
sacred. It is prayer. It is the watchword of the living,
it is the guerdon of the dead, the crown of the immortal.
When the final ‘ Well done, thou good and faithful ser-
vant,’ is said, it is the labor of life that is beautified, and
after the stress and storm it is the labor that cives us
‘rest.’ ” It may be a good thing to “ scorn delights and

that way.
Ketten, that eccentric musician whose genius cannot
now be regarded as problematical, is happily dead. In
one of his many moments of aberration he presented his
portrait, to a distinguished Sydney pressman, with the sub-
joined autograph legend: “To the Editor of the
• Henry Ketten, Sydney, April, 1881.” Medioc-
rity so highly appreciates genius that the picture is now
exposed for sale in a George-street music-seller’s ware-
house, priced at one guinea. Poor Ketten! Poor editor!
Lion-and-elephant story. The lion that rides on the
back of the elephant in Fitzgeralds’ circus noticed on one
occasion that the elephant had a sore neck, and was
much attracted by the raw flesh, which he ominously
smelt at and growled over. The elephant, perceiving
this, lifted up his trunk and gave the lion a thwack which
knocked him almost silly. After which the king of beasts
meddled no more with the elephant’s sore neck.

Boor old organist Best’s widow and children are very
badly off ; and so far there seems to Ue no offer of assist-
ance. Best was not over-popular with the profession, who
envied his attainments ; and, to this day, one of the best-
known musicians in Sydney', whom Best repeatedly
snubbed, actually declares that the latter was a third-
rate organist. Also, Best was about the only man on
record who ordered two whiskies-and-sodas for himself at
once. The slowness of Australian waiters, he used to
declare, was one of the wonders of the world.
Apropos the Sydney question-of-the day, what consti-
tutes evening-dress? Alexis Soyer, cordon bleu, econo-
mic philosopher, bon enfant, some 60 years ago (what
time he was chef at London Reform Club) had a singular
wrestle with this question —to the astonishment of
George Nugent, the fidus Achates of Lumley, manager of
Her Majesty’s Theatre. It was de rigueur to go to H.
Majesty’s in evening-dress of English conventionality.
Soyer had sought ingress in artistic raiment of splendid
velvet and brilliant silk, only to be denied by the polite
myrmidons of Nugent. One night he turned up in fault-
less black with white tie, and the rest of it, and was, of
course, “passed.” But, alas for Nugent! The cunning
Ajexis had had “claw-hammer” and vest so fashioned
with false, shiftable cuffs, lapels, and so on, manageable
by artfully concealed wires, that on pulling at a certain
tape which Alexis did pull at, once prominently seated
the raven blackness of “ evening-dress” became trans-
mogrified into a radiancy of colors, aurorean, re-
splendent.

One of Harry Baulton’s co. re Christmas trip from
Maoriland to Tasmania ; “ We absolutely couldn’t keep
warm on hoard. It was frightfully cold, and the man
who didn’t shut the door after him ran big risk of being
thrown overhoard. Then we struck fearful heat, a big
fire, and Amy Sherwin in Tassy. Paulton was the first to
go front the heat, and ice had to be applied to his head at
short intervals. Alma Stanley collapsed next,, and most
of the rest of us followed suit. Business? A mere
handful seemed to drop in unconsciously. Amy was
packing her crowds wherever she could.”
“ H.’s” remarks here on the play of “The Manxman”
fall in line with what Oscar Wilde said ten years ago of
Hall Caine, novelist, even before Wilson Barrett had
dramatised him. “He shouts so loud that you can’t hear
him ” was the verdict of that acute critic. Did he have a
melancholy foreboding that “The Christian ” was to be
the climax of that artistic vociferation ?
When Probasco’s circus was leaving Ktunara (M L.), a
pony belonging to boy Walter St. Leon bolted, jumped
over a 20ft. cliff, and walked away as if he hadn’t done
anything out of the common.
The teacher of a Sydney private school took his pupils
on a holiday trip at Xmas, and more than paid expenses
by holding concerts at the various centres visited. Thus
is a new horror added to places where the only prayer
that strays heavenward is, “Give us this day our daily
stranger.”
Amorv Sullivan, whose death was reported in last
week’s Bullbtin, and who was then alluded to as Barry
Sullivan's youngest son, desires to say that he isn't the
youngest son of Harry S., and that he is still alive.
Under these circumstances, he will be allowed to live.
At the same time, Tub Bulletin requests Mr. Sullivan
not to let this sort of thing happen too often. If that
gentleman happens to he dead in this paper
again he will have to stay dead, whether he is
dead or not. Owing to a too-continuons diet of pickles
and cigar- moke the nerves of The Bulletin staff are
none of the best, and gentlemen who call to say that they
aren't dead give it the jumps. The said staff doesn't see
why any man should want to complain about a little
thing like that. Most people are dead and have been
dead most of the time, and it isn’t fashionable to be alive,
and there is also very little money in it, and in most
cases it isn’t even ornamental.
The Federal Convention, in an unwonted
burst of plain horse-sense, has decided that,
except in cases where the State is involved, the
appeal to the British Privy Council shall be
abolished. The Convention holds that there is
something ridiculous about the idea of sending
Judge Way to London, 1(5,000 miles away, to hear
Australian cases, and sending the cases there after
him, and then the case, and Way, and the deci-
sion all coming Lack again in the same boat.
Premier Turner, in a very bad attack of drivel,
declared that he would rather sacrifice the
Federal Court than give up the right of
appeal, and Premier Braddon maundered dread-
fully about the right of appeal being a “ tie,” the
removal of which would “sap the foundations of
the Constitution.” He also declared that every
British subject had the sacred right of appeal to
the Privy Council, yet when Isaacs pointed out
that the people of England, Scotland, Ireland and
Wales didn't have it, Braddon looked not in the
least ashamed of his unspeakable ignorance, and
drivelled right on. Reid set down his 18-stone of
solid carcase on the Privy Council idea, and de-
manded a final Court of Appeal in Australia, and
finally the precious tie that binds us to the mother-
country burst with a loud pop.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
There is still time for the Federal Convention
CO think seriously over the public debt problem. Even if
the delegates can’t agree about the value of their railways
or rivers or any other assets, the debt question is so very
simple a proposition as to be almost beyond argument.
The smallest debt in Australia is Victoria’s £4O 12s. 4d.
per head. If the Federation, took over the whole of Vic-
toria’s debt and £4O 12s. 4d. per head of the debt of the
other provinces (the remainder being left for the present
as a provincial liability) every province wou'd be relieved
of exactly the same liability in proportion to population ;
even those 50 Kilkenny cats, the Federal delegat es, could
hardly dispute that. The next trouble is that the same
amount of debt doesn’t carry exactly the same amount
of interest, but that could be got over by an easy
process of account-keeping- until the debts were converted.
If N.S.W.’s interest bill on the amount taken over was the
smallest —say 1 110 s. per inhabitant per annum—the per-
fectly intelligible process would be to charge each of the
other provinces with its own particular excess over that
figure and deduct the difference from its share of the
Federal surplus. As the debts fell due they would be
converted into a uniform Federal stock at low interest,
and when they were all converted the account-keeping
process would cease.
Some method of taking over the whole provin-
cial debt might be devised later on, but this much can be
done immediately. The reason why something should be
done immediately is obvious enough. The credit of
Federated Australia must be a little better than that of
the separate provinces. If it is only ss. per cent, better the
conversion process, even on the comparatively small
amount which would be taken over under this scheme,
would save the Commonwealth about £325,000 per
year; if it is 7s. 6d. per cent, better the saving
would be about £500,000 ; and if the Commonwealth
took over the debts which mature earliest the benefit of
this reduction in the annual tribute to Bull would begin
to be felt very speedily. On the other hand, if the Com-
monwealth doesn’t take over the public debts, or any
part of them, its superior credit is simply running to
waste. The Federal name on the back of the great Aus-
tralian p.n. would cost nothing, and would save a lot in
interest, and if the provinces refuse to make use of it they
are throwing a big income overbpard out of pure cussed-
ness. The five provinces have about £7,000,000 of debt to
be renewed in the next two years, which is a very good
reason why the matter should be attended to note. The
conversion of this small sum,' to begin with, by the
Federal Government might easily save £20,000 per
annum.
••' * •
The municipality of Leichhardt has a loan of
£5400 falling’ due, and is struggling hard to get it renewed
at 5 per cent. If the 41 pocket-handkerchief munici-
palities of Sydney would only unite on the London County
Council principle, their joint credit should enable them to
convert their debts as they fall due into a 3.) or 3j, or per-
haps even a 3 per cent, liability, whereas the rate now
spreads from 4to 8 per cent. But the harrowing little
Bumble of Botany looks on the inhabitant of Botany
North as the same kind of violent and fraudulent out-
lander as the South Australian appears to the N.S.W.
1 ederal delegate, and the idea of federating with him is
scouted.
* • * *
The outrush of gold from Australia seems to
nave eased a little in the last quarter of 1897. During the
three months the N.S.W. banks’ stock of coin increased by
£12,917, and now stands at £5,941,587. The Victorian
accumulation increased by £16,657, and stands at
£7,358,753.
• ♦ * *
The A.J.S. Bank, for the second half of 1897
reports a profit of £1725, which is pqual to rather less than
6s. per cent, per annum on capital. But the previous
half-year showed a loss of £53;506, and the half ended
December, 1896, produced a loss of £48,200, so even this is
a great improvement. The report, alleges that the differ-
ence between the present profit and the recent loss repre-
sents the difference between the old ti per cent, interest
and the present 3 per cent, rate, but the 1 i percent, in
question doesn’t account for it'-by a. good way. Possibly
the balance is made up by economy in working expenses,
or by taking a more sanguine view of the securities.
During the half-year, the cash and liquid securities have
gone up from £1,165,000 to £1,725,000, which is a large
increase. Note circulation has increased £16,000, and
there is an improvement of over £130,000 in deposits. Ti e
cheerful manner in which old “A” and “B” deposits,
new fixed deposits, and current accounts are mixed to-
gether makes it difficult to say exactly where the increase
comes in, but the total has gone up to the extent above-
mentioned.
It is very hard to tell the exact situation, or to
define the prospects of the A.J.S. In 1893, the old
reserves (about £750,000) were set aside to cover losses in
realising the old assets. At June, ’96, there remained
£374,000 of this, and then the balance-sheet ceased to
give particulars about it. Six months ago it was men-
tioned casually that about £95,000 remained, and how
much remains now to cover further losses on liquidation
of old assets, or how much old assets remain to be liqui-
dated, or how unpromising they may be, is all a mystery.
When the last remnant of that old reserve goes the rest
of the depreciation must come out of earnings, or reserve
fund, or capital, or out of whatever profits may be made
by buying up “ B” deposits at a discount, and how far
they can stand the shock—if there is a shock—is another
mystery. It was broadly stated in London some time ago
that the capital was practically all lost, and if it is
assumed that the shareholders’ funds should earn 3 per
cent., which is about the average now, it would seem on
the present profits, that only £115,000 out of the alleged
£1,219,000 of shareholders’ funds really survive. Yet
there has been no writing-off and no suggestion of any,
and as the A.J.S. has less than £400,000 callable capital, it
can’t afford to write very much off anyhow.
Three-fourths of the assets consist of “ bills and
all other debts and other assets at old valuation,
£6,720,855.” How much is “assets at old valuation”
doesn’t appear. Presumably the old valuation is the in-
flated value as it stood in the optimistic ante-smash days
of 1892. There are awful possibilities under this item,
and no estimate of these possibilities is given. The A.J.S.
is—so far as the writer can recollect—the only recon-
structed bank of any size which still keeps the old boom
values standing in its balance-sheet. How far the profits
which may be made by buying up “ B ” deposits at a dis-
count may go to square things depends on the amount of
the loss, and the amount of “ B ” deposits which the bank
can buy, and the price it gets them at, and the possible
revival of trade and expansion of property values, and
other things. In fact, the only things which seem reason-
ably certain about the A.J.S. are that things look much
better than they did six months ago, when the A.J.S. was
the most awful-looking bank in Australia ; that the profits
are still less than those of any other bank in the Seven
Provinces; and that most of the information which
should be in the published balance-sheet isn’t there. An
application of the Victorian banking law in this case
would cast a good deal of light on some things that are
now very obscure.
* * • •
The Queensland National Bank reports that its
half-year’s profits, after providing “substantially” for
bad debts, is £Booo—no odd shillings, no pence, no beastly
farthing. The “ substantially” and the exact £BOOO seem
to imply that the bank put away towards losses as much
as it thought it could spare, while showing a decent pro-
fit, and that it doesn’t pretend to any fatal accuracy in
the figures. Compared with the loss of £25,425 last half,
the present alleged £BOOO profit, which equals 34 per cent,
per annum on capital, looks respectable, yet it is a violent
drop from recent expectations. The Q.N.B. is supposed
to save £56,000 per half- year by its great reduction of de-
posit interest. The last complete half-year at the old
rate the loss was £1607. The first complete half-year at the
new rate the profit, instead of being £54,393, is set down at
£BOOO. At June, 1896, the alleged protit was £10,021, so
now, after saving £112,000 a year interest, things are
£2OOO worse on paper than they were 18 months ago.
The cash and liquid securities (£1,494,293) are some
£30,000 less than they were six months back, and the un-
reconstructed deposits (£1,837,803) seem to be £770,000
less than they were last half-year. Even of this com-
paratively small amount one-half (£918,892) is Govern-
ment cash. The private depositor seems to lie very low
indeed.
The Q.N.B. balance-sheet is a difficult thing to
draw up. That concern gave new deposit receipts for
15s. in the £, the difference to go towards writing off
losses. At the same time it agreed to pay the other ss.
in the £ by instalments out of profits, so it still remains a
liability. If this liability doesn’t appear in the balance-
sheet that document doesn’t show anything like the
amount the bank owes. If it does appear, then the
assets must either remain at their old bogus value to
square the accounts or the accounts must be balanced by
some such gruesome entry as “ Loss, £2,100,000.” It
strikes a middle course. The £458,000 which is to be re-
paid to the Government out of profits is put down as a
liability, and the £1,039,000 odd which is to be repaid to
private depositors out of profits isn’t put down, and the
assets are apparently partly written down and partly not
—in other words, they seem to be written down far
enough to make things balance. The most beautiful
asset among them is “ debts in suspense, pending realisa-
tion of securities, £742,834.” The Q.N.B. has now
£456,000 paid-up capital, of which £286,000 is repre-
sented by “ premises, furniture and stationery,” leaving
just £170,000 to trade upon.
Of the .£BOOO profit, .£2OOO goes to reserve fund,
£4OOO towards repaying the ss. in the £ written off pri-
vate deposits, which, at this rate, will be all paid off in
about 129 years, and £2OOO towards repaying the odd ss.
in the £ of Government deposits which, at the same rate,
will be all liquidated in 114 J years. But as the Govern-
ment’s whole reconstructed 20s. in the £ (£1,833,326) has
to be paid by 1921 (23 years hence) it is evident that the
Q.N.B. will have to get a violent hustle on itself. (The
private depositors are to be repaid any time between now
and the general resurrection, so they don’t matter.) The
Q.N.B.’s reconstructed deposits now cost it only £2 12s. 6d.
per cent, interest all round, and even at that very moder-
ate rate it certainly isn’t doing much good. It would be
a risky thing to say that it looks solvent even on paper
despite all the writing down.
The directors make a weird announcement that
they have accepted the surrender and forfeiture of certain
shares. The Q.N.B. shares are not assets at present;
they merely consist of a liability of £2 per share, and if
things go badly the calling up of that amount is the only
hope the depositors have of receiving their money. Under
these circumstances, the idea of voluntarily relieving any
individual of his liability seems remarkable. Q.N.B.
deposits are now selling somewhere below 10s. in the £,
and if the bank would call up all its available capital so
as to give it some spare cash, or could turn a lot of its
dead assets into money, and would buy these deposits on a
large scale, it would make a profit on the transaction that
would do a lot towards putting it on its feet again. But
it apparently won’t, or dare not, make calls, and it never
seems to accumulate any spare cash worth mentioning.
• • * *
The fading process still goes on as regards the
profits of the Ballarat Banking Co., the half-year’s net
takings being £2637. Seven half-years’ profits how show
as follows:
December, 1894 . .£3710 December, 1896 . .£2936
June, 1895 .. 3449 June, 1897 .. 2881
December, 1895 .. 3463 December, 1897 .. 2637
June, 1896 .. 3250
The increased during the half-year, and the
bank has £70,046 in cash and Government debentures,
which is equal to 33 per cent, of its liabilities to the public
—one of the strongest cash reserves in Australasia. The
usual 5£ per cent, dividend absorbs £2337, and £5OO is
added to reserve, making it £53,500. But, in doing this,
the profit-and-loss balance is diminished by £2OO, so in
reality the funds are only increased by £3OO. In view of
these things, the management should drop that aggra-
vating odd i per cent, at its dividend. The 5J percent,
div. seems to be an irritating tradition with this concern.
The Ballarat Bank has a good reputation as one of the
soundest concerns in the seven provinces, but in these
times of low interest, 5 per cent, should satisfy any body
of shareholders.

That eminent three-ball institution, the N.S.W.
Mont de Pi6t6—where you can leave your watch at half
the average rate of interest charged by the private Moss
Cohenstein—realised £4297 last half-year, as compared
with £4225 and £4284 for two previous half-years, so
apparently the public has been leaving its watch there
with even more energy than usual. AlO per cent, divi-
dend (£3679) is declared ; £3OO goes to reserve, making it
£13,900; £3OO to contingency fund, making £4654, and
there is a small balance over. The Mont de Pi6td is the
one Sydney institution whose 10 percent, seems a dead cer-
tainty in all weathers, and when manager Lewis Levy,
J.P., sits discounting bills in the comfortable office, and
sees all that is left of Pharaoh, with two thin legs and no
stomach worth mentioning, hawking brass jewellery on
the wet pavement outside, he doubtless feels that things
might be a lot worse.

The Mutual Life Insurance Co. of New York
issues a gorgeous statement for 1897. Assets are now
£52,100,000, an increase during the vear of £3,900,000.
The surplus (£7,290,000) has increased by £1,190,000. The
annual income for ’97 was £11,121,000, which is £700,000
more than the income for ’96, and the new insurances
effected during the year were within a very small amount
of £28,000,000.
* * » *
The aldermen of Camperdown (Sydney) have
formally resolved, in view of the many recent shortages in
municipal accounts, all of which the auditors punctually
failed to discover, that the present system of municipal
audit is a gruesome farce. But considering the recent
case of auditor Groves, of Rookwood, who was fined £2
and costs by the unspeakably pig-headed Parramatta
bench for refusing to certify to the correctness of
accounts, which even the witnesses for the prosecution
(according to the newspaper report of the case) admitted
were not correct, what kind of decent auditing do the
Camperdown aldermen expect ? In Groves’s case, by the
way, the awful Justice Department, being appealed to,
briefly replied that it couldn’t interfere with the sentence
of the court. Considering how many fines are remitted
and sentences commuted by that Department, this is a
remarkable statement. Anyhow, it is distinctly the busi-
ness of the Justice Department to see that these Parra-
matta beaks never sit on a bench again.
• * * »
The Perpetual Trustee Co. (Sydney) after a long
struggle in its earlier days, seems to have at last become
a steady dividend-paying - concern. Last year’s profit was
£3095, whereas the takings for 1896 were £2967 ; for 1895,
£2571 ; for 1894, 2702 ; and for 1893, • 2414. A dividend
of 6 per cent, absorbs £l5OO ; another £l5OO is added to
reserve, making' it 45500; and the remaining 195, with
previous balance, leaves £592 to carry forward. If the
Perpetual manages to avoid the big-dividend thirst which
has reduced some Southern companies in the same line to
a state of shaky uncertainty and aggravated financial
grog-blossom, and resolves to have a reserve of at least
a 100,000 before it goes on any festive outbreaks, it should
have a fine future ahead of it.
* * * *
The Australian Kerosene Oil and Shale Co. has
closed up its soap and candle works at Parramatta
(N.S.W.), “in consequence of the influx of foreign-made
candles.” And yet candles don’t get visibly cheaper to
the ordinary consumer who uses a tallow-dip to go to
bed by.
* * * *
Business never was so bad with the Melbourne
money-lenders. The biggest of them told the writer last
week that since the new year he hadn’t lent £5, and
that the only enquiries he was getting for money were
from people who “ vanted to rob him.”
» • * *
The big drawbacks frauds down South don’t
seem to have led to any definite action so far. It would
appear that this is to be another mining-lease job—to be
laid to rest by a red-herring commission of enquiry.

A second sudden retirement of a big man’s big
man occurred down South the other day. As in the other
case, although the alleged discrepancies are large, nothing
further is likely to be heard of the matter. Knowledge
is indeed power.
* * * *
There has been considerable excitement in Syd-
ney speculating circles over an alleged invention for mak-
ing inflammable gas, for which patent was applied for 13th
Jan. by G. \V. Lee, agent; Frank Elliott, plumber ; and
Geo. Tatham, engineer. Before information re the inven-
tion was generally available the original £TO syndicate
shares (said to be 400) were reported as changing hands
at three and four times that sum. The idea is to pass
moist air over a mixture of diluted sulphuric ether and
citronella oil—thus carburetting it and making it inflam-
mable ; and an automatic device for regulating the flow is
included. The awful secrecy in which the affair has
been enshrouded has helped to make investors bite ; but
so far there seems to be no published expert evidence
as to the novelty or efficacy of the invention.
» • * •
The British Broken Hill co. has lodged a P.C.
appeal against the Vic. Full Court award of £4240 damages
to the Australian Smelting Co. on a breach of contract.
Whatever the legal merits of this hazy dispute, the differ-
ence between the Full Court’s judgment and the modest
£2O which Judge Hodges awarded in a previous trial of the
same issue seems to justify the defendant co’s appeal. The
alleged breach of contract is a tale of years ago when the
8.8. H co. and the Dry Creek Smelting Works were con-
trolled by what was practically one and the same board of
management. The bond, so says the defendant co., was
not regarded at the time as a possible source of trouble,
but rather as a friendly agreement which might be sus-
pended, strained or broken to suit the interests of either
party. Later on, the 8.8. H. co. “sacked” its original
directors ; the Smelting co. was reconstructed under new
management, and what had been practically one indivi-
dual became two. After a long interval of silence re the
unfulfilled contract, legal proceedings came to pass.
Judge Hodges heard all the argument, and gave £2O
compensation to the plaintiff. Subsequently the same
old story was told to Judge Williams, who fell off a bike
and got severely shaken before he had prepared his judg-
ment. Judge Williams, it seems, put his notes before the
Full Court, and that learned tribunal multiplied the
Hodges award by 212.
• * • •
Broken Hill Prop, directors have no important
announcements to make this half. The chief is that
exploration in the sulphide zone has been suspended, as
“ there is sufficient ore already proven to ensure the
largest possible output for a number of years to come ”
which, of course, is as delightfully vague as you would
expect. Also, the square-set system of timbering is to be
continued on a very limited scale only ; for the rest, as
ground is worked out the excavations will be packed up
with waste (though it is true the report doesn’t even say
that much). Anyhow, as declared in this column about
ninety 7 and nine times, the square-set sy r stem of timbering
with soft-wood, and most likely 7 any other wood, is of
itself quite inadequate ; and no man who wasn’t trained
in an American mine, where the conditions are very dif-
ferent, ever did, rely on it. Moreover, the timber bill
shows a big difference already 7. There was a time when
£lOO,OOO used to be down on this line in the co.’s half-
yearly sheet; this half the total is £15,984.

Latest official assays from the 715 ft. working in
Block 10 give 15 4oz. silver, 23-3 per cent, lead, and 26 per
cent. zinc. This, by 7 the way, is by far the most import-
ant working on the Barrier at present.
• • • •
A B.H. Prop, figure or two : Total divs. to date
(the mine is 13 years old), 46,520,000 ; this is in addition
to bonuses in cash and shares. Total silver (to Nov. last),
90,775,1530 z.; lead, 336,977 tons ; copper, 3593 tons ; gold
(first recovered in ’9O), 27,1580 z. ; besides by-products,
such as antimony and zine. The plant has cost about
£828,000 ; now set down at £279,758. In wages and sala-
ries during the last half-year £178,350 was spent; for
coke, t'67,234 ; coal, 4 25,462 ; freight, £40,000. Stamps
(postage) ran away with £353. And the fire in Block 12
cost £14,803 —which presumably includes the bonus of £1
per man to the couple of hundred men who risked their
lives and spoiled their Sunday clothes, and by whose “ able
assistance ” (directors’ words) and the officers’ efforts, “ a
very serious catastrophe was prevented.”
* * • *
During the last three months of ’97 and the first
month of ’9B not enough rain fell at Broken Hill or at
Stephen’s Creek (whence the town derives its water-
supply) to be registered by gauge. Because fair rains
have fallen along the coast, city people don’t seem to
recognise how appalling is the drought in the back
country of _N.S. W. and S.A. In fact, in very large areas
there, this is the ’96 drought not yet broken up.
* » * •
The blessings of true mateship have been fairly
well illustrated in old N.S. Wales, as well as in New Aus-
tralia, writes a Bulletin correspondent. The Wyalong
goldfield has been worked on the co-operative sy 7 stem, and
experience has shown that human nature is not y 7 et ripe
enough to give confidence that the other fellows are doing
their fair share of work. Moreover, it has been found
that amongst eight men—the usual claim is eight men’s
ground—there are usually at least three, and sometimes
eight, different methods of working proposed. Then when
a chance to sell the claim arrives, seven of the partners
are willing to take £lOO or £2OO each, but the last man
sticks out for about £5OOO for his interest, and when the
sale is off he offers his share for a fiver and two drinks.
Sometimes the mates try to freeze one another out—just as
their “ betters ” do—with various kinds of success. Co-
operation in mining is a fine, large idea, and would work
well amongst men who thought they were all equal in
every respect, but on the Wyalong field the mates do not
admit equality. Each one is better than all the others, so
we have sometimes seen a good old fight with bare
knuckles on the surface; while at others we have seen
contradictory shafts being sunk, each faction ignoring the
other—when they were not waging a war of extermination
as in a notable recent instance.

men who were “ handy with the pen ” got large fees for

English, the said experts, in most instances, lacking the
verbal opulence necessary to give their opinions realistic
f )rm. One expert who got £lOOO for his report paid hi»
ghost” £2OO for a couple of days’ word-painting.

An old friend of The Bulletin, writing from
Kalgoorlie, predicts that the “coming” field this year
will be Mt. Leonora, situate 70 miles from Menzies’, and
says there are people sanguine enough to believe that it
will vie with Kalgoorlie. Bewick, Moreing and Co., the
big English mining people, are said to be “ up to their
necks ” already in the new district.
• ♦ * *
What marvellous patches there must be in the
awful Never-Never country between Menzies and the
Murchison, in the Lawler’s district! Ex-mine-managei
Leyland and party have had a “ show” there since 1894.
A few cwts. —picked, of course—have been taken away on
pack-horses ; 11 cwt. in ’94 gave432oz.; 26 cwt. in the next
year gave 1310 oz. ; 13 cwt. in ’96 gave 260 oz.—this besides
several thousands dollied out. But it’s exciting work.
The party has had 18 horses speared ; and only the other
day Leyland had his head cut open by a native’s kylie.

W.A. Minister for Mines Wittenoom was dis-
tinguished by being burnt in effigy at Bulong this week.
Cause : his new regulation (probably illegal) prohibiting
alluvial miners from sinking more than 10ft.—denounced
also at a big Kalgoorlie mass meeting. Westralia is run
in the interests of the British investor nowadays, and of
the Ministerial entourage which picks the British in-
vestor’s bones ; and as the 8.1. likes big unscratched
areas the Govt, is going to give them to him. If the
working miner objects, the working miner may go to the
devil—but he mustn’t go below 10ft. The Bulletin, is
glad to see the much-trodden-on worm showing fight at
last
* * * *
Chinaman’s luck ! Twenty nine years ago a
“ Chow” worked an alluvial gold-patch in the bed of the
Ovens, Vic., and struck a couple of reefs. But the sludge
buried them; and having enough from the alluvial he went
back to China. Lately he returned, and after four days’
scraping - struck those same reefs, still untouched.
* * * *
Compared with January, ’97, the Gympie mines
are exactly 100 per cent, ahead on last week’s Exchange
prices. The Phoenix and Glanmire lines of reefs are
mainly responsible.

A correspondent : —Re par. in “Wild Cat” page.
The Jew is rarely seen on new rushes. I have been on
many in Victoria, Queensland, and Maoriland, and have
observed that until the field was tested and found all
there the Semitic presence was not noticeable. Have
only known two Jews as genuine diggers in my experience
—and they ate pork. The Jew follows up and deals with
the needs of a settled population. Levy, of the Burgess,
Kelly, and Sullivan syndicate, in Nelson province (M.L.),
was an illustration in point. Barney Barnato was another,
in a more legitimate direction. The Jew doesn’t graft for
gold with pick and shovel, but with brains. He sits around
while others fill up. Then he empties them.

In a private letter from Johannesberg — “The
crowd at the railway-station as each train leaves here puts
me in mind of the struggling masses at Spencer-street on
Melb. Cup-day. Everybody who can get away is leaving
—some for Klondyke, others for Japan, where mechanics
are said to be getting fabulous screws. ... I could not
have believed that a large city like this could go down as
it has gone. No business doing, and I myself leave it, like
most of them, empty-handed. Bad government and cor-
rupt administration! ”

The man Guest, mysteriously slain ata free-
and-easy house in Darlinghurst, last week, is de-
scribed as a “ backer of horses,” and the discovery
in his pockets of only two pawn tickets and a few
coppers is strong circumstantial evidence to that
effect. In fact, while the woman’s defence ap-
pears to be an allegation of accidental shooting,
the police case will, no doubt, be based on the
theory that he was not a “ paying guest.”
The Bulletin sincerely regrets to hear of
the death of John Steele Robertson, librarian to
the medical school of Melb. University and an
old contributor to this paper over the pseudonym
of “Steele Grey.” The verses titled “The Path-
way of the Sun,” for example, in 1893 Xmas Bul-
letin, are still cherished by good readers—
They are verses touched with a lofty and yearning
emotion—and the last might be Steele Robertson’s
epitaph.
Do you hear the West a-calling, brother mine?
Do yon hear the West a-calling, sweet and low?
'Tis the voice that ever calls in shade or shine,
In madness or in gladness or in woe.
’Tis the voice that through eternity and time
On the strong soul of a man laysits behest,
And ihe burden of its ever-heating rhyme
Is “ Ever to the Westward lies man’s quest 1 ”
In the West the prize is waiting for the bold ;
In the East the days are'Jlead and life is done;
And the banners of the shy are red and gold,
That beckon to the Pathway of the Sun.
And thus on to —
The knell of all things dead again is knolled
To-day, as it was knolled in days that were,
And the dole that ever was to seekers doled,
Is the dole again of them that do and dare.
We are born of dust that unto dust returned ;
We are seed of ashes that to ashes went.
As the embers old, so embers now are burned ;
As the dust of old, so dust is ever spent.
For aye and ever man his way shall wend
Where gold and glory wait him to be won,
Shall follow still the voice that has no end.
That calls hiin to the Pathway of the Sun.

A certain Australian Governor was recently
entertained at a big dinner by a high official of
his province, and when the host had led the way
to the dining-room with the Governor’s wife,
H.E., instead of taking the hostess, secundum
ordinem, under his own wing, calmly stalked in
solus. And it wasn’t till he noticed that nobody
followed that he sauntered out again with his ser-
viette in his button-hole, and made matters, if
anything, worse with the indignant lady of the
house by coolly explaining that he’d forgotten all
about her!

Yet another Adelaide Hospital doctor has
been dismissed in the person of Dr. Russell,
medical superintendent, the only remaining mem-
ber of the old staff. He was conge’d for refusing
to consult with Dr. Ramsay-Smith, senior physi-
cian. The Adelaide Hospital row started through
someone saying “ haw ” in an insulting manner
during a game of tennis ; and now, in its fourth
year, it is still young and growing.

Under heading “ Medical Ethics,” the
Australasian Medical Gazette reproduces,
with implied disapproval—“A Card. Dr. F.W.B.
presents the compliments of the season to his
clientele, and wishes all a hale, hearty and prosper-
ous New Year.” Well? Nobody will suppose
for a moment that any doctor really wishes his
clientele to become no clientele by getting hale and
hearty.

ridge prison the other day. Mat. Larkin, bright as

Murderer Phillips looks three years younger than

manure. The £130,000 bank-defaulter reminds one

than the lower-class convicts.”

C.R. : “ One of the reasons given by the
managing committee of Melb. Women’s Hospital
for rejecting the applications of lady-doctors for
positions on the resident medical staff was that
women have not sufficient nerve to undertake
serious surgical cases. It is women, too (the
committee is composed almost exclusively of
ladies), who talk this trash about doctors of their
own sex. Nobody supposes that there is amongst
the latter a practitioner with the delicacy and the
A SYDNEY STREET SKETCH.
dexterity of a Fitzgerald or a M'Cormick. But
they will, as regards these qualities, compare
favorably with quite a number of male medieoes.
The writer had occasion, some years ago, to have
an operation performed on his corpus. A doctor
living near by was summoned, and came with
his kit of tools and a chloroformer. The
latter was all right, but the doctor’s hand
was shaking like the head of a toy-man-
darin, though, judging by the odor of his
breath, he had made several valiant attempts to
steady his nerves that morning. The writer, not
wishing to be hackedabout promiscuously,refused
to allow the Unsteady Sawbones to go on with
the operation, and he departed in high dudgeon
with his kit of tools and his chloroformer. Now,
this is a sort of contretemps hardly ever likely to
happen in cases where the sawbones is a lady.
Next time the writer feels in need of a little
probing and slicing, he will call in the nearest
female M.B. to do the trick.

A police escort for Premier Reid ! Why,
when Minister Garrard took a look round the
Broken Hill schools, the other day, to see which
of the barns wanted painting, two troopers
scudded ahead. They don’t do these things in
the city ; the farce is reserved for the country—
where, of course (in the city clerk’s idea), every-
body is a gaping clodhopper, who needs impress-
ing. Therefore, there’s a standing-order to police-
officers to provide escorts to visiting Ministers.
In the not impossible event
of Australian military activity be-
coming necessary, how many N.S.
Wales officers could undergo the
consequent hard routine for more
than a week without getting
knocked-up? When .Col. Hutton
took command of the local troops
he found that many officers de-
voted their forage - allowance to
personal use, hiring chargers from
livery-stables on the occasion of any
official turn-out. The cynical com-
mandant accordingly took keen de-
light in getting his subordinates
within view of a crowd of people
and then ordering a general flight
over the highest fence at hand.
The Colonel himself would glide
over the obstacle wearing the grim-
mest of smiles, and he always rode
straight ahead until a clatter of
accoutrements and the breathless
objurgations of some tumbled war-
rior drew his attention. Then the
Commandant, still smiling sweetly,
would turn round and remon-
strate — “Demn it all, man!
you’re making yourself uttahly
ridiculous. Why the devil don’t
you learn to raide ? ” Consequence
—any mention of the respected dis-
ciplinarian still provokes a scowl
of disapproval from more than one
flabby officer.
Judge Rogers,at Albury (N.S.W.) Quarter
Sessions, said it was a most unfortunate aspect of
social life that so many respectable girls preferred
to stand behind a bar to the less alluring work of
domestic service. Of course Judge Rogers has an
idea why girls prefer the bar to the scullery. The
main reason is that in the bar they serve men,
whereas in the kitchen they serve women. In the
bar, if a girl is smart and popular, she gets per-
haps 30s. a week, to say nothing of occasional
presents from her friends—and even a barmaid
may have sincere friends—whereas in the kitchen
she gets at best 155., and fare which compared to
that of an hotel is niggardly and monotonous. As
a barmaid she gets to know all sorts and conditions
of men, and may (as hundreds of barmaids have
done) possibly secure from among them a good and
gilded husband ; as a mere Mary Ann minion of
“ the missus ” she hasn’t Buckley’s show of marry-
ing anybody much better off than a baker’s driver.
And yet Judge Rogers wonders why any respect-
able girl should want to be a barmaid and wear a
nice dress and talk to Johnnies, while she can get
12s. a week to wallop pots in an obscurity only
relieved by stolen back-gate interviews with
butcher’s delegates.

“ Eye-witness ” to The Bulletin :
Some folks who pride themselves on not taking all that’s
printed for gospel describe the newspaper reports of the
Gippsland fires as grossly exaggerated. I have been in
the midst of one conflagration and rode through another
fire-stricken district immediately afterwards. Of the most
disastrous fires no description could exaggerate the awe
and the horror, the mingled helplessnes and bravery of
the settlers amid the overpowering heat and stupefying
smoke and darkness, and the sickening ruin that has been
left as legacy. Wherein the newspaper accounts are in-
accurate is that the picture they paint is one of unre-
lieved gloom and universal destruction. The wind
chopped and changed so much that now the count y
looks like a chess-board ; and yet the black patch and the
untouched one both go down in the reporter’s notebook
as totally destroyed. The animal destruction is not a
creditable feature, for much of it was unnecessary. To
carelessly allow cattle to be hemmed-in in paddocks, to
leave horses in stables and pigs in sties, worst of all, a dog
on the chain, is either greed or cowardice or both.
* * • *
Why didn’t the Victorian and Tasmanian
and Maoriland parsons pray for rain to extinguish
the bush-fires? In drought-time, when a week or
two or three, or a month or so, doesn’t matter so
very much, they pray elaborately, and if rain
comes within six weeks or five months they claim
it as an answer. In case of a huge, screaming
bu.sh-fire there is no such margin ; if the answer
proposes coming in time to be of any use as an
answer, it is wanted inside 12 hours —a long way
inside often. Quite frequently it is wanted
inside 12 minutes. There is no chance in that
case of claiming as a direct reply to prayer the
casual drop of moisture which comes down half-a-

Suffield is still in his living grave, and the
N.S.W. Justice Department is jumping on him
with all its feet to make him fit that grave.
Suffield is a victim not only of Windeyerism, but
of Government by Contradiction. Both N.S.W.
political gangs, the ins and the outs, are interested
in justifying the actions of their respective Minis-
ters of Justice. A pamphlet fully dealing with
this monstrous wrong has just been published.

At a recent function in one
of the Australias the St. Dingo
Rifles acted as guard of honor to
the local viceroy. When the shivoo
was over and the corps was dis-
missed, the officers started in a
body for the nearest hotel. Follow-
ing them went Private Jones, who
had been severely spoken to in the
course of the morning for his awk-
wardness and other defects. He
seized by the tunic-tail the rear-
most captain, the one who had re-
proached him, and held him firmly
for purposes of remonstrance.
“ Look ’ere,” said the perspiring,
white-faced, infuriated Jones, “wot
the bloomin’ ’ell do yer mean by a-
torkiir to me like yer did just now ?
I ain’t a dorg, an’ I ain’t a-goin’
to stand it. I know I don’t know
my work too well, but I’m bloomin’
well sure I know it a bloomin’
sight better than you know yours ;
an’ I ain’t a bloomin’ dorg, anyhow.”
This torrent of remonstrance was
too much for the captain, who
hastily snatched the tail of his
tunic from Jones, and fled into
the. hotel. A brother officer re-
mained to remonstrate. “Now,
Jones, you ought to know better
than to talk like that. Remember
that you belong to the St. Dingo
Rifles, and the eye of the public is
on you.” “ I don’t care if the jore
of the bloomin’ public was on me,”
retorted Private Jones. “ I ain’t a
dorg, an’ he ain’t a-goin’ to tork to
us like that. I don’t take no sich
tork from nobody, and it ain’t no
use of you a-gassin’. I ain’t a
DORG, and you can tell Captain
Dash ter bile ’is ’ead.” Then
officer No. 2 also fled from the
scene. Good old volunteers ! And
Private Jones still answers the roll-
call of the St. Dingo Rifles.

* * * *
There has been a disturbance in
Melb. about the manner in which occasional
corpses are “ planted ” in the local general
cemetery. In some instances it is found
that fever-victims have been buried under a
thin layer of earth, not more than 2ft, from
the surface, so that a smart shower would
probably wash a man out of his grave, and
float him away in his “ piner.” The Board
of Health has snorted aloud, and the Minis-
terial head has ukased that every corpse
has a legal claim to 3ft. of earth above it,
as a guarantee against a premature resur-
rection. This evil is the result of burying in
layers—a system that robs the man who
never had a foot of land to call his own in
his life of the joys of proprietorship after
death, and forces him to share his little six-
by-two with half-a-dozen strangers on the
co-op. principle. In the days of the “men
who made the country,” land about Mel-
bourne was given away like dirt, and now
the Govt, is stumped for a decent paddock
in which the metropolitan dead may be
turned out to grass.

Libels on Queensland are common enough
and somewhat monotonous, but the following by
James Robb, a Bananaland Scotchman, on ’the
land of his adoption, has a slight flavor of redeem-
ing novelty: “Queenslanders have no ardent
desire to make the acquaintance of the cemetery
—knowing that when they reach their destination,
six feet below the burning surface of Queensland
soil, in two months the large white-ants will have
eaten them up root and stump, coffin included,
leaving’ only the metal plates of their false teeth
tor grave-diggers to pocket as perquisites.” The
Ayrshire Post gives this ingenious item to the
world.

A Maoriland J.P. is in trouble
over a woman and a charge of shoot-
ing, which is a little out of the ordi-
nary rut—the regular J.P. trouble
is over a woman and whisky. The
story goes that the honorary beak
was living with a girl that didn’t
belong to him, and he drove out
with her to the races. Her friends,
who were also at the races, con-
sidered that he was needlessly adver-
tising the family disgrace, and the
mother publicly belted him across the
nose with her umbrella. She then
departed amid the cheers of the
crowd, but presently a married
sister _ came along and gave him
four in the countenance with a
vigorous hand. After this the crowd
chased the J.P. back to his buggy,
and ran after him with mud as he
drove homewards. In the evening
one of the girl’s male relatives called
at the house to remonstrate, and he
and the J.P. filled the passage with
hair and dreadful vocabulary, until
the trouble wound up with some
shooting. The average J.P. being
a. vigorous, full-blooded person whose
vitality is evidenced by the successful
way he shoves his grocery-business a
long, is probably more apt than
other men to get into trouble over a
woman. Anyhow, the J.P.’s sin is
mostly a rich, mellow, full-bodied
sin with a woman in it.

Joking points from the inspired German Emperor.
Ihe latter, when abroad in his yacht, preached
to his crew every Sunday, and subsequently pub-
lished his sermons in book-form under the modest
title, “ The Voice of the Lord Upon the Waters.”

His Excellency the Governor forwarded copies of an
address of a religious character which he delivered on
.'f X car s on hoard the Sunbeam. It was received
with thanks.
It was received with thanks ! ” The writer,
who has heard “ Me Lord ” grope and stumble
wearily through many speeches, can understand
how terrible he would be attacking a defenceless
crew with a sermon. It was recorded that even
the most experienced sailor on the Sunbeam was

trip, but people were artfully led to imagine that it
was the effect of a storm.
* * • «
Gov. Brassey has fairly earned the belt for
vice-regal accidents and casualties. He was quite
a new figurehead when he nearly smashed himself
into pulp through a cycling mishap; a carriage
accident gave him a shaking up and he narrowly
escaped with his life; after which a fall from his
saddle-horse added another lucky let-off to the
list. His latest peril was a combination of possible
watery grave with the danger of getting bashed
between the two vessels, and a further (much fur-
ther) probability of shark-bite. The Bulletin
accepts the assurance of the Melbourne dailies
that his Excellency might have met his death in
Hobson s Bay last week, although the shark
theory seems untenable. No full-grown monster
°f the deep would be likely to bite Gov. Brassey,
though a young beginner might nibble at him.
Correct particulars of Gov. Brassey’s bath
hi Hobson’s Bay did pot appear in either Argus
or Age. Official particulars were printed in the
Age only, and these laid much stress on “ the
considerable swell,” “the swift-running tide,”
‘ the wave-driven launch,” “the swirl of the cur-
rent, and so on, all of which would leave the im-
pression that Hobson’s Bay was as bad as Largs
Bay at its worst, on the afternoon of Foundation
Day. In any case, it’s too much to expect of an
ancient mariner that he should see an A.N.A.
banquet through, put in an hour on the Nelson,
and then tranship himself safely to meet his wife
—all on the one afternoon.

THE FORGIVENESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She stood, the mirror of his mind,
His fevered thought’s embodiment,
Whose radiance made his reason blind
And filled his soul with discontent.
A look of scorn stood on her mouth,
But not for him whose dazzled eyes
Saw but the sadness of the South
Where morning never lives or dies.
“I have forgiven all your sin,
Though with its guilt your soul is crammed.”
He said—“ So ere the day comes in
Come to me though my soul is damned.”
And as he spoke her lofty head
Was lifted in excess of scorn,
While in her eyes a hate long dead
Showed like the dimness of the dawn.
“ So, you forgive me, just as I
Forgave you in my woman’s way—
Your infidelity, your lie,
And all the madness of your day."
Then fiercely shone the eyes of her—
“ I did forgive a man his deed,
But you forgive a woman, sir,
You are no better than your breed.”
W. BRUTON.
THE YARN OF THERO'SUN'S BOOT.
The bo’sun, having- wrung his hands,
A shrilling whistle blew
And piped his nose, as up I goes
To seek an interview.
11 Good bo’sun, of the Sunbeam yacht,
What cheer, yeo-ho ? ” quoth I;
He looks at me, and sez, sez he :
“ Avast, my lad—stand by 1
“ I wish as how upon this barque
My corpse had fallen dead
Afore my foot was doomed to mark
The skipper’s ’oly ’ead.
“ We fell into the foaming deep,
A mem-o-rable flop.
That’s not the worst, for he fell first,
And I come down on top.
“ I’m not a member o’ the Press
As ridicules the Queen,
Yer can’t imagine my distress,
Yer Sydney Bullyteen !
“ I serves the Governor afloat,
I honors him ashore ;
Just take a note that off the boat
I’m loyal to the core.
“ And, consequently, whilst we sank
Beneath the raging brine
I minded that his lordsnip’s rank
Was different to mine.
“ But, all the same, I had to drop,
And, meaning no offence,
I felt my shoes inflict a bruise
Upon his Excellence.
“ The British peer to whom I owe
My duty and regard
Was struggling in the sea below,
And I 'ad 'it ’im ’ard.
“ I saw the bloodstain on his brow
And cried aloud, ‘ O, Lord ! ’
When me and him (we both can’t swim)
Was safely hauled aboard.
“ His Excellence he only laughed
To see the narsty cut
Where I, the bo’sun of the craft,
Had kicked his cokernut.
“Hedoesn’t bear the slightest grudge.
The ’earty ’igh-born gent;
He’s making fun o’ what I done,
But I can’t rest content,
For though I eat, and drink, and smoke
As reg’ler as before,
I know that Heaven damns a bloke
What boots a Governor.
“ And every time he takes command
Of this here yacht at sea,
I’ll lay my neck agin the deck
Until he’s trod on me.”
