A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 1, 1898.
Mt Dear Myee—
Much of the gilt came off the gingerbread when
it was announced that the “diamonds” in the
ring dropped into the plate at the bishop-making
service at St. Andrew’s Cathedral, last week, were
non-existent. It was only “a plain gold ring ”as
per revised version of the incident. Great
Heavens ! can it have been a wedding-ring? The
newest and youngest Australian prelate, Rev.
Stone-Wigg, is the object of much hysterical
ladylike regret that such a clerical darling should
be destined to the dusky heathen in New Guinea.
What a weird taste in nomenclature the forbears
of some Australian bishops must have had !
Stone and wig indissolubly hyphenated combine
as oddly as Field-Flowers-Goe. But irreverent
young Australia doesn’t trouble much about
bishops or their titles. When Dr. Green, of
Grafton and Arinidale, was iti Melbourne recently
he was chief attraction at a churc h tea-party, and
was spoken at by Bishop Goe as “ my brother of
Grafton and Arinidale.” A small boy auditor
nudged his mother and whispered loudly, “ What
sort of a job has he got? ” “ He's not on a job, my
Bon,” replied the mother. “Yes, he is,” objected
sonny, “ 1 heard the preacher call him 1 my brother
who is grafting in Armidale.”’
At one stage in the consecration of Bishop
Stone-Wigg at St. Andrew’s Cathedral, Sydney,
the right rev. gentleman was carted off by a
couple of parsons to the vestry to put on a
“rochet.” Several minutes elapsed, but the partly-
finished bishopand his valets did not appear. Bishop
Goe grew so anxious at the delay that beads of
perspiration oozed from his brow ; but at last out
came the new bishop in a white night-robe to the
accompaniment of much tittering from the female
section of the congregation. I am told that
the delay in the vestry occurred through the valet-
chaplains being at variance over the canonical
method of getting the bishop into the garment;
one man advocated putting the right arm first
whilst the other decided contrariwise.
Anniversary Day regatta was a placid and tame
outing as viewed from the jumbo German boat
used as a flagship. A steady stream of visitors
was pumped into the liner all day per ferry from
the Quay, but its long decks weren't overcrowded
thereby. One of the features of the day was the
feeding. An invitation lunch was given in the
chief saloon, and the paying public were feasted
in other parts of the ship at so much per head,
while onlookers on deck, finding things rather
slow on the water, gazed at the luncheon through
the port-holes. For the first time the regatta was
made a society function, as Mrs. Merged, the
agent’s wife, dispensed afternoon tea to a favored
company of ladies in the saloon. Up to date the
feasting on Anniversary Day has been for men
only. There was some showy dressing on the
flagship. The aquatic girl was in great force. One
boat rowed by four girls in pale-green blouses kept
up a food pace within eye-shot of the flagship all
the afternoon, and the weary oars women were
finally given tow homewards by a friendly
yacht.
Rand wick, as a promenade of fashion at last
week’s meeting both on Wednesday and Saturday,
was quite deserted. The women who did attend
went out strictly with an eye to business, and
kept under the trees, putting their little bit on.
The stand sweep-promoter has had her day at
Randwick ; the plausible women who scattered
their tickets among surrounding strangers, in re-
turn for half-crowns which never returned,
have been absent in large crowds from recent
meetings. The most prevalent type of betting-
woman is the straight-out backer. The most
noticeable gown on Saturday, had there been any-
body there to look at it, was a white brocade with
sky-blue points. White muslin and lace unskil-
fully combined made one wearer look as if she had
step[»ed out in a breakfast-jacket and petticoat.
Govt. House was represented by the newly-arrived
brother, Thomas Brand, a mild-looking young
person in a suit of the fashionable pyjama-like
grey flannel.
A courageous man in the d.c. of a Sydney
theatre, the other night, solved for himself the
evening-dress problem with which the Philhar-
monic fogies are now wrestling, by appearing cool
and comfortable in white duck with tailless coat
and scarlet waist-girdle. This is the correct style
for evening dress in India and up North, and the
bold innovator who introduced it in Sydney was
the envy of all the surrounding males sweltering
in broadcloth. The musical person who wished to
make evening dress in reserved seats at concerts
compulsory was merely trying to take off by a
side-wind the women’s vast, obstructive hats
which conceal the singers. But at the Philhar-
monic sing-song a grateful audience would con-
sider it a boon to be mercifully shut off from some
of the derelicts in the chorus. “ Evening dress”
in Sydney theatres, especially since the.low prices
came in, is a shabby, shoddy farce. The Lyceum
circlers show depressing rows of straw sailor-hats ;
the hall-mark of fashion at the Royal is a gaudy
blouse topping a black skirt; and even the more
fashionable Her Majesty’s only decks itself
showily on Shabbas night.
On Friday at the Royal, when Addie Conyers
benefited, the house had a bright appearance;
the d.c. went into write-chokers and swallow-tails
for the occasion. It was chiefly a masculine
audience, and became delirious with joy over
Addie’s favorite Leicester Square song—one of the
baldest accounts of an every-day ordinary mash
ever set to alleged music, and yet the first bar of
it thrills the gallery to its lowest vest-button. The
principal boy of H. Rickards’ panto, has been one
of the draws of the show : she fas outclassed her
rival boy at the Lyceum because she wears her
tights more gracefully. The whole art of being a
stage boy consists in putting on tights, and Jack
apparently inserts herself into her white wedding-
suit as if it were one vast stocking, and arranges
all the trimming on afterwards.
Introducing his brother, Capt. Brand, to the
dairy-farmers at Kiama agricultural show as an
expert. Gov. Hampden declared that Australian
pastoral statistics staggered 'Ome supporters of
the agricultural interest, and joked about the
way in which Lady H.’s friends had politely ac-
cused her of drawing the long bow over some
surprising facts sent her by the Gov. for diffusion.
When she observed that some Australian farmers
milked IJOO cows per day, her English hearers
had metaphorically remarked “Rats!” Capt.
Brand also arose to say that, as the owner of a
dairy-farm raising 10001 b. of butter weekly, he
thought he knew something about the product,
but felt himself in a very small way of business
compared with the men he was addressing, some
of whom turned out as much per day.
By the death of Madame Juliette Henry,
whose portrait appears elsewhere in this issue,
the French colony in Sydney loses one of
its most prominent and remarkable members.
As the organiser of the French Literary
Circle, Madame Henry was for many years
directress of the only organisation for the ad-
vanced study of French literature in Australia.
Mdlle. Dreyfus, who has made a success of her
French lectures in Melbourne, avowedly obtained
the idea from the elder lady. Mme. Henry was
a tall, handsome and commanding woman with a
manner suggestive of the salons of the last cen-
tury ; her career since she left France was varied,
and should she have left notes for a biography, it
ought to prove interesting reading.
Melbourne Punch’s penlady deplores the fact
that.it is “ bad form” to testimonialise Sassiety
Don Juans, otherwise “so distinguished a mem-
ber of the peerage as the Earl of Shaftesbury
would not have been allowed to leave us so
quietly.” But I understand that his Shaftes-
buryship did get a testimonial —though certainly
not of the sort that any young man would be
likely to run after. ■
Some of the pars, about royal personages in
London papers are interesting as showing in what
infantile prattlmgs it is considered good form to
talk of royalty. For instance, in certain chatter
about the “ poor ” Teck widower, he is patted on
the back by a gossiper in the World (of all
papers !), who observes that “he is in a very bad
way, and has now got a nice clever man as a doc-
tor ’’—and praties of the bereaved person as if he
were an ailing brat of six who must take the nasty
medicine and let the nice kind doctor cure the
naughty tummy-ache. The same scribe records
that the Duchess of Albany had a nasty fall and
scratched her royal nose, but was “ very brave,”
and didn't shirk the arduous labor of opening a
bazaar afterwards, and she didn’t cry, she didn’t,
when the horrid gravel scratched her nosey-posey.
Also, that at a function at Windsor Her Gracious
looked “smiling and gracious,” and the “late
Lord Mayor ” beaming and radiant. It is futile
enough to chronicle royal grins, but imagine the
fatuousness of a public that would be interested in
counting the courtier-like smirkings of an ex-
tremely-commonplace middle-aged man 1
Tailors from the east who goto W.A. goldfields
generally soon retire from the business in disgust.
They can’t make a living in outlandish districts
where the male elite celebrate the coming of
summer by throwing off their coats to transact
business in their shirt-sleeves. The “evening-
dress” worry isn't much of a problem on the
Murchison. For eight months out of the 12 no-
body in that benighted region sees a coat or vest.
But the social reporter has got there all the same,
and glorifies accounts of battery-christenings with
descriptions of Mrs. Expert’s grass-lawn and Miss
Esmeralda’s taffeta glace. And it is an under-
stood thing that all such records are paid for at
per inch.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Lord Brassey first came to Victoria as
Governor with sticking-plaster on his head,
caused by a bump on the Sunbeam, on the way
out. In his cycle-accident and his horse-accident
he bumped his head. In his Hobson’s Bay ad-
venture he has bumped his head again. Fate
knows where Vie can be bumped with least
liability to injury.
“The poor Governor’s friends are hardest on
him. He gets a ducking and bumps his head,
and,- by way of consolation, Capt. Currie, at Vic.
Yacht Club’s smoke-night, says: ‘lf Lord Brassey
had been a prominent cricketer or owner of race-
horses, he would be now the most popular man in
Australia, but ’ (oh, that but!) *he had fostered
the manly sport of yachting.’ Therefore, by
inference, he isn’t popular. True enough, but,
when a man has bumped his head, why bump it
again by throwing at it a hard fact like this?
“ At the Princess’s, during the ‘Virginius’
revival, the dialogue was at times almost drowned
by the subdued snuffling of harrowed spectators.
It’s a terrible pity that real-life mourners cannot
sorrow gracefully after the manner of novelists’
heroines. You know the sort of thing— ‘ Pale yet
beautiful, with pearly drops chasing one another
down marble cheeks!’ But it can’t possibly be
done, so regrets are useless. Even stage emotion,
if at all convincing, smacks more or less of the
ridiculous. For instance, the weep-method of
Maud Jeffries (whose histrionic lot has fallen in
watery ways) bears out the contention that grief
and hay-fever have pretty much the same symp-
toms. Certainly, in extra-tragic moments, both
Miss Jeffries and Lilia M’Carthy (when she got the
chance in the Claudian prologue) discard nasal
effects for a sort of plaintive eerie keening.
“ The lugubrious Roman chronicler who first
wrote up the Virginian legend must have deter-
mined, by hook or crook, to lacerate what Gov.
Brassey calls ‘the tender parts’ (suggestive of
boarding-house pullet) ‘of human character.’
Dramatists Knowles and Barrett have added all
they could to its pile of pristine pathos, with the
result that, if you dodge the dismals at
one corner of the play, they’re sure to overtake
you at the next. Spectators, conscious only of
luke-warm sympathy for betrayed Dentatus,
sneak out their handkerchiefs at the first squawk
of the abducted heroine. Surely no section of
sentimentalists can feel itself uncatered for with
three corpses (assassinated patriot, massacred
maiden and strangled villain) to gloat over, and a
brace of stage-murders and another of funerals to
pick and choose from. Then there are so many
minor miseries and so few relieving touches of
brightness that the final tableau of lunatic father
dying a natural death beside his slaughtered
daughter’s bier is unanimously hailed as an unex-
pectedly cheerful ending.
“ Apart from histrionic horror, * Virginias *
melts audiences to tears by the power of its
associations with ‘old, unhappy, far-off school-
days. impositions, exam.-crams., &o. Oh, those
night-mare Roman history names ! Picture your-
self a trembling examinee again, with three
minutes left to answer a question like this: Give
full name of every Cains in your history period,
with exact dates and minute particulars concern-
ing each.’ , , •

“ One of the pleasantest functions of the past
festive week was the luncheon-party given by the
Yacht Club on board the Nelson on A.N.A. day.
Wednesday was about the only cool day in a week
of scorchers. The scene during the regatta was
brilliant, and a small—a very small—element of
sensation was lent to the whole by llis Ex.’s
plunge into- the lukewarm waters of the hay.
This much-chronicled incident, which resulted in
nothing more serious than a ducking, is regarded
by some as a judgment on the vice-regal one for
his unsportsmanlike treatment of the crew of the
Helen. The yachtsmen who sailed the Helen in
an easy winner, and by so-doing netted for His Ex.
.£5O, received hardly a word of thanks, and the
yacht was as barren of provisions as the ancient
mariner’s boat.
“In Govt. House drawing-room on Saturday
night were gathered together, at Lady Brassey s
invitation, that august body of intellectual woman-
hood known as the Austral Salon. Taken thus
en masse in evening dress the salonites do not
appear at their best. On Saturday night, though
the electric-light was mercifully veiled in gauze
bags and the company was planted in rows,
weirdly-dressed heads surmounted by marvellous
feathers and flowers, and garments betraying
hasty ‘running-up’ at home, proved that the
* woman of mind ’ seldom has any of it to spare
for fashion. There were a few exceptions to
the rale of dowdiness. Lady B. herself looked
entirely elegant in yellow satin and superb black
lace, and a Sydney woman in a gown of
black with elaborate sequin trimming and an
orange-velvet belt, showed up like a peacock
in a fowl-yard. It was a night of heat so
fierce that the vast multitude of addresses,
arranged round the drawing-room walls in two
unlovclv rows, wilted and curled up and fell about
like withered leaves, and the refreshments served
at the end of the programme were the most
popular number on it. His Ex. eclipsed the small
modicum of gaiety going by reading lugubriously
lines addressed by the poet Cowper—pronounced
vice-regally ‘ Cooper ’—to one * Mary ’; also some
dreary verse of . Byron—why does the unlovely,
prosaic millionaire persist in flying to Byron ? A
song from ‘ The Geisha,’ by Mrs. Frank Stephen,
in blended black and green, and a pretty ‘ spoken
piece’ by Mrs. Levy, of Sydney, were the most
admired items of the evening.
“Signor Buzzi, the only professional per-
former, volunteered a solo in view of an unlooked-
for gap in the programme. Mrs. Bingham
warbled tunefully and looked refreshingly cool
in. primrose brocade fichued with white lisse.
Miss Buckley in white satin, with corsage and
sleeve fixings of geranium color, was less sooth-
ing to the eye. An all-black toilette temporarily
added 10 years to Mrs. F'reeman-Thomas’ apparent
age, whiie an equally sombre gown made Miss
Dorothy Brand twice as effective a damsel as the
muslin gowns of her daylight wear. Old ladies in
the audience praised his Ex.’s good sense in giving
his aides plenty to do in opening and shutting
windows in the concert-room instead of keeping
them kicking their heels in idleness all the time.
Gov. Hopetoun, in his day, believed in impressing
his guests by an army of gorgeous flunkeys, but
the Brassey domestic staff apparently runs to
check apron rather than plush. Austral Salonites,
handing over their wraps t’other night to a couple
of any how maids in the vice-regal cloak-room,
plumed themselves complacently as they thought
of the sprucely-uniformed, well-mannered, softly-
spoken abigail in attendance at their own club-
rooms.
“Profiting by the ‘awful example’ of the
Hawkesbury trip, the Vic. Government, deter-
mined that history of that kind should not repeat
itself, hired the huge * Hygeia ’ and invited only a
very small and select number to accompany the
delegates down the bay. Eatables and drinkables
in vast quantities and an exciting sham tight
helped to make one of the worst days of the
season endurable. The trip came to an end at six
o’clock. When the Hygeia reached Port Melb.,
the whole ship's company rushed frantically for
evening papers to see the cricket-score.
“ Melb. girls’ devotion to cricket was clearly
manifested on Saturday afternoon when scores of
them sat parasol-less in the blazing sun and
parching hot wind, to see one hero after another
go down before tho English bowlers. Joan of
Arc’s sufferings at the stake must have been mild
compared with the tortures of those fair girls who
sported light muslin blouses with transparent
sleeves, and whose arms slowly blistered and
seared beneath the scon lung rays in sight of the
multitude. Hut the sufferers seemed to heed it not,
and sat under the scanty awnings through the
terrible afternoon till stumps were drawn. All
the visiting celebrities who were not down the
bay with the Premier were in evidence. G. H.
Reid wasted visibly in the reserve, and Wilson
Barrett in Claudian attitudes viewed the play
with close attention The ladies of his co., too,
turned up in force. Maude Jefferies looked cool
and calm in white with some pink roses in her
hat. Handsome Lilia- M‘Carthy was in black
and heliotrope. After M‘Leod’s brilliant innings
of one run, that hero clad himself in garments of
fashionable cut and strolled elegantly among the
girls, receiving congratulations on all sides on his
marvellous performance. And every maiden who
could get within speaking distance of him sent
gushing messages of love and admiration to Clem.
Hill, who was regarded on the spot as the saviour
of his country.
“The Convention bi-weekly garden-parties will
keep Melbourne humming for weeks, though up
to now they have been only politico-social. The
female tails of Convention comets and the lydies
of local politicians have foregathered ; but the
higher tone has been supplied only by the wives
of official persons. Society, pure and simple, will
come more numerously when it knows that there
are free cakes and ices, not to speak of a brass
band and the pleasure of talking to Speaker Mason
twice a week. Society is never far off the scent
when there is anything free and good going.
“ One of the Brassey aides, notover-p'umpatthe
best of times, is said to have shrunk visibly since
the exposure of his bold, bad brother Bill. But,
according to the law of averages, no family of any
size can expect to dispense with a scapegrace,
and, anyway, the Melb. representative of the cul-
prit’s clan is admirable enough to atone for a host
of fraternal failings. No one, high or low has
aught but good to say of him.
“ Apart from official allowances, the present
Mayor of Melb. can afford to shed more splendor
on bis civic position than most of his predecessors
in office. For one thing, he is strong in the pos-
session of a rich wife, one of a family of gilded
n.ining-eentre girls. Another of these golden
maids married a famous cricketer, while a third
went to law with her trustees, some years back,
because they withheld their consent, and conse-
quent settlements, on the occasion of her marriage.
“ Bendigo, by the way, like Chicago, begins to
have quite a reputation for heiresses. And some-
how the climate up there kills off the old folk
comparatively early, which makes the sailing so
much plainer for the legatees. Comparatively
recently a clergyman thereabouts advertised for
an upper-crust duenna for the orphan heiresses of
a local quartz king. In due course the girls were
domiciled with the near inarriage-eonneetion of a
high and mighty Melb. potentate, and already are
both married to handsome scions of their cha-
perone’s toney clan.
“ Lord Brassey’s naval scheme whereby mer-
chant ships trading to Australia were made train-
ing vessels for midshipmen, promises to create a
nautical disposition among the natives. Three of
Devitt and Moore’s line carry ‘sons of gentle-
men’ who will not live in'the fo'castle or do
menial work, and already several lads have joined
and paid the premiums for a course of instruction
in inidshippery. An up-country lad wrote to the
agents in Melbourne, the. other day, wanting to
know what the wanes were, and if he would be
able to have a ‘fling in London?’ Knight
Wrixon bewails the ambition of town-bred young-
sters to wear a black coat and a pen behind the
ear, rather than go upon the land, whilst up-to-
date baokblockers hanker after a blue jacket and
a life on the rolling deep. Possibly a time is
coming when the girl who wants to * get on the
land ’ will need to marry a Chinaman.
“Piano teacher Guenett, musical critic of the
Argus, ran his bicycle against a cart-wheel, the
other day, and has his left arm broken near the
shoulder. The music-man was one of the nrst m
tlie above office to mount a wheel, which for years
he, with gripped-in trouser-hems and flying coat-
tails, has navigated across Princes Bridge into
town as painfully and nervously and with as
disastrous results as those which ensue when
Brassey navigates his Sunbeam. Now all the
Akg us men, except six-foot Donald M Donald,
have caught the epidemic. Even portly editor
Haddon glides carefully about his front garden
in spare afternoon hours. ” —Yours truly, Ouso.

Justice Mathew Henry Stephen, now in
his 70th year and supposed to have an early retire-
ment under consideration, was the first native
Australian to ascend the Supreme Court Bench.
His father and grandfather were also Supreme
Court Judges. He was born in Hobart, but has
spent almost all his life in Sydney.
Died recently, at Portland (Vic.), William
Carney, who arrived in Tasmania 77 years ago.
Also, at Ballarat, Mrs. Catherine Manning*, who
arrived at Port Phillip the year after Fawkner,
and lived in a tent where Maryborough now
stands. And thirdly, George Glyde, who arrived
m W.A. in the time of the first Governor—lB26
to 1832.
On Monday, 16th Jan., John Davies,
J.P., was elected chairman of petty sessions, at
Essendon (Vic.), for the 30th time in succession.
His predecessor was John Thomas Smith, the
seven times Mayor of Melbourne, and first intro-
ducer of the donkey to Australia. A generation
ago, Mr. Davies represented N. Melb. in the Leg.
Assembly.
Wade, Harris, Murray, and a few other
barristers who would like a few years’ rest on
N.S.Vi7. District Court Bench, were so nearly
even in Ministerial eyes, and were supported by
influences so likely to prove irascible if one of the
named were preferred to the others, that the
canny Reid Cabinet passed over the lot, and
offered the first vacancy to Frank Rogers.
A point in connection with Righteous
Wrixon's jeremiad about the eagerness of the Vic.
State school boys to wear black coats and push a
quill pen for a living, and their utter unwilling-
ness to “ graft ” in a field or workshop, is that
Wrixon himself has never been seen out of a long
black coat by any human eye; in fact, he is
utterly unimaginable without that respectable
garment.
One S.A. pastoralist who, 14 years ago,
wholly held, or had a large interest in, five big
squattages in his own province and the far west of
N.S.W., lately lost the last of them—abandoned
it after a three years’ drought, during which not
12in. of rain in the aggregate were registered.
And he was an oldster.at the game, too—stood, at
one time, on about equal terms with Thomas
Elder and Peter Waite.
It seems definitely settled that J. H. Fin-
layson, editor and part proprietor of S.A. Regis-
ter, stays London as correspondent of that
paper. ‘ Finny” wielded a pen dipped in gall,
and did much to keep the Hospital squabbles alive.
Although a sworn political foe of Kingston’s, he
was one of the latter’s greatest personal friends
until the medical squabbles came along, but they
renewed their friendship when they met in
England at Jubilee time. Finlayson started as
office-boy on the Register, and climbed right ud
the ladder.
Birthdays in February interesting to Aus-
tralians are: Editor Finlayson (S.A.), 55 on the
3rd ; Arthur Garner, 47, and editor Haddon
(Argus), 59, on the Bth ; B. R. Wise on the 10th ;
Judge Molesworth 56 on the 23rd; Inglis Clark
(Tasmania) and Grant Allen, both 50, and Julius
Vogel 63, on the 24th ; and Premier Reid 53 on
the 25th ; General Downes, once Commandant of
S.A. Army and now a pensioner at Geelong, will
lie 64 on Feb. 10.
The news that Thomas M‘llwraith has gone
back to Paris, where he is under the care of high-
class physicians, and that he will shortly return
(in the saloon, presumably) to Queensland, is an
interesting comment, in a small, silent way, on
his alleged “penniless” condition. A penniless
man can’t go wandering expensively about the
Continent of Europe, and get high-class medical
attendance.
The crank who got up the monster Scots-
men’s petition re “ Britons,” etc., was well known
in Melbourne during the Hopetoun regime. He
was chiefly conspicuous for an aggravated Hieland
uniform, and the huge hedge stake with which he
used to pound the pavement at every step.
Nothing short of death would force the writer to
acknowledge himself a fellow - Briton of that
aggressive individual, whose bitterest revilers in
Melbourne were the Scot men themselves.
Dear Bulletin, —Re
the Smith plague. At
Salisbury Downs station ;
10 or 12 men at men’s
hut sitting under veran-
dah. Boss comes and
calls, “Jim !” Eight men
rise and go towards him ;
then he calls “ Jim
Smith ! ” and five out of
the eight continue to ad-

damn it all, I want Jimmy
Smith ! ” And he got him
at last.—O.B.
The appointment of
Rev. Stone-Wigg to be
be Bishop of New Guinea
knocks out the hopes and
endeavors of the ex-
Queenslander, Canon Tay-
lor. Years ago the Canon
(now working in Corn-
wall) tramped from Cook-
town to Cape York, and,
crossing over, looked wist-
fully into the black faces
of the Papuans. He
reckoned upon being made
first bishop of the new
territory, and when Pri-
mate Smith went north a
year or so ago the Canon’s
was the name constantly
associated with the ven-
ture. The Canon’s scheme
was,_ however, expensive.
He insisted on being pro.
yided with a steamer and a permanent staff to
work it, and, besides, he was determined to pick
his clergy from England. Anyhow, his dream is
over, and his numerous and voluminous letters
to everyone in Australia who could further his
ambition will now cease.
Mrs. Crooke, who died the other day in
Melbourne, was a grand-daughter of Thomas
Jamison, M.D., R.N., who was in 1808 appointed
“Surgeon-General of N.S. Wales and His
Majesty’s Settlements of Sydney.” His son, Sir
John Jamison, was one of the surgeons who
attended Nelson when he was fatally wounded.
Sir John owned Regentville, Penrith, most of the
Emu Plains, and after him was named Jamison-
street, Sydney.

An irascible Anglican is exerting himself
to induce the C. of E. Bishop of Colombo to sail
for Sydney with a view to instituting legal pro-
ceedings against Cardinal Moran for alleged
slander and defamation. It is contended that
the Cardinal, in citing the Bishop as a besmircher
of the morality of Anglican missionaries, preju-
dices the Bishop’s position, and damages his influ-
ence with the body over whom he rules.
During his 23 years of office N.S.W.
Government Man-butcher “Nosey Bob ” Howard
has hanged 54 men and one woman. Has made,
say, one hundred yards long of corpses.
A Mount Gambier J.P. (strange as it may
seem) recently bathed in the dusk at Beachport.
When he got to shore he could not find his clothes
and had to make his way home arrayed in sea-
weed.
The retirement of Dr. F. N. Manning
from the position of N.S.W. Inspector-General
of the Insane may not, as alleged, have been
directly due to any action of the Public Service
Board, but the medical profession will neverthe-
less be unable quite to rid itself of the impression
that if the P.S. Board had appr.-ised Dr. Manning
at his proper worth, the country would not now
be losing his valuable services. It may be said
without offence that no doctor in N.S.W. has
ever personally stood so high in the esteem of his
fellows as the retiring Inspector-General.
During the Duke of Edinburgh’s first visit
to Sydney, Lord Newry- now the Karl of Kil-
morey—was one of his suite, and one day my lord
got into a dispute with a cabman—Flash Harry—
concerning a fare. “Look here,” said the latter,
“five bob ain’t much to quarrel about, but I'm
going to have it or take it out of yer hide, me lord ! ”
Newry jumped into the cab. ‘ “Drive into the
Domain to a quiet place,’’said he, and Flash Harry
did so. Half-an-hour afterwards he drove back to
the club with one eye like a tomato, and a lip
looking in at his ear. My lord jumped out.
“Here you are,” said he, and he handed up a
sovereign. Newry was about the best of a very
shady lot.
When M.L. Minister for Lands M’Kenzie
first put up for Parliament he was one night ad-
dressing, an intensely Scotch and somewhat hostile
audience, down Dunedin way. After his speech
an old man arose at the back of the hall, and, with
a “ twang ” strong enough to stack wood, “ put ”
the following : “Jock M‘Kenzie ! D’ye nae think
it presumption for a man o’ your calibre to put up
agin a man o’ the calibre o’ Meestir M‘Lean—
whose shepherd ye hae been?” The Biblical
knowledge of the people before him struck
MTCenzie with lightning quickness and with well-
simulated fervour he answered: “No sir! No!
For I would ask you if the Lord did not choose
David, a shepherd, as King over Israel ? ” On the
wave of that reply he swept in.
Handsome Paris Nesbit, Q.C., of South
Australia, is another example of the divine
saying about the near alliance of great wits
and madness. The last letter the present
writer received .from him recorded an improve-
ment in health. Since then— presumably—the
heat oppressed him and Nesbit has gone down
But only for a while. Some years since he was
confined in Kew (Vic.), and lie devoted his forced
leisure to translating info English verse some of
Goethe’s poems. Not quite the thing you could
expect of a lunatic. One of the most cultivated
professors of Adelaide University said of Nesbit—
and this was a man who had mixed with high
literary society in all parts of the globe—that
he had the keenest and brightest brain of any
man he had met. After a little rest Nesbit will
again be seen in his chambers. There is not a
better man living than Paris Nesbit, and one
wants badly to say this in the hour of his mental
darkness.

Josiah Clifton
who died the other day at Auckland,
JM.Jj., was a man of great force of character
and considerable eloquence. At one time he
was reputedly worth millions, and was a
director of the B.N.Z. But when the boom-
burst came, and the bank had recovered its
overdraft, Josiah was a much-shorn poten-
tate. One story told of him is worth repeat-
ing. He had made a big reputation amongst
the natives as a pacificator, and Chief Wiremu
Tamilian a, on his death-bed, stated that he
wished Birth to receive a present of land at
Matamata. The tribesmen were turbulent
and not too anxious to comply with the request'
but Firth fixed the matter up by erecting a
monolith in memory of the deceased chief
right in the centre of his land-grant, and had
inscribed on the monument in Maori the
words, “ Tamihana’s last words were—Let
Hohaia (Josiah) remain on the land.” Firth
was the author of several books, and a lengthy
conversation which he had with historian Froude
is recorded in “ Oceana.”

“At the concert of the Royal Amateur
Orchestral Society,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, “Prince Albert Edward turned up,
accompanied by the doddering Duke of Cambridge
and Prince Edward of Saxe-Weimar. Wales has
very little hair indeed, except a fringe inside his
collar, and his beard is quite grey. Cambridge is
bulbous, and likewise, only more so, bald. Judg-
ing by appearances, his faithful gamp would be
of more regular service if he swallowed it each
morning, so that it would be in the place where
Cambridge gets wet most frequently.”
In Auckland (M.L.) years ago an estab-
lishment occupied by ladies, single but certainly
not celibate, was locally known as “the Con"
vent. ’ The late Bishop Luck, who, despite an
ascetic face, wore a beard which gave him the
look of a Yankee patent-medicine agent, was mis-
takenly driven there by an unregenerate cabman
whom he told to take him to the Convent. His
lordship, after having in horrified accents re-
counted the occurrence to Charles Otho Montrose,
journalist, concluded by asking pathetically :
“Why do they call that dreadful place the Con-
vent, Mr. Montrose?” “Dunno, my lord,” re-
plied the irrepressible ‘ Monty.’ “ But after what
has happened to your lordship, I shall endeavor
to get it re-christened ‘The Drum Ecclesiastic.’ ”
Thomas Arnold, Mrs. Humphrey Ward’s
father, still lives—-a professor in the R.C. Uni-
versity, Dublin. He was once Inspector of Schools
m Van Diemen’s Land, where he married Julia,
daughter of old Anthony Fenn Kemp, of N.S. W.’
Corps and Rum Traffic celebrity. Mrs. Arnold
inherited the fine old-crusted-port temper of her
papa. When her hubby, for conscientious reasons,
went over to the R.C. faith, she made things very
lively for him : when Arnold was one night wor-
shipping in St. Mary’s R.C. church, Hobart, the
lady stood outside, with a supply of road-metal at
hand, and smashed as many of the windows as
she could. I his was her way of expressing her
disapproval of Mr. Tom Arnold’s apostacy. Mrs.
Humphrey W. is also of a mystic and religious
turn of mind. Another illustration of heredity.

What Graham Berry has to do is to write his autobio-
graphy, run it for a twelvemonth through Age or Argus,
gather it in a book, and make a pot of money.
The kind of birdlime scattered in Duffy’s reminiscences
of Victoria would be peppered all through Berry
who, in addition to the unspeakable advantage of quorum
pars magna fui, has that of being a journalist, though
nothing like so accomplished in that line as Duffy, much
as he surpassed him as a politician. But Duffy was the
journalist throughout, a more unreal Disraeli. Berry
far more of the history-maker than Duffy, whose skill
makes his ant-heap Icok a Himalaya, plunges into the
middle of things at the verv outset of his career
His impressions of Wilson Gray, Higinbotham!
Heales, O Shanassy, and countless other men not
forgetting M'Culloch—why, there would be no read-
ing like it, and everybody is in Majority-land, except
him and Duffy, leaving David Syme to run the race
with these toughs. History of Protection and land
legislation, the Darling grant, by a keen observer, who
happened to be outside the whole affair, the incalculable
advantage of Berry and Service. And, even outside
politics, indeed, Graham could tell of an exciting life
breasting the blows of circumstances, and grappling with
his evil star. “ The Run-down Dingo ! ” P *
Old T. G. Gregson, once a political and
social notable in Tasmania, was one day waiting
for the boat from Kangaroo Point to Hobart. It
was blowing and raining hard, and the roaring fire
in the inn’s coffee-room tempted him to patience
and a glass of hot grog. Here he got talking with
a stranger who proved so intelligent and well-edu-
oated that Gregson, who knew everybody in the
Island of any educational standing, was puzzled.
So, at last, after much punch and many cigars, the
old man took the liberty of asking the 'stranger his
name. The man seemed confused. “You won’t
recognise my name, sir,” said he. “ The fact is I
have been unfortunate, and I have only recently
worked out my sentence. Ah, sir,” he went on
don t shrink from me ! If I have done wrong I
have paid the penalty. Yes, I have paid the
penalty. Gregson rang the bell. “ Paid the
penalty ! ’ he echoed. “ You’d better pay the bill
and go. ” And the poor wretch slunk from the
room. “ Fancy ! ” exclaimed the old man, when
telling the story. “ After capping verse for verse
and line for line, from Horace and Virgil, to find
that my gentlemanly scholar was fresh from the
penitentiary. Faugh! ”

Aboriginalities.
Snell: “ A contribution to the chronicles
of the butting ram. My special ram began life as
a little, white, woolly lamb of unimaginable inno-
cence, beloved by a township, and grew suddenly
into a large, lusty, ravening animal that literally
possessed the place, and butted himself into the
position of a bloated and unquestioned autocrat.
When the spirit moved Jack, he would butt any-
body, from a lady to a red-gum stump, but he
loved best a fat small boy, or a big metal wash-
tub. The latter he would steal from a back yard,
and butt into a mangled ruin, driving it through
Waddy with the rattle and clamor of an old-time
battery. Jack was a terror and a joy to the boys
of Waddy. When there was a means of escape it
was the juvenile custom to bash the riotous
jumbuck on the forehead with clods of dry earth.
When this was repeated two or three times, Jack
went butting mad, and would butt a new track
clean through the township if nothing immovable
intervened. This is how he was cured : One day
the boys found the hub of a cart-wheel, and ran a
stout rope through it, and suspended it from the
top rail of a fence. The bottom rails were missing,
and the hub swung about 18in. from the ground.
Then Jack was inveigled into the trap with green
vegetables, and his enemies worked him up by
bombarding him with clods, and he fell. The ram
went for the swing-hub, and his first drive caught
it “fair,” so that it wliirled clean over the rail,
and took him in the rear with a fearful boost, that
drove him all of a heap. Gan you understand the
surprise and the fury of that ram ? He gathered
himself up again, and went forth to war. Rearing
up he dropped the hub another hot un, and it
came back at him unexpectedly, and knocked his
chin in with an upper-cut. But Jack was game,
and there and then commenced the world’s record
catch-as-catch-ean butting match. The whistle
sounded, and the innocent children were called to
school, when the fight raged most furiously. Two
hours later they rushed again to the battle-ground,
and found Jack spread out, utterly done up. The
hub lay at a distance, the inch rope having worn
through under the ram’s attack. Jack did not
stir from that spot for three days, and then moved
feebly and in great dejection and he could never
be induced to butt anything again even under
pressure of the most grievous abuse.”

True Bill : “Way back in the ’7o’s I was
tramping with a mate from Hill End to Sofala.
Entering the latter town after a long day’s march
through snow and sleet, we made for the first
pub., and, being wet and cold, we called for
brandy—dark brandy. ‘ Roman Catholic or Pro-
testant ’ ? asked the man from Erin ; and, quick
as thought, my mate replied ‘Roman Catholic.’
A bottle of Hennessy’s *** was thereupon un-
corked, and, after having smacked our lips over
the excellence of the ‘craythur,’ I made bold to
ask our host what was the difference between
‘ Roman Catholic ’ and ‘ Protestant ’ brandy ?
‘ One carries the heads, me bhoy,’ he replied, with
a wink, ‘the other doesn’t.’ Thenceforward, we
were devout Catholics.”

The appearance of Prof. Morris’s diction-
ary has suggested the idea of opening an academy
for the study of the Austral language. A Bulle-
tin correspondent has prepared a paper for the
first annual examination :—
EXAMINATION PAPER.
[The candidate must not answer more than six ques-
tions, and the eighth question must on no account be at-
tempted.]
(1) “ Who are you slinging-off at?” Is this phrase good
Austral-English ? What does it mean ?
(2) What difference is there, if any, between the terms
“ a narrow squeak ” and “ a close shave ” ?
(3) Do you consider the opinion of George Bird, ex-J.l.,
regarding the origin of the word “ larrikin a correct one,
or do you prefer the Daltonic theory? Do you consider
Sergeant Dalton a myth, and, if so, how often ?
(4) Prof. Morris says some connect the Australian word
“ gin ” with the Greek for woman ! Do you favor this
view ? If so, how do you account for the fact that we call
gin “Old Tom”?
(5) Is opossum of Keltic origin ? If so, trace its pedigree
to its latest royal ancestor.
(6) Is there any philological connection between
“Baraechi” and “barraoker”? Are the final endings
“ i ” and “ er” merely extinct case endings? -
(7) Australians are said to pronounce the word “ cow ”
as if written “ kee-ow.” Is the Vic. stock-tax in any way
responsible for this ? Give your reasons.
(8) Why did not George Giffen play in the test-matches?
Give his reasons. . .
(9) Prof. Morris has included the “ toe-rag ” in his dic-
tionary. How do you regard this lack of good taste on
the part of a man of delicacy and refinement?
«i * * * *
The shire council, or some other Philis-
tinic, unimaginative power, has perpetrated a
ghastly outrage on the good old mineral spring at
Hepburn, near Daylesford. Bor 30 or 40 years—
in fact, ever since the time when Hepburn got its
first testimonial from the diggers of that mining
district —the local inhabitants and dyspeptic
health-resorters had swilled mineral water at a
place where it gushed invitingly from a hole in
the ironstone rock. But a few weeks ago the fat-
headed authorities aforesaid, being anxious to
boom the sleepy hollow as a Victorian spa, built
a common or garden kind of wall around the rock,
and covered the top with concrete to “keep the
gases from escaping,” since when the main flow
of their pungent Hepburn water is through a
screwless brass tap protruding from the red
brick wall! A new-chum, arriving at the
“ springs ” with a large tumbler and a
simple determination to cleanse his or her in-
terior from all imaginable diseases, loses a lot of
valuable faith at the sight of this arrangement.
The idea that Providence can be dispensing cure
for rheumatism by the medium of a tap is not
easily maintained. Anyway, the oldest inhabitant,
a hard-shell Scotchman, has ceased to be a walk-
ing advt. for the water which built up his iron
constitution, and his beverage nowadays, says the
reckless ancient, is whisky as often as he can geb
it. A charmingly peaceful spot—Hepburn Springs.
The hotel on the high road hasn’t changed hands
for a quarter of a century or so. All things there-
abouts are as they were in the beginning, with the
exception of the brass tap, and the brick wall, and
a noisy, puffing engine-house, where a speculator
fills torpedo-shaped vessels with aerated mineral
water for the Melbourne market at the rate of
five shillings per gallon.

Dear Bulletin, —Here is one
case of Lynch-lawin Australia. We
were about a hundred diggers on
the Cloncurry, in ’7O, and several
Bneak robberies had been com-
mitted ; so one day a man went to
the mouth of every shaft on Shar-
key’s Flat and whispered the magio
words “Roll up.” In those days
we had no shoes, and every man,
bar two, walked about a mile bare
foot to his work and carried his
crib. Assembling accordingly at
“ The Bishop’s ” humpy we found
that “ Bill, the Soldier,” was ac-
cused by his quondam mate Brummy
of stealing some 20oz. gold from his
tent. A jury was appointed, the
accused selecting six and the ac-
cuser six. The trial was short.
Several pieces of gold, sworn to by
prisoner’s mate,.were identified by
the butcher and storekeeper to
whom they had been sold, and “Bill
the Squeezer,” the only other
diner-at-home, had seen Bill emerg-
ing from Brummy’s humpy that
day. “The Soldier” was found
guilty, and, though some proposed
to hang him, and others to cut his
ears off, it was decided to give him
11 lashes with a stockwhip and
hunt him out of camp. Bill took
his gruel well, calmly put on his
shirt again saying: “ What d’ye
think o’ that, boys 1 ” and was
hunted off to Normanton.—M.T.

There was a bit of a dust-
storm out on the Never-Never the
other day, and the Tibooburra paper
prints some things about it. One
man was riding a white horse. He
looked down, but couldn’t see the
animal; after half-an-hour or so he
caught sight of its head. That was
the worst experience. Less-accomplished corre-
spondents, however, agree that, though it was in
the afternoon, the darkness was as intense as in
the blackest night.

Dear Boubti n,- -Travelling
down from Bairnsdale to Cunninghame
(Vic.), the other day, we had on board
a shearer (full of whisky) and a black-
fellow and his gin. The shearer started
to use lurid language, and damned the
boat, crew, and passengers with a great
damn. The blackfellow walked up to
the man of wool, tapped him on the
shoulder, and spake thus: “Sir, it is
most ungentleman to use such prepos-
terous language in the presence of a
lady.” Whereupon the shearer man,
unused to the Lake Tyers mission class
of black, however familiar with the
“gib it tixpence ” or garden-variety,
muttered, “ Well, I’m blowed ! ” and
collapsed utterly.—M.
* * * *
In a one-horse township in Tas.
(writes a Bulletin correspondent) lives
a youth named Quin who can build
anything from a toy windmill to a mail-
coach. When not much more than a
child he made several wind-machines,
wheel-barrows, &c., with no help, no
tuition, and no tools save his pocket-
knife and an axe. Recently I saw a
four-wheeled buggy he had constructed
—iron-work, wheels, tires pole, springs
and body complete, and his tools were
the knife, dull axe, an old pair of tongs,
and a hammer. His parents, despite
many offers of help, refused to appren-
tice him ; and now lie is 17, and wast-
ing his life scrub-cutting for cocky
farmers.
Snake-stories are frequent just now. Melb.
papers tell of a snake entering a bird-cage at
Horsham in quest of a light lunch. Whilst the
proprietor of the cage was looking for a stick, the
snake secured and swallowed the canary, and then
could not escape between the bars of the cage
because of the lump of undigested bird in its
interior. When the present writer first met that
story the bird was a cockatoo. The owner
killed the imprisoned snake, hastily cut it open,
and the bird popped out, ejaculating : “ That was
a hot shop ! ”

“ Dead Finish ” : Jtte “ Summer, A Wail
of the West,” in a recent issue, let me say that
Mr. Luftig’s entomology isn’t as reliable as his
The late Madame JULIETTE HENRY.
Photo., Freeman.
versification. The West is already staggering
under such a multitude of deserved curses, that
perhaps an extra gratuitous one won’t break its
back ; still, in the interests of truth, I should like
to point out that when the grass is “ withered and
yellow and sere ” the insect plague is at a mini-
mum. In fact, if you offered 10s. a head for sand-
flies you couldn’t get one at such a time. They
only appear after rain, when the grass is young
and green, and don’t last more than a week, but
during that week —Holy Moses 1
Dear B., —Be “Wanderer’s” inquiry : the
lunar rainbow is a not-uncommon phenomenon in
high northern latitudes. I have seen it about
half-a-dozen times—on one occasion it was so vivid
as to produce a just-discernible secondary bow.
The appearance is always white.—M.
Miss CLARE MANIFTELD (“Sweet Lavenderat
Sydney Criterion),
Photo., Falk.
Unlike brother Panton, of Melbourne,
Licensing-Magistrate Johnson, of Sydney, is not
inclined to encourage the Australian wine-in-
dustry. At the central quarterly licensing meet-
ing, a few days ago, two applications for new
native wine-licenses came before him. The police
objected on the ground that the applicants sold
fruit and confectionery on the premises specified,
and for that purpose kept open on Sundays. They
explained, however, that in many such cases
licenses had already been granted, but in others
refused, and until the matter was finally settled
they intended to formally object. The bench,
having intimated its determination to grant such
licenses to restaurants only, Mr. Crick, for one of
the applicants, pointed out that there was no force
in the objection lodged since the applicants were
legally entitled to do that to which the police took
exception. If the applicants in any way broke
the law they would be dealt with. As a matter
of fact, these native wine-licenses gave little
trouble, and he could not recall one prosecution
from that source. It was absurd to object to
confectioners and fruiterers and yet grant these
licenses to restaurants which not only kept, open on
Sundays but, in some cases, all night as well.
Such objections were contrary to the spirit of the
Act which was passed to encourage people to
drink Australian wine and give every facility for
its sale. The magistrates refused one application
and the other was withdrawn. Mr. Crick in-
formed beak Johnson that the matter would
crop up again when he would test the validity of
the objection.
The End of the World
May be near or it. may not, but while there are
no reasonable grounds for apprehending the “ end
of the world ” is near, it is liable to eome, prema-
turely, to many who do not listen to nature’s voice
of reasoning and promptly check any approaching
symptoms of liver or kidney disease. How this
can effectually be done may be judged by the fol-
lowing statement, which is one of thousands of a
similiar nature: Shepparton, Vic., Oct. 23rd,
1897 : “ About five years ago I was suffering from
kidney and liver complaint. I had the advice of
three doctors, with no relief. I was advised to
try Warner’s Safe Cure. I did so, and in a very
short time I was completely cured. I have been
in splendid health ever since.
[Mrs.) Annie O’Shea.


THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of o]>ol in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
HEAVY GOLD SHIPMENT.
The incoming English mail steamer brings one
million Guinea Gold Cigarettes shipped by
Thomas Ogden, Liverpool.
Observe, then, what a magnificent surplus The
Mutual could have exhibited to-day, had an
illiberal policy been pursued in relation to the
assured —a surplus exceeding that of any other
office by more than 150 per cent. But, fortunately,
the Company preferred to sacrifice a mere useless
and vainglorious display to the solid interests of
the policy-holders. Who shall say that, in taking
this course, the Mutual Life of'New York has
not chosen the better part? and that, in so doing,
it has not well earned the high reputation which
it has attained in every part of the world as “ the
Policy-holders’ C 0.,” “ the Co. which does the most
good ? ” The intelligent reader will not allow him-
self to be misled by the parading of sums of no
use to the present generation, and in excels of
any possible needs of the generations to con e.*

DICKY’S FIRST SIGHT OF THE SEA.
DICKY (with faint remembrance of Tannleebooya
Creek at flood-time) : “She's pretty 'igh up,
ain’t she, dad? Spot that cocky’s backyard.”
assisted imagination of young Australia warbles seven
times in ten :
In an Australian Glade.
The scene—a wild Australian glade, awaj r on the Mudgee
line,
Surrounded with oak, blue gum, and Australian turpen-
tine ;
A young man kneeling on the ground, his face blanched
white with fear—
As he bent and anxiously gazed at one whose end was
drawing near.
Was it an awful reality, or only a hideous dream—
Had he caused the life blood of his friend to flow in a
crimson stream—
Was it his own careless hand that had laid his comrade
low,
Had he fired the reckless shot that had caused the blood
to flow —
Alas ! it was too true he had shot his old comrade ;
Whilst shooting one early morn in that Australian glade.
And now we’ll print the second verse on its own demerits,
and not because it’s typical
The dawn had broken in the East, a clear bright morn in
May ;
And to him it was to be a well-remembered day.
But the heaviest sorrow that pressed upon his mind—
Was the knowledge his slain was an Atheist and to meet
God unresigned.
And Oh ! what anguish that thought brought, and he
prayed to be forgiven— v
For sending one so unprepared to meet the God of
Heaven :
Oh God ! if by future striving I earn a place with Thee ;
Will Thou let me grant it to my slain in eternity?
All the brightness of the morn seemed turned to evening
shade:
To him who prayed so earnestly in that Australian glade.

“SURPLUS.”
The term “ surplus ” is applied, in life insurance
affairs, to the balance, ascertained periodically,
between the assets and the liabilities. Out of this
balance or surplus, distributions of profits are
made to the policy-holders entitled to participate;
due provision being made in the valuation for the
rights of future participants. The natural ten-
dency of distribution is to diminish the surplus
for the moment; but in the case of a prosperous
company, the decrease is speedily more than
covered by accessions of profit from various
sources. It is clear, however, that the more
liberal the periodical distributions of profits, the
more gradual will be the growth of the surplus.
Conversely, if a life insurance company withho ds
from its policy-holders the full benefit of the pro-
portion of profits immediately available, and
prefers to accumulate in the interest of future
participants, it follows that the surplus will in-
crease more rapidly in proportion. It requires
but a moment’s consideration, however, to per-
ceive that it is easy to keep in hand more than
the future will require under any possible circum-
stances ; and, when that course is followed, in-
justice to present participants is inevitable.
Now, The Mutual Life of New York, while
careful to make abundant reserves for future
bonuses, has always been equally careful to dis-
tribute to its policy-holders the full immediate
benefits which they ai'e entitled to claim. The
Company has never sacrificed the interests of
policy-holders entitled to immediate participation,
to the formation of an imposing but empirical
surplus, never likely to be required even in the
remotest future. The surplus shown by The
Mutual Life at the end of 1890 was .£0,105,444.
It is a steadily increasing surplus, which, need-
less to say, is more than ample for all the
demands for which it has been created.
But, supposing the Company had, throughout
its history, acted on the principle which
has been adopted in, at least, one prominent
instance —of reserving a large proportion of the
surplus at every distribution, and, in addition,
reducing the surrender-values allowed for discon-
tinued policies, what does the reader suppose
would have been the amount of The Mutual’s
surplus in 1896 ? Not six millions, but upwards of
11/f millions sterling ! This is not guesswork. The
figures are based upon the official report issued by
the Insurance Department of the State of New
York, which gives annually the exact record of
every company doing business in that State.
Since its organisation, The Mutual Life has paid
in bonuses and surrender values to its policy-holders
<£44,445,244, which is more than twice the amount
paid for similar items by the next largest company.

MOSTLY MELBOURNE.


JOUR DAILY BRASSEY.
The staircase broke away from the yacht,
and His Excellency disappeared feet first
into the sea, followed by Jessop. Although
His Excellency and Jessop are good swim-
mers, the situation was one of extreme
danger, as in the bustle of the moment no
steps could be taken to keep the two vessels
apart. If they had come together there
was a great risk of jamming either of the
two men between them Fortunately, the
boats remained apart sufficiently long
to allow Jessop to make his reappear-
ance first and His Excellency some 4 V.
few moments after. 'l\ Ajr

CEYLON TEA.
CAPTAIN FERGUSON, P.S. to HIS EXCELLENCY THE
GOVERNOR, writes as follows :
“ I beg to inform you that the sample packet of Nirvana Tea yon were good enough to send
me was prepared for His Excellency, and the Tea seemed to be of excellent quality.
Mr. C. R. BLACKETT, Govt. Analyst of Victoria, says, September 21st, ’97:
“ I have analysed the Nirvana Tea, and am able to affirm that it is of Eirst-class Quality.
Rich in extract, it is of pleasant aroma and flavour, and is absolutely pure. .
Dr. J. F. ELLIOTT (Elliott Bros., Ltd., Sydney), says:—
“ This is to certify that I have examined several Samples of Nirvana Tea taken from stock.
lam satisfied that it is an unusually fine, pure Ceylon Tea, consisting of young leaves It has
a very pleasant aroma, is rich in extractive, and contains little tanm or astringent matter
Owing to its being packed in lead at Colombo, its freshness is preserved, and it is a pleasure to
recommend such a high-class tea.”
CEYLON TEA is put up in Lead in Colombo
in order that it rnay reach the public in the ori-
ginal packets, pure, fresh, untampered with.
On sale by all Grocers, and Wholesale at the
Sydney Agency, 56 Margaret-street, where, by
writing, any person unable to get supplied
locally can obtain a free sample, pamphlet, and
price list. /

E. S. CAPE [Auditors.
The following resolutions were carried unanimously, . , , ■ , , , , . ...
<< That the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and ordered to be circulated among
“That the thanks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General Manager, and Officers of the
Bank for their very satisfactory management of the affairs of the Man r
In moving the adoption of the Report, the Chairman, Mr. Edward Knox, said .
“ The Balance-sheet we to-day have the pleasure to present shows a considerable increase in the business of the
Bank, combined with an exceptionally strong position, and will, I feel sure, be considered by the Shareholders as
h ,'? The feature is the large increase in Government Securities, which at this time last year stood at
£305 000, and now amount to £1,009,000. It is a coincidence that the amount is within a few hundred pounds of the
Reserve Fund, which stands at £1,010,000, but it is a coincidence only, for the Board do not consider it a wise policy
to earmark any of the assets of the Bank as held specially for the purpose of a Reserve rund. ’ .
“ Comparing the present Balance-sheet with that of June last, you will find that our deposits have increased by
£209,000, our note circulation by £76,000, and our bills in circulation by £218,000 ; while, on the other side, our liquid
assets, consisting of coin, cash balances, and Government Securities, have increased from £3,301,000 to <B,OOO.
Our advances and other investments have increased from £8,496,000 to £8,583,000, and the gross total from £12,-61,0 )0
to £l2 765 000, showing increased activity in our business, which on all points has been well maintained. Ins is lie
more satisfactory seeing that the effects of the drought have resulted in shortages of some of the most important
exports of the colony. ~ T . ,
“ The prospect of an abundant harvest, which some months since appeared almost assured, has, L regret to say,
not been realised in some districts, owing to a deficient rainfall. It is, however, estimated that sufficient wheat will
have been harvested to render foreign importations unnecessary for the present year. A deficient wool clip and a re-
duction in our flocks and her Is caused by the drought are also serious drawbacks, but the recuperative powers of the
colony are such that two or three good seasons would overcome these.”

Smoke
Chief
POLITICAL
POINTS
The population of S.A. increased last year
by 3291, and that of Maoriland by 14,894.
New reading—for Australia to-day : Let
me attend the frames of a nation and I care not
who makes its laws.
The eternal J. P. T. Caulfield is announced
as an opponent of Crick for West Macquarie.
Blather versus Brains. ‘
The Bulletin won’t be surprised if, in the
long run, Copeland, and not Slattery, opposes
Reid for King Division.
From straws that are blowing about, it
seems plain that John Norton is out after George
Black’s scalp for Gipps division at N.S. W. general
election.
Old “ Whitehorse ” Stevenson is among
the resurrected N.S.W. politicians. When mem-
ber for the Wollombi, he was popularly supposed
to sleep on the mat of the Roads Department.
Whispered that Pilcher, Q.C., now in
London, has an eye upon N.S.W. Agent-general-
ship. The cold and pallid Pilcher divides his time
between London society and Sydney law courts.
R. D. Meagher; of Dean notoriety, has
thoughts of opposing Protectionist Kelly for “the
Tweed,” next election. He will find the “Kelly
gang ” a hard lot to beat, and will only split the
votes for ah outsider.
Queensland possesses 2600 miles of rail-
way, costing, in round numbers, 18 millions. Thus
she boasts 27ft. of rail to every man, woman and
child within her borders as against 10ft. in N.S.W.
—a rate unparalleled in any other dependency.
Soon after N.S.W. Leg. Assembly meets
some democratic member should move that at
the dissolution all members’ free passes shall go
back to the Railway Commissioners in order that
all candidates may stand on a common footing.
Proposed in Adelaide to legislate in favor
of compulsory service by youths between 14 and
18 years, in military cadet corps, for the double
purpose of giving young fellows something definite
to do, and of building up an effective reserve force.
If reports are true,
the public will hear a
good deal more of the
late Carruthers-Norton
libel action when N.S.
Wales Assembly meets
again.
ANTICIPATED!
"Mother sez could yer lend ’er the baby, coz she
wants ter go out sellin’ matches?”
“ An’ then tell yer mother she’s too late, coz I lent
her to Mrs. Wagg to go out beggin’ with.”
A hint to whom it may concern. No
notoriously remorseless usurer has the remotest
earthly chance of election to N.S.W. Parliament.
Any person of this sort who ventures on to the
hustings may expect to be greeted with dead cats.
It is recorded that when Premier Reid
met at Melbourne the Sydney express-train which
had so many misfortunes en route, he remarked to
Lands Minister Carruthers that there “ must have
been a Jonah on board.” There’s many a true
word, &c.
In the last two years the Westralian
Govt, has sold 2709 lots of “town, suburban, and
rural lands,” the area aggregating 78,121 acres,
while in conditional purchases, homestead leases,
homestead farms, and goldfields selections another
555,414 acres have gone.
At N.S.W. anniversary regatta-luncheon,
Gov. Hampden was mildly utopian over the hope
that there would never be any rivalry between
Britain and Germany but such a commercial emu-
lation as would benefit both. “Rats!” First
act, price-cutting ; second act, throat-cutting.
The Laborists hardly
gained a great viotory at
East Adelaide, one of
their strongest centres.
The Tory nominee,
beaten by 212 votes, was
away in M.L., and did
not even address the
electors. The election,
remarks The Bulletin’s
correspondent, proves
that internal squabbles
have done the Labor
cause much harm during
the last twelve months.
A general - election just
now in S.A. might mean
a Labor disaster.
The Age and its
weekly satellite, the
Leader, continue to nag
at Turner for his eco-
nomy, and urge further
loans from London in
order to encourage in-
vestment, assist trade,
enliven industry, circu-
late money, and ‘ restore
prosperity” generally.
Turner is praised in a
patronising way for' liis
well-meant moderation in
having lived within his
means so far, but he is
given to understand that
“ the time has arrived.”
Victoria, it appears, will
be justified in borrowing
now because she has not
borrowed for a long time,
which goes to show that
there should be a limit to
all things, even well-do-
ing, and that it is not
well to be too righteous
for fear one should get a
moral big head, and be
inordinately perked up.
Evidently it is D. Syme’s
opinion that Victoria
should go a-borrowing in
London in order to show
that she doesn’t want to
appear any better than
her neighbors.
Cabled that Carington defends the Seddon
Govt, legislation from attacks made by ex-Gov.
Onslow. This and similar exertions on “ Bob’s ”
part, and the gossipy prophecies of certain
N.S.W. officials, show that the Mount Rennie
lord still has his eye on the Federal Governor-
Generalship. '
Cabled that the French Premier refuses to
produce the confession of guilt stated to have been
made by Capt. Dreyfus. In this matter, it would
really seem the French authorities are much in
the position occupied by Parkes when, in N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly, he bed about, and refused to pro-
due-, the maniac O’Farrell’s confession.
A Federal Convention poem, published in
Melb. ’Erald, makes a painfully-humorous appeal
to Australian national sentiment:
But are we not one people after all —
Australians—howsoe’er each state we call ?
When New South Wales exclaims : “ Hurrah, M’Leod 1”
Is our Victoria less of Noble proud ?
A while ago N.S.W. Premier Reid said that
the general-election was to be fought on thequestion
of Council-reform. Now he vaguely suggests that
Council-reform and the fiscal question should be
“ removed from the turmoil of a general-election ”
and decided by the Referendum. In that case,
what is the general-election to be fought about ?
The beginning of the end of George Black,
N.S.W. Labor M.P., has apparently arrived.
“ The Workers,” The Bulletin reads, “ say that
it ill accords with democratic equality for Black
to be dashing about with a buggy and horse,
which are kept at Parliament House stables.”
This is printed in a paper run by a man who ap-
parently hopes to jump Black’s seat.
The alleged statement of Labor-member
Hughes that Lyne, when he was a member of the
N.S.W. Govt., did nothing but “ securely line his
own pockets,” is one of the few calumnies aimed
at that luckless politician which won’t hold water.
Lyne’s poverty is, and has long been, so notorious,
that it is tolerably good evidence that he never
made more than a bare living out of politics.
They have a tired, perfunctory way of
doing things at the Federal Convention. For in-
stance, by way of throwing light on the debt
problem, Premier Turner planked down a state-
ment of the amounts owing by the different pro-
vinces at June 1896—19 months ago. Which is
about as useful as if the return were made up to
June 1872, or up to the time of Captain Cook.
The one-time potent cry, “ One man one
billet,” is being got ready for its revenge at the
coming N.S.W. elections. Lists of members who
made the cry a gospel are being prepared, in
which some names will figure as possessors of half-
a-dozen paid billets. The cry in its political sense
may have been silly at the start, but it now
threatens to knock silly most of its old champions.
If “Tom” Slattery really wants to get
back to Parliament why not go back to his old
love ? Kenneth Mackay could be made a field-
marshal, or lent to England in her hour of trouble.
Nagle Ryan is dead, and they no longer serve
rum-and-milk in a bucket at the homestead, but if
Tom went up to Booroowa on St. Patrick’s Day,
and the election was held soon after, he’d go in
flying.
Thirty-three years ago ex-Premier Patrick
Jennings spoke and wrote in favor of a customs
union between the various divisions of Australia.
He then propounded and advocated a plan of
Federation which, called only a customs union,
would have effectually prevented any province
from raising a barrier against the trade or inter-
OUT FIRST BALL.
HE: “In a mixed company you can always tell a married couple, because
the wife is sure to contradict the husband."
HIS WIFE : “ Nonsense, William!”
HE ( triumphantly): “There you are!”
course of the others. Had his advocacy succeeded
the rest of Federation would probably have arrived
long ago.
According to N.S.W. Minister Carruthers,
if the Darling, Lachlan, Murray, &c., are put
under Federal control, the value of land along
them will depreciate 50 per cent. Yet the land
and the water will be there just the same, and the
water will belong to the Government and the land
to the people who make the Government—just as
they do now. What docs the ignorant, parochial
Carruthers suppose will happen to the land and
the water ?
The N.S.W. Premier now explains that he
wants to postpone the Federal vote until all inte-
rest has died out, in order that the people in the
country districts may have time “ to gain a clear
idea of the question from reading and from public
discussion.” Considering that the question has
now been discussed almost; continuously for seven
years, how much clearer an idea does Reid sup-
pose they are likely to get by a few weeks’ more
discussion ?
The sauerkraut must have got into the
noddle of Consul-General Kempermann when, at
the Sydney German national kick-up the other
day, he referred to William the Unspeakable as
having “ established such a flourishing state of
affairs as had not existed in the Empire since the
30 Years’ War.” It’s William who does all the
flourishing, though ; the people do the grunting—
under a burden they’re not likely to carry very
much longer.
Alderman Penny proposes to oppose M‘El-
hone for Fitzroy at N.S.W. general election.
Penny isn’t wanted in Parliament. It is bad
enough that such nonentities should be aldermen.
Alderman, like attorney,-is in N.S.W. a Parlia-
mentary synonym for cheeky commonplace. Why
on earth Penny ever came to be called Penny
while there was such a word in the language as
farthing is a mystery that will only be solved
when Woolloomooloo Bay gives up its dead.
Horace Tozer has been recently doing the
Backblocks and thinking out loud for the public
benefit thusly : “Nearly all the country is occu-
pied by people who live out of Queensland. . . .
In no instance did we find owners of property
living on stations we visited. . . . They were
nearly all residing in N.S.W., Victoria, or South
Australia, while the stations were run by a resi-
dent manager and a few men. ...” Banana-
land is the most absentee-ridden province in
Australasia.
H. : “ Gladstone is copiously ante-obited.
From an Imperial viewpoint, the unity of Greater
Britain has been completely squashed, through
the incompetence of English statesmen. Impos-
sible, at this time of day, to begin work which
ought to have been continuous all through Vic-
toria’s reign. When the boy is grown-up it’s no
good thinking what you ought to have done for
him as a child. Miserable little Englanders, in
the Imperialist view, were Palmerston, Peel,
Cobden, Gladstone.
Noticed by keen-eyed Bananalanders that
Tomjoe Byrnes spent nearly all his recent vaca-
tion spell enjoying the hospitality of the cursed
Sassenach—while ould Ireland barely saw the tip
of his nose. And this is how he orates in Bris-
bane: “ I have stood in old England and taken a
pride in seeing her splendor and wealth —and in
meeting her people and sharing her hospitality.”
No allusion, of course, to the amount of splendor
and wealth coming from the grinding of over-
taxed, rack-rented Ireland.
The proposal of Convention - delegate
Glynn (S. A.) that the Commonwealth might take
over the railways with or without the consent of
the States made old Simon Fraser, of Victoria,
boil over in senile rage. “It would be intoler-
able,” shrieked Simon, “if the internal develop-
ment of a State were submitted to discussion by
the Federal Parliament.” For that matter, it is
quite as intolerable that the internal railway de-
velopment of a county should be submitted to dis-
cussion by the State Parliament, yet that happens
every day.
In 1896 the average number of applica-
tions to Sydney Benevolent Asylum for relief was
1011 weekly, and the Asylum authorities described
it as the worst year they had ever known. In
1897 things had grown so much worse that the
weekly applications averaged 1196, which is very
rough on the jolly-and-fat policy. But the report
mentioned that the second half of ’97, though
worse than any previous year, wasn’t quite so
awful as the first half, and the Sydney Freetrade
papers at once published, in large letters, De-
crease of Poverty.”
Apropos of the Freetrade party s constant
assertion that poverty has diminished in N.S.W.
under the jolly-and-fat policy, The Bulletin
offers the following cheerful figures, which can be
found on page 749 of Coghlan’s “ Wealth and
Progress of N.S.W.” (They only go up to the
end of 1896, unfortunately.) At the end of 1894
there were 6025 paupers dependent on the State in
N.S.W., or 48 out of every 10,000 inhabitants. At
the end of 1896 there were 6998 paupers, or 54 out
of every 10,000 inhabitants-the highest propor-
tion on record. - Good old Jojlyandfatt
Tom Slattery, the Protectionist Sydney
lawyer who proposes to run against Reid at next
election, reckons that Melbourne is a dead cer-
tainty for the Federal capital. If the idea is to
have the capital conveniently accessible to an
enemy, so that he can grab the Treasury and the
military head-quarters and the head offices of the
banks, and chase the Groveriiment into the bush, at
the very beginning of trouble, there are points
about Melbourne. Also, if Slattery is looking for
a flat place in a far corner of Australia, and a
muddy river, and wants to dump the seat of
Government right up against Fitzgibbon’s poetry,
Melbourne is full of points.
“ Softgoods ” Sargood has returned from
abroad, and has favored Melb. papers with his
views of the engineers’' strike. The' strike, he
says, will prove a blessing in disguise :
The effect of it will be, and has been, to induce manu-
facturers to try what they can do with unskilled hands,
and I feel sure the ultimate result will be the wider
adoption of the American system of using machines
supervised by unskilled hands. This must in the long run
tend to materially reduce the cost of production.
This is the boldest and straightest declaration of
the gospel of Fatmania up to date. To “materi-
ally reduce the cost of production ” is the highest
and most glorious endeavor of the Sargoods, whose
idea of the millennium is a state in which the
masses are abolished, and nothing remains but the
capitalist and his self-acting engines—the god and
the machine.
The Political Labor League of N.S.W. has
resolved that the Labor-party shall go to the coun-
try at the general election on four main issues :
The abolition of the Legislative Council, a State
bank, old-age pensions, and local government.
They are all good so far as they go, but they don’t
go anything like far enough. Federation, which,
at the moment, is as big an issue as all four put
together, isn’t even mentioned, yet N.S. W. is des-
perately in need of some party that will nail its
party banner to the mast on this question., And
ipparently even the Labor-party hasn’t the
GRAHAM BERRY (sketched at first day's sitting
of Convention).
courage to demand the most urgent and the most
difficult local reform of all—the abolition of bor-
rowing, so that N.S.W. shall live honestly on its
own income instead of being a 3-per-cent, Lazarus
begging at the back-door of Bull. Certainly
neither of the other parties seems at all likely to
advocate it either.

GREAT EXCITEMENT
IN MELBOURNE.
, Merit, like murder, always “ will out.” Mere
assertion never proves anything. Only practical
demonstration of claims to distinction can con-
vince the public mind. The brain of man is the
harbinger of doubt. It accepts nothing that can-
not and is not supported by proof—proof founded
on truth, walled about, domed and turreted with
facts. Facts are the offsprings of development
and development is the material and legitimate
progeny of doubt. Doubts lead to investigation
investigation to discovery, and discovery en-
lightens the mind, expands the intellect, and
brings man into closer affinity with nature.
Ihings that are commonplace enough to us at the
present age would have, less than a century ago,
subjected the projector or discoverer to imprison-
ment and torture, and perhaps death. The dis-
coverer of the earth’s rotundity and the propa-
gators of the fact groaned and suffered beneath
the rack of ignorance and superstition, and were
put to cruel and ignominious deaths. When
Herschell first swept the horoscope of Heaven
with his wonderful telescope, and proclaimed the
existence of the stellar world, the million of glit-
tering spheres whirling in space, compared with
some of which our earth is but a tiny speck, an
avalanche of malice and manevolence was hurled
against his devoted head. So on down through
the whole realm of natural science, until within a
few decades, every new discovery, every fresh
revelation of fixed natural law, was met with a
tidal wave of credulity and doubt. That word
impossible” has stood a colossal barrier on the
highway of ■“ Acceptance and Belief.” It took
ages to beat it down, but thanks to modern ed nea-
t’ ol1 * which has ungyved the hand and unfettered
the brain of man, that monarch of nature, and
permits him to go on untrammeled, solving her
great problems and interpreting her wondrous
laws.
To-day there is passing in review before our
eyes such a panorama of recent discoveries in the
world of natural phenomena and other branches
of science that we stand convulsed and amazed,
but it must be patent to all who have given the
subject attention that the advancement made in
the medical field eclipses all others, and towers
like a white shaft to the clouds above and beyond
conception.
The Polypathic system in the treatment of
chronic disease is undoubtedly the most advanced
of all medical science, and if one were to judge bj
the the vast throng that may be seen daily at their
offices in their magnificent institute, 84 Collins-
street, Melbourne, it would- seem that half the
population of the city were clamoring for their
services.
The success attending this new system is almost
miraculous, but. it .is’in accordance with natural
and applied science, and wins its constant vic-
tories over disease because of the true following
out of that which natural ability and the highest
developments of Medical Science has shown to be
sure and true. All sufferers are welcome to the
of the POLYPATHIC

The N.S.W. volunteer forces (writes a
Bulletin correspondent) have been asking for
leave to pitch their tents at the Easter encamp-
ment a couple of miles distant from those of the
Permanent and partially-paid forces. These latter
and especially the Eancers and Mounted Rifles*
are noted—continues this libellous scribe—for the
open manner in which they annex any of the
gaudy accoutrements of the volunteer to which
they take a fancy, and experience teaches the
aforesaid volunteer that there is safety only in
Canon Pennefather, an Anglican dignitary
of Portsmouth, Eng., has created a big row by pro-
hibiting the marriage of a divorced man. The
mandate did not issue until the wedding-party
turned up at the church, and there was a tremen-
dous scene. The bridegroom now threatens the
Canon with the law.
The present summer,is the third consecu-
tive summer of intense drought in Victoria, and,
with the Gippsland bush-fires thrown in, it is
reckoned' the. most disastrous season for agricul-
turists and pastoralists in the last 35 years.

” If so, the chances are that the easy-going
. thoroughly enjoyed the novelty of the
situation.
