SPORTING NOTIONS.
The physical strength of Clem Hill was very
apparent in his hard hitting and fast running last Satur-
day. after he had put in fonr-and-a-half hours at
the wickets with the temperature at from 100 to 107 in
the shade. And the Englishmen—they also did a lot of
good hard work under difficulties.
* * • *
The behaviour of the “shilling part” of young
Australia during the first day of the Melb. test match was
worth remembering. Up to lunch-time the youthful
observers sat silent and depressed. The luncheon interval
gave them an opportunity to cultivate a “ nasty ” feeling
on the subject of international cricket, hue they still
hoped for an improvement in Australia’s prospects.
When play was resumed, another wicket fell (six for 58),
and Trott’s dismissal took all the heart out of them pro.
tern. For the next half-hour they jeered considerably at
the game in general, and the English side of it. in par-
ticular. After the 100 went up they continued to pass
remarks and make “merry” over incidents. The score
got to 200, and the crowd got its tail up again. Great
enthusiasm at the beating of the English bowling by Hill
and Trumble. Finally, the crowd went away, chuckling
that “ Australia hadn’t done so blanky had after all.”
When the Australians at Adelaide sent six
Englishmen back to the pavilion with 17 runs to their six
names it. was the bowling that did it. Not had luck, not
over anxiety on the part of men fighting against ti emend-
ous odds, but simply the wonderful Australian bowling
that did it. When six Australians, batting with the luck
of the toss in their favor for the third time in succession,
were put away for 24 runs, it was the “ unduly-watered ”
wicket, or an inexplicable “ rot,” or “one of those things
there is no accounting for in fact, anything but the
bowling and fielding that did it. The Australians onlv
show their true form when the Britishers are getting
miserably beaten. If they happen to get out before
they can get “set,” as frequently happens to the
visitors, they don’t show their true form. Hill’s
magnificent score was true forui—just what might have
been expected. A good performance hy any visitor who
has been catting much below his English average is
treated as a great surprise.
Does it ever occur to local cricket writers that
no Australian batsman has finished a season in England
with a remarkable average? The English top-notohers,
on their native heath, invariably cut bigger figures than
the best of visiting Australians, yet a Maclaren at, ’Ome
doesn’t equal his doings abroad. The Englishmen’s
averages in Australia are expected to go higher than they
go in England, likewise with Our Boys. Five of the
previous English visiting team—Brown, Ward, Stoddart,
Maclaren and Peel—played up to this expectation
in the test-matches, and thereby won the ruhber
for their side. Only one man, Brockwell, was a
pronounced failure, judged by his English average.
This time there have been only two reasonable or notable
successes, Maclaren and Ranji, whilst Druce, Wainwright,
Stoddart, Hearne, Mason and Briggs have been failures
in eieven-a-side matches. Six of the Englishmen have
batted below their “ form,” in fact, and four of these
being new-chums it looks as though Stoddart, with all
his experience, hasn’t arrived at an understanding of the
difference between cricket conditions in the two coun-
tries. He can’t pick batsmen who will be certain to take
kindly to the hard Australian wickets.
* * • #
If the Australians are true “ sports ” they
ought to bring a national libel-action against their daily
press. Melb. ’Erald, of Saturday, expressed local cricket,
feeling as follows: “Heavy hanks of thundery-looking
clouds were rolling up from the south-west, and the
Au tralians were praying for night first, and rain after-
wards—that is, if they could manage to make a decent
stand during the afternoon.” Also, the ’Erald, with
charming candor, said : " No doubt the news of the
downfall of the Australian batsmen interfered to some
extent with the attendance in the afternoon.”
» • • •
When the Englishmen only put up 174 against
the Australians’ 323, and had to follow on, it was sweet to
think of the depression of the alleged lion at the other end
of the cable. There are a good many Australians who
want test-matches won \hiefly in order to spoil J Bull’s
insufferable boast, that at cricket and everything else he
can whop creation. And when the last of the English
trickled out on Monday there were pleasant hopes that
the eternal British braggart would be taken down.
The English second innings was a better
attempt—with every Stoddartian man-jack doing all he
knew to avert disaster ; and when The Bulletin stopped
for this week, the score stood at five for 174. The con-
sistent Maclaren was out for 49 ; the tolerably consistent
Ranji was ditto for 55 ; and the English idea that the
match might be pulled out of the fire looked as yellow as
a seasick ghost in the last stage of jaundice.
• » * •
An Australian medical in London writes to
The Bulletin : “Tripe without onions is the stuff served
up by the local pressmen—excepting Daily Mail George
Bull,' late of Sydney Star— re the cricket matches, and
the blowing after the first test would turn all the meat in
a brine barrel. For goodness sake hire two Queensland
mosquitos to sting Ranji moribund, give Storer the sandy
blight, and have Maclaren kidnapped hy a tribe of
bunyips, or kill a dozen sheep and inject the liquefied
pluck into our batsmen and howlers. The stay-at-home
Britisher ignores defeat but remembers victory, and
Australia will never get another loan if the increasing
contempt for its people is not nipped here. What are the
Sydney trams, Melbourne Yarra. and Adelaide churches
for if not to get rid of obstructionists? But Stoddart’s
team in a crushing battery, feed 'em on poisoned rabbits,
or dress ’em in Geelong tweed, hut somehow and anyhow
win a match.” Since the above was written it has been;
done —two times at least.
• • * • •
Ranjitsinhji owed his batting failure in tbe first
innings to his inordinate tendency to chatter. “ There is.
one Australian bowler troubles me more than all the
others, and that’s Trutnble,” said Kumar Shri in conver-
sation. So when the dusky man walked jauntily out
Trott whipped off Jones and put on Trutnble. The oracle
worked, and says he can never sufficiently thank Ranji.
for the valuable tip.
> • • •
The question is how many runs would Maclaren,
Ranjitsinhji, and Drime he likely to make against the
English bowling on hard Australian wickets ?
* • • *
Ranjitsinhji, complaining of the barracking of
the crowd in Adelaide, mentioned that he had postponed
an operation to play in the match. What a difference
between Ranji and Stoddart. When surrounded by a
mob of pressmen, in Adelaide, Ranji talked b.v the yard,
but Stoddart simply said, “ I’m very disappointed, but I
wish to sa.v t*>at I have never played on a better wicket
in Australia.”
• * • •
Ranjitsinjhi, writing on one of his innings in
Sydney, affected modesty by saying “ 1 contributed a
useful 112.” What adjective does Ranji modestly use to
define his six in the Adelaide test-match? Probably before
R. gets that desired word out, anoi her finger will be dis-
located.
» * • *
Ranjitsinhji, interviewed, picks Darling as the
finest batsman in Australia, and much ahead of Hill. But
what is the “finest” batsman—the largest scorer at the
end of the season, or the fastest scorer, or the one who
makes a good average in the most attractive style? And
what is the most attractive style ?
• • • *
The Englishmen began to cry out for Bobby
Peel in Adelaide. “If we only had Bobby here!” they
ejaculated, when Australia had 3 3 up for two wickets on
the first day. “ Well, I guess you’ve got time to cable
and have him here for the tail-end !” interjected an Aus-
tralian barracker. There is no doubt the English bowling
is very plain. Hearne is the only man who can keep the
runs down.

Giffen, it would seem, fell in badly in attempt-
ing to coerce the Australian cricket authorities. Darling,
Hill, and others will each receive a much bigger total than
Giffen s required £l5O. They, in fact, receive all travel-
ling exes., 12s. a day for hotel bill, and £5 per week all
through the season.

F. S. Pictet, of the Tas. v. Vic. cricket-team,
who got an arm-fracture through slipping in the recent
match, is now fully convinced of the folly of wearing
smooth-soled tennis-shoes instead of spiked cricket boots.

Quite a number of people were actually glad on
Saturday week when Alick Bannerman— that is to say,
Alick M‘Kenzie—was tub out after making six runs (in-
cluding a fourer) in 36 minutes. He ought to take ad-
vantage of his height. If Sid. Gregory were as tall as
M’Kenzie he would make about 250 runs an hour.
* • * *
“ Why call Stoddart’s team of cricketers the
‘ English ’ Eleven ? ” somebody asks. They are so-called,
The Bulletin supposes, because all of them, including
Ranji, are taken from English county teams. The same
rule is followed in Australia, Percy M’Donnell, in his
time, was a Victorian, a Waler, and a Queenslander by
turns.

Briggs taught Maclaren the game of cricket.
Perhaps Mac. still clings to the old belief that little Johnny
is a wonderful bowler on any sort of pitch.
* * * *
Harkness, the present Mayor of Bendigo (Vie.)
a Cornishman and a big iron-founder at Golden .Square, is
running the civio show on temperance principles, as of
yore. The gloom of his festive occasions suggests that
his name ought to be Darkness. T’other day, when
Stoddart, Ranji, Richardson & Co. were victims to the
Mayor’s hospitality, a few remarks concerning the in-
human cruelty of soft tack for exhausted cricketers were
exchanged across the table bv at least two of the noil-
teetotal visitors. At the finish Maclaren, Druce and
several others adjourned to the corner pub. and drank to
the speedy reformation of his worship in whisky and soda.

was reached by th* Sydney Cricket Ground people on the

• * * •
The Glebe barrackers had a terrible time on
Saturday last, when Mackenzie and Truniper, playing for
Paddington against Glebe, made 147 and 120 respectively,
both not out.
• • * •
The crowd is not a bad judge of cricket, and its
howling at Tom Garrett simply means that it considers
the veteran lags superfluous. Garrett’s judgment as a
captain is getting to be on a par with his fielding, arid,
taking one consideration with another, it looks as if he
should proceed to give the young men a show. He has
resigned from the selection committee of his province.
A sensible decision, if he means to continue in active ser-
vice.
* * * *
Cricketer Ernest Jones umpired in Melbourne
lately for an interprovineial drummers’ match. The
mercury was 107° in the shade, and Jonah made him-
self immensely popular by stopping the game every 15
minutes for drinks !
* • * *
Dear Bulletin, —Will your scribe who com-
ments on the “ awful importance” of a successful bats-
man returning to the pavilion chokefull of vanity, tell us
how a man who has scored 100 or over should behave in
the face of an admiring multitude? Does he know what
it feels like to have to walk a hundred yards under the
eyes of 30,000 people, and to the accompaniment of
thunders of applause? Under such circs, the more
modest a man really is the more theatrical he is likely to
appear. The party who conies through placidly and with
dignity is almost certain to be a very berg of cold con-
ceit. It takes a tremendous amount of egotism to make
a man appear “ natural ” in the face of acknowledged
success.—Yours, S.
• • * •
If some of M Kibbin’s slow bowls are not
throws, what is throwing? The swarthy N.S. Waler
appears to get his “ questionable deliveries ” winked at
for the simple reason that- they are “ tripe” in compari-
son with his really over-arm work.
• * * «
Mr. Hordern’s English 22-footer importation,
Bronzewing V, was tried in Sydney Harbor on Anniver-
sary Day, and again on Saturday last. Her suit of sails;
is a picture—nothing like it has ever shown before in Port
Jackson, but, as a racing boat, she is completely out-
classed by the local article. There is something wrong
with her bow, as she throws the water up like a mud-
punt ; and if she may he taken as a fair specimen of the
'22-footer from the other side, Mr. Hordern may be
thanked for the opportunity given Australia of proving
its superiority in live-ballast boat-sailing. Mr. Hordern
has now given Joe Donnelly an order for a 22-footer, a
big contingency to he added to the price if she downs the
champion Effie ; which looks much as if he is already
■quite satisfied about the Britisher's chances.

What a strange whim of Mr. Sam. Hordern to
name all his boats the Bronzewing! His latest British
importation is called Bronzewing V.
• • * «
At the recent N.S.W. Rowing Association
meeting a proposal was brought forward to send a N.S.W.
eight-oar crew to England, the mover speaking in suc'i a
confident tone that it looked much as if he had the matter
of expense up his sleeve. There were many speakers
•completely carried away in favor of the notion until Mr.
Horniman brought them to earth again by inquiring,
“ Whv send our crew to the other side of the world for a
licking, when they could get it just as easily across the
Victorian border ?” This was a poser, and the meeting
decided to refer the mat ter to a committee, with the hope
of fixing up with ihe other provinces an amateur definition
which would hold water at Henley.

chief for next Melb. Cup ; or she may figure as a heroine
at the V.R.C. autumn meeting.
» * » *
Old Dondi rather astonished his people, the
other Saturday, by winning a two-mile steeplechase at
Caulfield in Sn'iin. 59sec. The veteran hadn’t won a stake
since his “top-weight” days-not for three and a half
years, in fact—but with the comparative luxury of list,
on his back he took the lead when called upon and
romped home. Whernside in the same race at 12st. 101 b.
ran like a non-stayer at the finish, and perhaps he lacks
stamina, as a weight-carrier, big as he is. The Millers
have never seemed to reckon Whernside a stout horse.

N.S.W. looks like playing a very good hand in

* » * *
With high-handed authority the Executive at
Randwick last week again dropped’down on those eccen-
tric individuals known in racing vernacular as “ lick-
tackers.” Doubtless these gentry do upset wily owners’
calculations, but there is nothing illegal in their actions,
whereas all the ready-money betting that goes on in the
Paddock is distinctly illegal, lkey Mo and co., however,
bring in a good revenue to the A.J.C., while the
“tacker” only weighs in his modest ten bob—hence the
different tactics adopted.
9 * * *
Premier Kingston missed a chance last session,
when his Gaming Act went through, to appropriate, say,
5 per cent, of official totalisator receipts for the public
revenue. S.A. racing clubs now enjoy a complete
monopoly, and the new act will increase the year’s tote
receipts for S.A. by at least t'lo,ooo. A 5 per cent, tax
on gross takings would net several thousands a year to
the treasury. Already Premier Turner pricks his ears—
as witness his Charities Bill of last year.
* • * »
S.A. Govt, makes a final stab at local book-
makers by framing regulations making it a condition of
racing-club licenses that tote-agents shall be excluded
from racecourses. Some of the hookies threaten law if
ejected, and it is certainly doubtful if the regulations are
not ultra vires.

“Daimio after his miserable failure in the
Southern Counties Handicap Steeple at Lingfield is,”
writes The Bulletins London correspond, nt, “looked
upon as a rank dead ’un by the critics. He debuted on
the same course ; but when stable-backed at 4 to 6,
carrying 12st. 71b. in a field with four third-raters, he
was fourth to a bad third, the winner, Rathcarne,
list. 101 b., 4 to 1, twelve lengths ahead of second. Daimio
fenced beautifully, and in a thoroughly English mannah,
hut hadn’t enough speed to straighten his tail. Halfway
he led, but never saw the remaining twelve furlongs,
though a selling-plater finished behind him. Escott was
up, and it looked a genuine trial.”
* • • »
In again losing the A.N. A. Wheel Race—if he
did lose it by a hair’s-breadth \V. J. C. Elliott struck his
usual ill-luck on the track. When he went out for the
final he had backed himself for £BOO, his firm had laid
him £lOO to 0, and the first prize was £220 —£1120 in all.
Yet, after riding a singularly good race, and winning, in
the opinion of such judges as Fletcher, Barden, Ken
Lewis, Jim Carpenter, J. Williams (the N.S.W. handicap-
per), and Lenne (the Vic. handicappeij, the decision went
to L. M. Jackson. Jackson hung on to Elliott’s wheel the
whole last lap, and it was therefore dollars to doughi uts
that Jackson would win in the finish of the sprint—
so great is the advantage to the man who hangs second.
The bookmakers were festive over the decision. One who
would have been hard hit called upon Elliott afterwards
and thanked him for riding a black machine. Said the
bookie: “Jackson’s wheel caught the Judge’s eye first,
not because it was in front but because it is a white one;
yours is black.” Elliott, by the way, being 32 years old,
is one of the best advertisements going in favor of the
wheel. Certainly no youngster could look fresher than
he did the dav of the race.
* * * •
Fifty per cent, of the crowd that saw the finish
for the A. N. A. Wheel Race thought Elliott had won by
six inches at least. Elliott thought likewise, seemingly.
The most surprised man at the A.N.A. meeting
was Porta when he rode around the track after winning
the First Class Handicap, and didn’t get' the ghost of an
ovation.
L. M. Jackson narrowly escaped trouble in the
third semi-final by rushing to the front when the bell
rang. A couple of seconds later one of the men imme-
diately behind him came violently to grief and brought
seven others down with him. Only the four placed men
stood tip, and Jackson followed up his luck—which cer-
tainly uas luck, rather than judgment—by winning the
coveted £2lO in the final.
• * * »
The March meeting of N.S.W. League of Wheel-
men is to be held on the Agricultural Ground, the meet-
ings on the Cricket Ground having proved a loss to the
League, owinsf to the heavy charges of the Ground people.
The Cricket Ground loses its rival as regards surround-
ings, but, so far as the track proper is concerned for
bicycles, the greater width of the Agricultural Ground
makes it better for big handicaps. For multicycles, the
Cricket Ground s higher banking makes it more suitable,
but paced races and record-breaking are not loved by the
crowd. What it likes to see is a ding-dong finish in the
straight. Record-breaking can be done just as well on a
quiet afternoon, in the presence of the officials and the
representatives of the machine being advertised.
» • • «
Bilmartin’s departure for ’Murka makes a cer-
tain perjury case, pending in Adelaide this month, very
interesting. Bill, when in Adelaide, was paragraphed in
the Advertiser as one of the participants in a prize-
tight, in which William was alleged to have been badly
knocked out. Martin went for the ’Tiser for libel
damages and got a verdict, the chief witness against him
being committed for trial for perjury. With the Plugger
away, the case probably collapses.
» * • *
The close of the New York six-days’-cycle-race
was a sickening spectacle—some of the riders grinning
and muttering in sheer temporary insanity, some falling
from their machines asleep, and being dragged off the
track for a short rest, and Miller—the eventual winner—
was perfectly unable to stand, though still able to go
pretty strong when lifted on to his Wheel agaiu. His
distance was over 2088 miles.

World, which used to contain 120 pages, and more than
two-thirds of those advts., is now cut down to 24 pages.

At the inquest upon the boxer Croot, killed re-
cently at London National Sporting Club, Sir George
Chetwynd testified that, “except for science,” the fatal
set-to was “ most disappointing.” And yet the fashion-
able ring-patrons keep up the pretence that it’s science
they want, and not gore.

At Hamilton, N.S.W., some 2000 people wit-.,
nessed the match between Virgo and W. Oughton for the
Northern District championship of that much-neglected
game, handball. The game was splendidly contested.
Virgo winning by two points. The stakes were £4O, and
a lot of money changed hands over the event.
• * * •
A foolish Austrian, one Sigmund Bachmann, is
just now travelling from Sydney to Adelaide, or vice
versa, in pursuance of a trip “round the world’’for a
wager. He had backed himself not to speak a word of
English during his journey, and to he back at his starting
point within two years of his leaving it. The Bulletin
feels sorry for Sigmund. These apparently idiotic wagers
are seldom as stupid as they 100k —not on the layer’s side
of the wager. Whil.-t Sigmund is pushing his hike in
foreign parts, the other fellow is probably in pursuit of
Siggy’s girl, or working some equally attractive game.
Beware of the man who taunts you into stopping away
for two years.

Melb. Age has some fine points after all.
On the dispute occurring re the sailing of the
Bulimba, both Age and Argus pointed out that
permission had been duly obtained for the vessel
to sail on the Sunday ; but the Age sent, a re-
porter to interview the men, and from them jt was
learnt that the “permission for Sunday-sailing”
is getting quite a common occurrence, the Bulimba
having worked the game four times in Melbourne
and twice in Sydney in three months. But the
Argus had no word of all this.
“ The indifferent harvest in the Riverine,
N.S.W. district, has considerably raised the price
of chaff, corn and hay,” writes 6 x 8 to The Bulle-
tin. “Many carriers can’t afford to feed their
horses ; and a piteous spectacle just now is a team
of horses turned out on scanty grass for the first
time after a hard day’s work. With heads raised,
an anxious look in their faces, and ears pricked
attentively, the poor animals stand in a heap all
night near the waggon—waiting vainly for the
accustomed ‘ cub-cub ’ invitation to the feeding-
boxes. ”
HOW YOUNG AUSTRALIA FEELS ABOUT IT.
Oh, list to the lay of Australia,
Oh, list to her triumphing trill,
Concerning the way that her failure
Was stopped hy the genius of Hill—
Clem. Hill!
Here’s a hip ! hip ! hurrah ! for your skill.
The trustworthy Trott with his penny
For the option with Stoddart had vied,
And, not for the first time by many,
Had made the way clear for his side.
For the first of the fun they were let in,
But, oh ! for the chance that they got;
Things very soon showed there had set in
A most undeniable “ rot.”
Oh, sad was the tale of that scoring :
Soon after the play had begun
Two batsmen were duck-eggs deploring,
M’Leod was no louder than 1.
Young Gregory, chancing to miss it,
V\ as bowled hy a yorker for nought,
And Iredale, brief in his visit,
By Storer was prettily caught.
My brothers, Australia was crumpling
Like leaves in the breath of a flame,
’Twas all Broken Hill to a dumplirur
That England would crush us with shame.
But Hill faced the task that they set him,
And, proof against every shot,
With Trumble to aid and abet him,
Soon stopped that unfortunate “ rot.”
Some folks may have built up our nation ;
What matter! at cricket they're nil.
But Australia knows, with elation,
’Twas saved by a party named Hill—
Clem Hill !
Here’s a hip! hip! hurrah! for your skill.
ZEF.

“In N.S.W. Riverina district drought is
still supreme,’ writes a Bulletin correspondent.
Away from the all-butdry Murrumbidgee
water has grown terribly scarce. Not eight miles
from the river a shilling was paid recently for a
bucket-full ! In the (long-dry) Old Man Creek
country aid is denied from below as well as above.
Many settlers there are boring and sinking wells,
but in nearly every case, and at all depths,
only salt-water is struck. Fancy striking, sur-
rounded by bleating, parched sheep and cattle,
in a drought-struck land, at a depth of 60ft., a
flow of beautifuHy clear water—and then finding
it absolutely undrinkable by man or beast! ”
Greville Turner, Ltd., are fully supplied
with all sizes of the Rambler and Greville bicycles.
Many of the former are fitted with the new
feather spring for saddles, which reduces vibration
to a minimum. The change gear is worth inspec-
tion. ihis invention gives the rider the power of
propelling with either a high or low gear at will,
and without stoppage of the machine. By special
arrangement with the English Dunlop Co., G.T.
Ltd. will convert any rims and tyres into West-
wood and Dunlop at a small cost.*
N.S. W. Agricultural Society holds its Syd-
ney show from 6th to I2th April. The Society’s
schedule is devoted in every page of its 150 pages
to the agriculturist’s interest; so that the lady
who wins the high jump first prize, without break-
ing her neck, receives .£l5, whilst the non-beauti-
ful and unenergetic first prize potato secures <£s
tor looking merely succulent; also, the lady who
wins third jumping prize gets .£3 35., while the
deep-uddered sow of any age in milk, with litter
of pigs, receives a first prize of £2 for looking
beastly useful and pleasant in her pen with her
family Apart from these malodorous compari-
sons, the Society, on the whole, promises to boom.

new grand stiffid, and this, together with the new
terraces, will enable 20,000 more people than for-
merly to view the ring contests. For those who
were born thirsty, the Society has renovated and
increased the refreshment booths, while the official
stand, with the press-box, will have accommoda-
tion for a, great number of full bottles on ice. The

NIS COMPANY is being formed for the purpose of pur-
chasing the Property and Assets of the Don Explora-
tion Syndicate Limited upon the terms as set out in an
agreement dated and executed by
* .u for the Vendors, and by as Trustee
for the new Company, and thereafter of working the same
for the benefit of the new Company.
The Assets consist— (a) a Leasehold Property comprising
10 acres : (b) a Leasehold Property comprising 5 acres,
situated on the Queen of the North Reef, on far-famed
Palmer River Gold Field, North Qeensland ; (c) a Block
Claim of 5 acres ; (d) a Leasehold of 4 acres on the reef
formerly known as the Captain Cook on the same field,
being really a continuation of the Queen of the North
line.
Note.— c and d have not yet been formally granted, but
1898 ex^)ec *' ®*at. they will be obtained early in January,
It is needless to remind the public of the phenomenal
richness of the Palmer goldfield, but it may not be super-
fluous to quote the following extracts from the published
reports of R. L. Jack, Esq., F.G.S., F.R.G.S., Government
Geologist:—
Notes on the Palmer as a Beefing District.
“The Palmer Gold Field occurs in strata which are un-
doubtedly a continuation of those of the Hodgkinson It
has so far, been chiefly an alluvial field, having yielded
?LI £5,000,000 worth of gold since its discovery in
1873. The reefs have never had a fair trial, although
many of them more than deserve it. In the early days,
when alluvial gold was plentiful, and flour sold for 2s. 6d.
per lb., and horse-shoe nails for their weight in gold it
was natural that nobody would ever turn aside to look' at
a reef.”
THE QUEEN.
“From the surface to a depth varying from 220 to 270
feet, 7082 tons of stone have been taken, yielding 15 1670 z
of gold—over 2oz. per ton—and yet the mine has been
idle since 1887, except for desultory workings which never
touched the deep ground, which has been standing under
water.
Mines of the above class, and with a similar history
abound m the neighbourhood of Maytown. Nothing that
was not quite abnormally rich could possibly pay in the
old days, and besides there was a general belief among
the miners that the mundic stone below the water-level
would not carry gold, or if it did it could not be profitably
extracted. No reef that did not show coarse gold on
being smashed up with a hummer was judged worthy of
a second thought. Many valuable reefs probably remain
to be discovered.”
“Another valuable mine, the Recompense, was dis-
covered only on Christmas Day, 1895, by one of two
brothers who stayed at home because he was too hard up
to go to a cricket-match at the Laura. There was only a
thin vein at the surface showing a little gold. It has
since opened up well down to 100 ft. A crushing of 6 tons
from the first 22ft. of the shaft gave lloz. 9dwt. 13gr. per
. A Party of miners have recently been employed recover-
ing the tailing-s from the Queen of the North,' which gave
them a return of 18dwts. per ton, showing that, by the
employment of the latest gold-saving appliances, the re-
turns are confidently expected to reach not less than 3oz
per ton on average crushings.
It is estimated that the quantity of water in the mine
will always be sufficient to keep the machinery going at
all times and seasons. & *
The Vendors have sufficient faith in the mine to require
no cash payments, but are fully satisfied to take 10 000
fully paid up shares in full payment of their interests ’
This Y enable the Directors to devote the whole of
the Contributing Capital to the devolopment of the pro-
perty, although it is expected that only a small portion
will be required to be called up. 1
At the present time some permanent work is being pro-
ceeded with. A main shaft, 13ft. by 4ft. 4in. has been
started, and it is expected to strike the reef at 300 ft. from
the surface. It will be of sufficient dimensions to enable
the work to be pushed ahead with vigor.
.o-iorm e ®rtmated cost of sinking the main shaft 300 ft. is
fon^\ whlch .'" c l ud “ timbering, wages, &c., leaving over
£BOOO to provide for the necessary crushing and pumpino-
machinery. The purchase of a powerful and complete
plant in working order is now under consideration of the
Directors.
Fortnightly reports on the mine since July, 1897 to
date, containing information too voluminous for a Pro-
spectus, can be inspected by intending shareholders at
the offices of the Company.
The Provisional Directors reserve the right to declare
rte Company formed and floated with a less number than
50,000 shares.
Applications for Shares can be made in Brisbane to the
Secretary, the Solicitor to the Company, or any of the
Provisional Directors, from whom all information may be
obtained. - y e
Certified returns of crushings of some of the Palmer
River Gold Mining Company’s Mines are in possession of
the Directors, and speak for themselves. They can be in
spected at the Office by intending Shareholders A litho
graph of some of the working of the mines on the Queen
of the North Line is appended. u

E. LENTHALL OLDFIELD, Principal.
Not many years ago a N.S.W. country
alderman, who was pervading Sydney with, his
cheque-book peeping ostentatiously from his
breast-pocket, fell in with an acquaintance, who
suggested to him that, in order to let people know
who he was, he should have some cards printed.
So they hied them to a printery, and the
a ! derman selected a huge card with a big
sunflower in the corner, and gave instruc-
tions for the printing. Having received
the cards, he sought the office of the Even-
ing News, of which he was a country cor-
respondent. “Sam Bennett in?” he airily
inquired. (The proprietor, old Sam Bennett, had
been dead three years) “Ah, well; when he
comes in, just give him this, will you? What s
the matter with it? ” He took the card back, and
read, “ Alderman Dash, Press underneath.” The
whole batch presented the same misunderstanding
of his instructions to put “Press” beneath his
name.
“ Anomalies and Curiosities of Medicine,”
by G. M. Gould and W. M. Pyle, of IT.S.A.,
records some cases which would be unbelievable
unless the standing of the authors is taken into
account. It tells how children have become
mothers at eight, nine, and ten years old. Also,
how women 60, 70, 90 and—ye gods! —and 103
have done the same. Likewise, it tells of a dame
of 52 who had twins, and of another of 62 who
was blessed with triplets The book also records
how in ’72 Mrs. Timothy Bradlee, of Trumbull
County, Ohio, gave birth to eight children at
once. She had only been married six years, and
had already produced two pairs of twins. She
herself was a triplet, and her father and mother
were of twin births, and one of her grandmothers
had given the world five pairs of twins. A lot of
morals seem to hang about this.
JELP. : “ The applicant for a selection out
West (N.S.W.) isn’t always the most keen-witted
of men, and in the intricacies of Lands Dept,
forms and verbiage is liable to come to grief.
When, for instance, he applies for his selection he
is summoned to a Land Board meeting, but is in-
formed that in his absence the board may proceed
to deal with the case. And as he lives a hundred
miles or so from the board-town, and, moreover,
has some starving sheep to cut mulga for, or some
thirsty bullocks to travel to water, he frequently
concludes to let the board ‘deal.’ Generally the
board adjourns the matter and issues another
summons, similarly worded ; and again (probably
having the same cattle still on hand) he concludes
once more to let them 1 deal ’in his absence. By
this time the board is satisfied that the poor devil
is a shuffling dummy, or tank-grabber, or some-
thing equally objectionable, and surprises him
with a notice that owing to his non-appearance
his deposit and survey-fee (amounting often to
,£3O or £4O) have been forfeited, and the applica-
tion recommended for refusal. After that there
is the usual political appeal to the Minister,
I whereas the whole matter might be made simple
if the misleading notice were somehow amended.”

How to excel at Billiards.

billiards was witnessed in one of the principal

THE
Elder Conservatorium of Music.
The Conservatorium will be OPENED in March
next, and the courses of instruction will embrace
the following subjects :
Pianoforte, Singing, Organ, Harp,
Violin, Violoncello,
Harmony and Musical Composition,
Sight Singing and Musical Dictation,
History of Music,
Ensemble Playing (chamber music),
Concerted Music (vocal and instrumental),
Other Orchestral Instruments,
Orchestral Playing.
Diplomas in Music will be granted to successful
students. „ ... ,
FOUR FREE SCHOLARSHIPS will be
offered for competition—Two being reserved for
South Australia; two being open to all students
in Australasia.
Provisional syllabus may be had on application
to the principal music-sellers. Full particulars on
application to
CHAS. R. HODGE,
Registrar.

Private Hassard, of the Gordon High-
landers, had no less than three close shaves in the
charge up the Dargai Pass. One bullet carried
away his haversack buckle, another pierced the
haversack itself, smashed a Kropp razor, passed
through Hassard's kilt, and wounded a man
behind him in the leg ; and another struck his
ammunition-pouch and knocked him down. Not
the Fatal Three that time, at all events.

From the Age Death Jingle column :
In memory of John .
We will not murmur, will not ask of heaven the reason
why
The young, the good, the honorable, like John, are
called to die.
Inserted by his loving daughter.
A daughter who addresses her father, alive or
dead, by his front name, is very lacking ,in
respect. Perhaps distance lends familiarity.
Byron’s “ Prisoner of Chillon,” Bonivard,
has gone into nothingness. The alleged confinee
■was said to have been chained for years to a pillar,
round which he walked till his footprints wore
tracks in the stone floor. During 1897 the foot-
prints, over which sentimentals used to gloat and
weep, mysteriously disappeared. As footprints
are hard to steal, Deputy Vuillet asked the Govt,
in his place in the Swiss Diet, where they had
gone to. Thereon uprose Councillor of State
Vecquerat, and hardfacedly explained that there
never had been any genuine tracks of Bonivard,
but that, as the sentimental public demanded
footprints, the Govt, had duly provided them and
renewed them every year, but that by an over-
sight the repairs were forgotten last year. Will-
iam Tell has fallen away into a myth. Rousseau’s
grave has no one in it. Gladstone has been proved
by Andrew Lang to be a sun-myth. And now
Bonivard shares the fate of Mrs. Harris —“ which
I don t believe there's no such pusson.”
Dr. Roger Williams, of London, points out
that cancer is steadily spreading. Recent statis-
tics show that deaths therefrom have more than
quadrupled between ’BO and ’96. Williams also
shows that this increase in the cancer death-rate
is accompanied by a lessened number of deaths
from tubercular diseases. From a study of family
histories, it seems to him probable that good
hygienic conditions preserve individuals from
phthisis only to allow them or their descendants
to become cancerous. He also states the queer
paradox that “augmentation of the cancer-rate
indicates good sanitary conditions.” The exist-
ence of “ cancer houses ” and “ cancer areas ” has
been absolutely proved, the cancer areas being
chiefly in low-lying valleys, especially where the
livers overflow their banks. That cancer is an
infectious disease has been practically provi d,
though not yet accurately demonstrated, and the
diminution of deaths from tuberculosis where
cancer increases tends to confirm the contention.

Habitue : “ Melb. Public Library trustees
have given up in despair the attempt to keep any
soap in their lavatory—such is the invincible love
of cleanliness amid the poor Yarra-bankers. An
official was planted in a cupboard near the wash-
ing-basins, and had his eye glued to a gimlet-hole
for a few hours. At last he pounced on the
banker clutching his beloved soap and starting off
for Brander’s Ferry. Instead of encouraging the
poor chap in the desire to keep a few of his 150
million pores open, beak Panton gave him the
cruel wash enforced at M‘Gee’s Hotel, Melb.
Gaol. Still the soap keeps going.”

COUGHS COLDS, AND RHEUMATISM
the inevitable results.
In this inclement weather, when so many are
suffering from colds, coughs, and rheumatism, the
story of Mrs. Mary Brown, of 71 Simmons-street,
Enmore, will be read with devouring interest.
When a reporter called at this lady s resi-
dence, he found her busily engaged at the wash-
tub, what she called doing a day s washing.
It was indeed difficult to believe by the amount
•vT
rtf “ elbow grease ” she laid into her labors that
this interesting personage had lately been in-
formed by medical men that hers was a case which
would probably develop into chronic rheumatism.
“ I have called, Mrs. Brown,” said the reporter,
“to ascertain the truth of the rumour that has
reached me of your having recently suffered from
a severe attack of rheumatism.”
“ It is quite true, but I am now happy to relate
that I feel as well as ever I did. I have been
suffering on and off from rheumatism for the last
five years, and the slightest change in the weather
used to give me most awful twinges. It chiefly
affected my arms, and I can assure you, sir, that
it took me all my time to do my hair. As to lift-
ing this tub, that was a matter of sheer impossi-
bility. If it had not been for some of my friends
I could never have got through my household
duties. This complaint appeared to affect my
entire'system, and as to the money I have spent
in doctors’ advice and so-called remedies, why it
makes me positively disgusted to think of. I
began to think that if my sufferings continued life
was not worth living. I remember one day, as I
was sitting by the fire, and endeavouring to em-
ploy my mind by reading the Evening News,
that an advertisement caught my eye headed Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People cures Rheu-
matism, Sciatica, &c.’ Now, having wasted so
much money already, I was at first loath to try any
more advertised medicines, but I thought in for a
penny in for a pound, so I invested in three boxes
of this specific. ” -
“ I hope you were careful to ask tor UT.
Williams’ Pink Pills ? ” . .
“I took particular notice to do that, fori had
read of so many worthless imitations that I was
careful to ask for, and obtain, the genuine article.
“We think it highly probable, Mrs. Brown,
that before long some of the compounders of these
worthless imitations will be spending time in
seclusion, with the accompanying eccentricities of
diet, official garb, and escort.” .
“ And, indeed, it would only serve them right.
BroWn (my husband) laughed at me when I told
him I was going in for a course of Dr. Williams
Pink Pills, and said, ‘ Oh, you are going to chuck
away some more money, are you?’ ”
“ I said. ‘ Yes, I am,’ and right glad am I that
I ran the risk of ‘chucking away some more
money ’ for.l am happy to tell you that they have
done me all the good in the world. Talk about
the new woman ! Why, I feel now a brand new
woman, and my husband only this morning said
to me, ‘Well, old girl if you were off-color a
few weeks ago, you certainly look the pink of
perfection now. I suppose it is your Pink Pills
that have done it.’ And so it is. Not only have
T lost mv rheumatic pains, but my appetite has
returned, and I can now enjoy my food. In_ fact,
my appetite is so good that Brown says I will eat
him out of house and home.
“How many boxes have you taken, Mrs.
Br “lhave had seven boxes in all, and I have so
much belief in them that I intend always to keep
a box by me, and I shall recommend them to all
tv friends. You are quite at liberty to publish
this statement, as I believe in giving credit to

Indigestion—lts Ravages and
Cure.
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS TO
THE FORE.
(From the Peak-hill Times).
Mr. F. Carr has been a resident of the town
of Peak-hill for a period of nine years. For the
greater portion of that time he followed the oc-
cupation of a miner, until he met with a dyna-
mite accident whilst working in the Carrington
Mine. He was a strong, active man, able to do
a day’s work without feeling the least fatigue
whatever. After he had recovered from the
effects of the explosion he tried to follow his pre-
vious occupation, but found that it was impos-
sible, and so he was forced to seek some other
means of earning a living, and, trying many differ-
ent callings, finally took up that of night watch-
man, at which he has been constantly engaged for
the last couple of years. He says: “ About eight
months ago I became subject to severe pains
and loss of appetite, and feeling as though it
were a trouble to move. These pains increased
in such a way that I found myself complaining to
my friends and acquaintances, who were _of
opinion that indigestion was the cause. I tried
first one medicine, then another, until I believe
I had pretty well sampled all the chemicals in
the drug-shop. But these were of no avail; the
drugs went, but the pains increased and became
so bad that I was commencing to think there
was no hope of even relief, and that I should
soon know the ‘ grand secret. ’ I could not fol-
low my occupation, and was forced to give in.
I had noticed in the Peakhill Times on several
occasions accounts of wonderful cures effected
by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, so I determined to
try them, and mentioned my intention to a few
who came to see me, and they informed me of
others in town whom they had benefited, al-
though not suffering exactly the same as myself.
This information encouraged me, and I felt a bit
brighter, as there seemed a ray of hope left yet.
I can assure you I did not allow much time to
elapse before I dispatched a messenger to the
chemist’s, and procured a box. I took these
o©
4
6
r
V
regularly, and in nine or ten days there was no
possible doubt whatever but that they were doing
me good. I was commencing to look for my
meals before the meal hour would arrive, and
able to get out of bed and lounge around the
house. I continued taking the pills, and as soon
as one box was empty I’d get another, and in the
course of a few weeks I was able to walk up town.
Besides the return of strength and desire for
nourishment, I was very much pleased to find
that the pains had nearly all gone. But I was
doomed to further misery and disappointment, as
in a little while my feet began to swell, which
gradually increased until they seemed ready to
burst. The swelling extended up the leg nearly
as far as the knee. My walking about was again
stopped, and to all appearances I was going to
pass through another time of suffering. 1 his
was probably due to my having taken too much
exercise, after being so long invalided. However
I continued with Dr. Williams Pink Tills, and
by the time I had completed the third box the
swelling started going down, and did so quickly.
From that out my recovery was certain, and it was
not many weeks before I took up my occupation
as ‘guardian of the town during the hours ot
sleep,’ and I am happy to say that I am as good
and as strong a man as I ever was in ray hie,
thanks to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and I strongly
recommend them to any person who may be suf-
fering from inward complaints, and are in search
of a genuine medicine. —F. CARR. ’
A Bloodless Girl's Trials.
(From the Pbahran Telegrahh, Vic.)
Hearing of a remarkable cure effected by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, our reporter
called on Miss M. Warne, of 68 Tivoli-road, South
Yarra, Melbourne, and, in answer to his ques-
“Oh, yes; Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People cured me. Don’t I look cured ? ”
“Well,” he replied, “you don’t seem to have
much the matter with you. You look the picture
of health.” T
She smiled. “Well, three years ago I was
simply dying. I had doctors and doctors, as all
my blood was turning to water, and I grew thin-
ner and thinner every day. My friends at Heath-
cote were very anxious about me.”
“You were suffering then,” he said, from
anaemia ? ” l ....
“ Yes, that was what the doctors said it was ;
but they could not cure it.”
“ Well? ”
“ It happened that my elder sister heard of the
virtues of the Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People, and I thought I would just try them.
“ And you did ? ”
“Yes, with the result you see.”
M A most charming result, truly,” he thought.
“ They acted like a charm, and now, though 1
am always busy, I never was so strong or happy
in my life. They are the only medicine that ever
did me any good.”
“Wonderful,” he thought. Sceptic, you are
convinced. Thus he reasoned, and thanked
Miss Warne for her interview, and was about to
withdraw when she said :
“Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are at the present
time doing the same thing for my younger sister
as they did for me. In a short time she too will
be cured!”
“I sincerely trust so,” he replied, as he pre-
pared to take his departure, and knowing that
time at that moment was valuable to both Miss
Warne and himself, thanked her for the informa-
tion imparted, and hurried back to his office.
Tortuous, Raking Rheumatism
YIELDS TO DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS
FOR PALE PEOPLE.
It would be difficult to conceive a more tho-
roughly typical son of the soil than Mr. Samuel
Coe, of Strathpine, about 14 miles from Brisbane.
Yet that man was, in the early part of May
last, bent double with pain, racked night and day
with the tortures of Sciatica ; he could not walk,
he could not ride, and life was to him a veritable
burden. He had been a sufferer for a year, and
it seemed to him that there was no hope on this
side of the grave, when he read of and tried Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. To that medicine Mr. Coe
unhesitatingly ascribes the credit of the change
effected in his condition. Hearing of his case, a
reporter journeyed out to Strathpine to hear from
his own lips the story which he very gladly and
- cry thankfully tells to others. When met by the
reporter Mr. Coe was riding a useful-looking bay
horse, sitting him comfortably and presenting no
appearance of ever having been an invalid.
o
“I called, Mr. Coe,” the reporter said, “to
gather from you some personal confirmation ot
your letter to the proprietors of Dr. Williams
Pink Pills for Pale People.”
“ Well,” was the reply, “ I am sure I am very
glad to do that. Every word in the letter is cor-
re ' W ould like to ask you for some details,” said
the reporter, “ of the history of your case.’’
Mr Coe then went on to describe his first attack
of Sciatica, but first of all dismounted from his
horse and stood by the roadside in leisurely style
with the reins thrown loosely over his arm 1
was in Sandgate with a load,” he said, m May,
1896, and all of a sudden something came over me
which I thought was a paralytic stroke. 1 was
sea reelv able to move. A neighbor stopped me
and asked me to help him with a bale but I told
him I had a paralytic stroke and could not do so.
I got home and was very bad. I was bent double;
could not walk as far as that tree (indicating
a tree about a dozen yards away) if you
gave me a million pounds ; could not mount
a horse or sit on one, and had no sleep.
I went to doctors (naming three prominent
Brisbane physicians and two at Sandgate) but got
no relief. Some of them wanted me to go to the
hospital, but. held out.no hope of being able to help
me. The disease made an old man of me. 1
dragged along for about twelve months not only
with pains down my hips and legs but all up my
back. In May last I read of Dr. Williams Pills,
and obtained a box. The first box gave me relief.
I felt the blood shooting in a warm stream through
the affected parts, and before I had taken many
boxes I was out of pain and able to go about my
business.
Lest his case should be taken as singular, Mr.
Ooe said “ There are others at Strathpine who
have been cured with these pills, and no doubt
they would give you their experiences. At any
rate you can ask any of them about my case.
They can all tell you of the great change there is
in me.” _ , ,
It is little wonder that Mr. Coe looks upon the
Pink Pills medicine as something to be warmly
commended.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
A SAFEGUARD AGAINST HEADACHES.

“F.C.” to The Bulletin :
Re your remarks as to the composition of Bourke Land
Board Court: what about Wentworth? One member is
a big general storekeeper, with the largest hawking-boat
on the Darling. For the cwt. he sells to a homestead
lessee, he sells a ton to the squatter. The other member
is a coachbuilder, and, of course, cookies can’t buy £5O
buggies. I have nothing to say against the honor of
either, but their position is most anomalous ; and, while
human nature is what it is, the cookies will always feel
doubtful in a case of dispute with a squatter. Who is re-
sponsible for such appointments ?

"Single Again!"
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The second bell had just rung. A babel
oi voices and reiterated good-byes. A quarter-
master doing his best to speed the stream of de-
parting friends who blocked the gangway as they
leaned back to grasp again the outstretched hands,
or look once more upon the faces that strained
towards them from the hurricane deck. One
woman still lingered as the crowd pressed past.'
She put her hand in its shabby cotton glove on her
companion’s arm. Her figure was small and
shrunken. Her white face was turned to his,
with anxious eyes, the blue of which looked as if it
had been washed pale with tears. The handsome,
dark eyes above her did not look into hers. They
had a restless, half-furtive look, as they wandered
over passengers and deck and dull, grey river—
with lashes half-closed on the too-full, puffy under-
lids.
“Remember,” the woman was saying, “it’s all
sewn into your belt. I wish it were more, for I
worked hard for it, Tom, and —and—oh, Tom,
you’ll not forget your promise ? ”
“Forget, not I.” He took her hand from his
arm. “Look here, little woman,” he said. He
drew back his coat and showed a scrap of blue
ribbon pinned on to his vest. “ That’s what I’ve
done, just to show whether I mean to forget.”
Her face brightened into a faint smile, but the
look of fear and distrust had become too habitual
to ever quite leave it. “ Once you are there, you
will be free from temptation,” she said, more hope-
fully. “Then, with your cleverness, you are sure
to succeed. You will conquer the old habit and
come home,”—her voice broke —“oh, Tom! it
will be a real home then. There will be no dread”
—the tears ran down her cheeks; she caught his
hand in hers. “Swear to me,” she said, “that
you will never give in, that you will fight against
this curse, and that you will not come back until
you have conquered.
“ I will never come back till I have retrieved
myself. I swore it long ago—so don’t be afraid.
I am not going to make you miserable any more.”
He bent his head to kiss her, and as he watched
her hurry away there were tears in his eyes, and
his weak, easy-tempered mouth was set in a firmer
line.
He saw her watching him from the wharf, as
the huge ship swung heavily round and slowly
moved ahead, but he did not see her form
straighten and her soft eyes brighten, nor hear
the deep breath that filled her lungs as at last she
turned away with a new-born elasticity in her
seep. The dusk of an autumn evening was already
closing in. . A grey mist enveloped the city, and
the unsightly sheds and rough piles of the wharves
loomed through it. The steamer made a smooth
track on the murky river; the wet mist became a
drizzle, and dripped from the man’s hat. He
shivered, and instinctively his hand went to his
pocket. It was empty. A sudden breeze stirred
the flap of a sail and sent a shower of moisture
over him. His eyes were still watery and his
mouth quivered. His fingers felt for the belt
beneath his coat and closed on it eagerly. But
the next moment he withdrew his hand sharply.
“No, damn it! ” he said, half-aloud, “not her
money.” He leaned again on the bulwark.
The electric-light was beginning to flash from
the port-holes and sparkle on the water. The wet
of the rail soaked through his sleeves. He
shivered again—then felt in another pocket, and
stood tip straight as he heard the clink of small
?° ins ‘ With trembling haste his hand clutched
quick footste P 3 he stumbled dovvn
,
t>. , , , , , , , ,
Burton was awakened at last from what
seemed to him a long and hideous nightmare,
The sweat from his agony was still damp on his
brow > and even y et he felt an awful terror that
the nightmare might return. He was very weak,
and when he trembled his trestle-mattress swayed
beneath him. All was quiet now, after an eternity
of hideous shapes that had wriggled and crawled
U P the Slde of the tent, and stretched long swaying
necks down towards him from the roof with hiss-
mouths, and tongues that shot out at him till
be shrieked in his vain struggle to escape,
But now, at last, he was at rest. Thank God
for that peace and quiet. A whiff of tobacco-
smoke crossed his face, and he turned his head to
see a man leaning back in a chair and watching
b ' m - .
Doctor,” he said, Ive been in hell.’
“Yes, my friend,” said the doctor, dryly, “and
you’ve tried to take some of the rest of us there
Look here, Burton,” he went on, leaning
over > “ what’s the use of your stopping here ? I’ve
pulled you through this time, as I have several
fcimes before ; but what’s the good of it?”
“ I swear ” began Burton.
“ Bah ! ” interrupted the other, “ I’ve heard
that before. It’s the loneliness that’s doing it. Go
ba °k to your wife, and get her to help you. Mix
with other people and work at your profession;
you’ve got one, you say. Why on earth did you
come here at all ? ”
“was the wife. She knew it was a prohibi-
tion-settlement—prohibition, with a sly-grog shop
round every corner ! She thought I would be
cured when awa y frOIU temptation. How can I
go back to her, doctor, and tell her her scheme
was a failure? Why, man!” he cried, “it was
her savings that brought me here. .How can I
break her heart ? She says she can’t live if I don’t
Ive heard them say that before, sighed the
doctor, “ and yet they do live, God help them ! ”
Burton covered his face with his hands. “ Her
savings,” he muttered with a sob in his voice,
“ that she scraped and starved for. My God !
what a blackguard I have been ! ”
And the doctor said nothing,
* * * *
All on board the steamer Oriflamme were
in a state of excitement. They would touch at
Southampton in half an hour, and at South-
ampton would be letters and friends. A young
subaltern, home from India on his first leave,
was already straining his sight to pierce the dark-
ness, and his eyes were dim. The band struck up
“Home, Sweet Home,” and at the first notes he
spun round on his heel, and, catching a girl’s
hand, waltzed madly down the deck. Other
couples followed with shouts and laughter. An
impromptu concert was in full swing in the bows,
and an occasional strain of comic song rose above
the clamor of voices in the intervals of the dances.
“Hullo, there’s Burton loose again!” shouted
one of the singers. “ Come along and give us a
song, old fellow.”
“Shut up, Harry,” muttered a companion, “ we
don’t want that drunken brute ! ”
“Oh, he’s all right,” rejoined the Other— “ the
captain’s stopped his Come on, Burton :
what’s it to be?” Burton took the proffered
banjo, and his fingers, shaking on the strings,
gave a quaver to the vamped accompaniment.
His flaming eyes and fever-hot face measured the
worth of the captain’s mandate. His excite-
ment increased as all joined in roaring the vulgar
refrain. “Oh, when I was single, my pockets
would jingle—l’d like to be single again. My
wife she died—-oh then ! oh then ! ”
A shout interrupted him. The pilot was on
board with the letters. And, as the rest rushed
to the saloon, Burton rose slowly. His excite-
ment had died away, and, as he shambled along
the deck, his face was white and his eyes were
dull. A horrible depression was stealing over him,
and he trembled as he grasped the. companion-rail
and stumbled towards his cabin in search of his
only comfort.
“Parcel for you, Mr. Burton!” called out a
steward. It was a newspaper; he tore off the
wrapper as he went, and in his cabin he turned
the paper round in his hand, wondering in a
stupid kind of way who had sent it. “No letter
from Mary. Funny thing, that. She must have
heard I’m coming.”
He shook his head foolishly, then an eager look
came to his face, and he hastily fastened his
oabin-door. His hand shook as he dragged a
brandy-bottle, three-parts full, from its hiding
place.
“ Poor Mary! ” he said presently, half aloud,
“ Can’t go home to her like this. Must reform
first.” He unfolded the newspaper. “When
I’ve finished this bottle, I won’t touch another
drop—never ! ” Then, as he raised the bottle
for another gulp, his eye caught a paragraph
heavily marked with ink. The lines danced before
him, but he could read the headings. “ Shocking
occurrence.” “No apparent reason.” “Ver-
dict, suicide while temporarily insane.” And
lower down he read his own name. “It was es-
pecially sad,” said the scribe, “as her husband
was expected shortly home from Australia.”
The paper fell from his hand. He clutched the
side of his berth, then fell back against the bulk-
head, his fingers twisted in the coverlet. Over-
head, the passengers tramped to and fro. A boy
was still excitedly continuing the song. “My
wife she died —oh then! oh then ! ” shouted the
half-cracked voice. Burton grasped the brandy-
bottle and held it to his trembling lips. “My
wife she died, and I laughed till I cried ”
The bottle fell with a crash, the man began to
laugh. It was a terrible laugh ; for, once he had
begun, he could not stop. He laughed till he was
bent double—laughed till the tears rained down
his cheeks, and sobbing breaths tore his chest;
and when a loud knocking sounded on the door
he only laughed the more. Then the sobs came
quicker and shook his whole frame, till he fell
forward on the floor.
The young subaltern passed down the alley-way,
whistling cheerily. He had a letter from _ his
mother in his hand, and he held his head high.
He ceased his whistling as he saw a steward trying
to enter a cabin, and stood listening for a moment
to the hideous sounds on the other side.
“ Poor beggar,” he said sympathetically, as he
passed on. “ Got ’em again ! ”
MAMCOSE.
For Sale by Tender.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She had a somewhat negleoted and weather-
worn appearance, when I first saw her, as of a
property that had been “up” for a considerable
while before —but had been withdrawn, the ten-
ders not reaching the reserve price. She had
refused the dentist and the chemist before I oanie.
Then she straightened her cap and called on me.
I had just begun practice as a doctor of medicine,
the only unmarried one in the district. The
others, fortunately for themselves, had taken the
precaution of entering into partnership before they
came to the river.
My predecessor had had the utmost difficulty in
extricating himself and departing solitary, and, as
I learned from different sources since (in confi-
dence, of course), was engaged to at least half the
women of the township before he left; the other
half being either already married or engaged else-
where. lam not naturally egotistic, and. if I
were, it certainly was not very flattering for a
building so long in the market to turn her atten-
tions to me.
I asked her to be seated and she sank languidly
into an easy chair, the easiest one in the room—
my consulting-room at Mrs. Horton’s house, where
I lodged.
“ I feel so faint, doctor” —and she tenderly em-
braced the region of her heart. I enquired
further into the symptoms.
“Palpitation of the heart, doctor; palpitation of
the heart; and I feel so faint,” she repeated, with
her gaze directed carpetwards and her eyebrows
bent to resemble two new moons.
I wrote out a prescription and gave it to her.
She lingered.
“Change of air,” I said, “is what you need
now more than anything. Take a trip to Forster
—pleasant little village, sea air good, sea bathing
good. Take my advice, and go.”
She went, but only as many a boomerang has
gone; for, having completed the tour to Forster
and back, she was in my consulting-room next
day. And she is there now, waiting for me to
write out another prescription.
ALICE M'KINNON.

W T was a very easy matter to decide on George’s Christmas
JL present. You remember how he used to rail against cycles,
and declared he would never ride one until they could beat a horse
over a mile. Well, as soon as Stocks beat that wonderful horse’s
record for a mile —I forget its name—by nearly a second, he has
been crazy about records, and Stocks, and pacing, and all that
kind of thing. He was simply delighted with the Dunlop Racer
we gave him, and went into hard training immediately. I know
he will never rest until he holds all Australian records on Dunlop
Tyres.
He says now he has got a “ DUNLOP ” he can soon wipe out
Barden. I hope he will, don’t you ?

The Dog as is Down.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was rusty and crusty and bluff—so they say,
He was sparin’ o’ speech—lie was gruff in his way;
In church or in chapel he never put nose,
He’d a hand for a friend and a fist for his foes ;
He was short at his hymns, he was long at his beer,
And his language was lurid and curious and queer.
A cantank rous old card ! ” was the verdict on
Brown,
But he alius stood up for the Dog as was Down !
His morals were varied and scant was his creed,
And the life he had led was a caution indeed—
He’d been hunted by traps, and he’d hunted the
whale ;
He’d been sometimes in clover and sometimes in
gaol.
In a fury o’ frizz through his hat came his hair ;
I hrough his pants came his knees—but it wasn’t
with prayer ;
And when trouble was ragin’ he rose, did old
Brown,
bared his big arm for the Dog as was Down.
V I don’t care a hang for the Right or the Wrong,
But give men a show,” was the tune of his song ;
“ The man as is up can take care of himself,
Or his friends ’ll do’t for him as long as he’s pelf ;
But I never could laugh when the cowards would
howl
To see a chap fall, though the fall was a foul :
It makes my blood boil just to hear’em,” said
Brown,
“And I’m bound to sail in for the Dog as is
Down.”
He carried, did Brown, all his principles through,
And the Devil had share in his sympathies, too ;
“ A good ’un to fight was the Devil,” he’d say,
“ And he never would creep or would crawl in his
day ;
As a stoker he couldn’t be beaten and—well,
There was very much need for His Nibs and his
Hell,
For what would you do with a cargo,” said.
Brown,
“ Of cusses who kick at a Dog when he’s Down ? ”
And this was the Lamp for the race he had run,
His Law and his Prophets all rolled into one:
“ Them folks who are always a-gassing o’ God,
Their ‘ Love o’ the Lord ’ is the fear of His Rod;
But I fancy He’ll drive me the bargains I’ve
driven,
And He’ll measure my dues by the measure I’ve
given,
For I guess He’s a Man in his way,” said old
Brown,
“ And He won’t bh too hard on a Dog as is
Down!”

P. LUFTIG.
Norfolk Island.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pitcairners, missionaries, and niggers ; a
couple of new settlers, a Chief Magistrate, five
square miles of fertile soil, and the best climate in
the world, make up the sum of Norfolk Island.
The Chief Magistrate is so called because he is the
only magistrate. The island owns no comparative
terms ; everything is'superlative, even hospitality.
The casual and infrequent stranger has the best of
everything put before him, and the islander who
is honored by his visit will give him tea to drink
three times daily—himself, perhaps, only tasting
the soothing beverage once a month.
* • * »
The funny things about the island are the
duties. There is a ruinous duty on nearly every-
thing a civilised palate requires, and as there is no
money on the island the native taste remains
uncivilised. A pound of tea lasts a family for
about a month. The leaves are often boiled over
a dozen times, and when finally discarded are as
white and light as down. As for drink, not an in-
digenous soul on the island has ever smeltany. The
Chief Magistrate seems about the only person who
pays duty on wines or spirits—unless—well, it
would be interesting to have a glance at the Cus-
toms returns for the missionary-station.
* * » *
Woman holds a peculiar position on the
island. She is the intellectual superior of the
native man, who is usually a big, blundering
fellow ; and she wears the trousers, metaphorically
but effectively. When she marries, she makes
her husband live with her own family for the first
year, and the great mother-in-law sovereignship
quenches for ever in that time any boss-instincts
the male might otherwise own. At the end of the
year the husband is usually broken in, and the
young couple then live together.
* * * *
Once the magistrate was rung up at
midnight.
“ What the devil do you want, sir ? ”
The man broke into wild weeping. Men often
weep at Norfolk Island.
“If you please, sir, I’ve been living this last
year at my mother-in-law’s house, and two days
ago my baby was born, and to-night my mother-
in-law fought with me, and my wife took her part,
and they beat me, and turned me out of the
house! ”
Here the recital was interrupted by a loud
wail from a bundle under the man’s arm. The
magistrate was bewildered.
“And do you mean to say, sir, that the un-
natural mother has actually turned her child out,
too ? ”
“No—boo-hoo,” sobbed the man, “ t waited till
my wife and my mother-in-law were asleep, and
then I crept back —boo-hoo, boo-hoo—and stole my
baby—boo-hoo-hoo ! ”
“Well, well!” said the magistrate, interested,
but perplexed.
“ Well, if you please, sir, I took my baby to my
own mother, but she won’t keep it for me.”
(Women are a close borough in Norfolk.)
“Well, what do you want me to do ? ” growled
the magistrate, who commenced to think the
infant was to be plastered on him.
“Please, sir, I want you to give me an order on
my wife and my mother-in-law, to command them
to deliver to me the feeding-bottle, and I can
keep the baby myself ! ”
• » • *
The island has only three mails a year.
One would judge from this that it was far enough
from the world to be removed from sin, especially
as women rule, and the place is teetotal; but—
sad to say—the principal pleasures of some of the
people are larceny and lying. Yet the thieves are
shrewd. They only steal in families, thus the
thefts are always condoned, and never come to
court. One man had about £5O worth of corn,
wire, and potatoes stolen from him. The thief
was his son, aged 18. Confession showed that the
culprit was badly in love with a girl other side the
island, aged 14. He stole the potatoes as a love-
offering (here is the subject for an epic, ye gods !).
With the corn he hired an animal the islanders
call a horse, to take his love out riding on; and
the wire he sold to his uncle for a wedding-ring !
From some points of view, the incident was
typical.
AMROSE PRATT.
The Nigger-Kicker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WAS making back north from Wilson’s, on the
Gascoigne. Wilson’s was a good enough show for
the chaps that found it, but the crowd that came
after got little or nothing. I was one of “the
crowd.” There had been plenty rain that year,
and the country looked lovely. Grass hid the
ground everywhere—long grass that rustled
against the stirrup-irons, and sometimes half hid
the pack-bags in front. Crossing a claypan, or
riding by a creek-bed, great sheets of water flashed
and sparkled in the sunlight. Birds were every-
where fluttering and chattering, while the bull-
frogs sang seconds in constant chorus from pool
and waterhole. About noon I met a W.A. pro-
cession. A dozen niggers fastened together by a
long chain, the chain looped and fastened round
each man’s neck, and the loose end held by a
native tracker. They were all stark naked ; the
iron glistened dully on their black skins. It was
120 in the shade.
The sun was pretty low down when I reached a
sheep camp. It consisted of a big bough shed,
with a 6 x 8 tent in one corner ; also, a well, sheep-
yards, and blacks’ “mias.” The nigger-boss came
out as I rode up—a big, stout man in pyjamas,
with a loud voice and a wonderful flow of lan-
guage. ' He welcomed me cordially, grabbed a
young gin by the back of the neck, and kicked
her outside, and then sat down for a chat.
“Making back to Pilbarra? Yes, there are lots
of chaps up and down this track now. I had two
here only three weeks ago. Met the trooper to-
day ? By ! times are different now. He’s
been out two months and only got a dozen. I
remember the time when he could get 30 or 40 in
a fortnight. The ‘traps’ were coining money
then. How ? Why, they get eighteenpence a
day for each nigger—tucker allowance, you know,
but they feed ’em on kangaroo. You think that
chain must burn them? Well, perhaps. Good
enough for them, anyhow.”
Soon he began to cut a damper up into tiny
blocks, on each piece placing a bit of meat yet
smaller. Then he lifted a small billy of tea off
the fire, poured it into a bucket, and filled up the
bucket with water. Into this mixture he put two
spoonfuls of sugar. “ The blacks are very fond of
sugar,” he observed gravely. “Oh, you may
laugh, but they are. Go to hell for it, they would.
Here, you! ” and for five minutes hia
language became unprintable.
He w'as calling the blacks to tea. They came
crowding up, some with shirts on, some with no-
thing—men, women, and children. He cursed all
the time he. was giving the rations out; sometimes
when a nigger was slow in coming he would
chuck bread and meat at his head. One old grey-
bearded black was the last to come; he looked
frightened. The nigger-kicker leaned back and
snatched his stockwhip from the wall. “Here,
you ! Why don’t you come to your
feed ? ” The whip curled round the man’s shoul-
ders. He came forward, whining a little, like a
beaten dog ; snatched up his allowance, and
cleared. “ That’s a useless skunk, anyhow. The
boss and me ran down four the other day, but he
stuck to the young ones and sent that old fool out
to me ; I’m d—d if I keep him, though ; I’ll get
rid of him, somehow.”
Next morning, as I was saddling up, he came
down with two niggers, all riding. “ That skunk
got away this morning,” he shouted, “ but we’ll
get him before dinner ” ; and the three cantered
off.
About ten o’clock I was crossing a cork-tree
flat when I heard -a, shot and then a shout coming
from a belt of. mulga on the right. I rode over.
Lying on the ground was the old man of the
night before. "His hand grasped a long slender
wooden spear ; on his temple was a red-blotch,
from which a few drops of blood were trickling.
Looking down at him were two blacks- and my
friend the nigger-boss. He looked up and grinned:
“ Had to do it; self-defence, you know,” he said,
cheerfully. “Good little gun that—what, are
you off? Well, so long. Grand weather, ain’t
it?”
NOMAD.
Rockhampton (Q.) People’s -Newseaper—
which rarely has an issue without half-a-dozen or
more pars, snipped from The Bulletin ; some
acknowledged to “ Exchange,” and some doctored
a little and passed off as original—the other day
went for this disreputable paper—by name —in a
rousing leader. This reminds that, as a rule,
whenever a reptile contemporary slates this d.p.,
it does it with much publicity, and when it
scissors the unobtrusive par. it says vaguely
that it collared it from “a contemporary”
or an “exchange,” which kinder takes the
rough edge off the scissors. The newspaper-
man with a conscience usually acknowledges his
debt ; the man with no conscience steals, and is
ready to lie about it; and the man with a little bit
of conscience doesn’t acknowledge and doesn’t lie,
but compromises. The man who runs the Peo-
ple’s Newspaper has, evidently, a little bit of
conscience, but not much ; which is why honest
London Freethinker of Dec. 19 reprints a Bul-
letin par. and credits to the occasionally-honest
People’s Newspaper—not knowing any better.
The P.N. man is advertised as a freethinker, too
—probably because it’s so mighty free with other
fellows’ thinker.
